Les Miserables	madame magloire has had greater difficulty than myself in accustoming herself to what she calls his imprudence, but at present she has learned to put up with it.
Les Miserables	we both pray; we are terrified together, and fall asleep.
Les Miserables	if the fiend were to enter the house no one would try to stop him, and after all what have we to fear in this house?
Les Miserables	there is always some one with us who is the stronger, the demon may pass by, but our lord lives in it.
Les Miserables	that is enough for me, and my brother no longer requires to say a word to me.
Les Miserables	i understand him without his speaking, and we leave ourselves in the hands of providence, for that is the way in which you must behave to a man who has grandeur in his soul.
Les Miserables	"i have questioned my brother about the information you require concerning the de faux family.
Les Miserables	you are aware that he knows everything, and what a memory he has, for he is still a good royalist.
Les Miserables	it is really a very old norman family belonging to the generalty of caen.
Les Miserables	five hundred years ago there were a raoul, a john, and a thomas de faux, who were gentlemen, and one of them seigneur of rochefort.
Les Miserables	the last was guy stephen alexander, who was major-general, and something in the brittany light horse: his daughter, maria louisa, married adrian charles de gramont, son of duke louis de gramont, peer of france, colonel of the french guards, and lieutenant-general in the army.
Les Miserables	the name is written faux, fauq, and faouq.
Les Miserables	"my dear madam, recommend us to the prayers of your holy relative the cardinal.
Les Miserables	as for your dear sylvanie, she has done well in not wasting the few moments she passes by your side in writing to me.
Les Miserables	she is well, works according to your wishes, and loves me still: that is all i desire.
Les Miserables	her souvenir sent me through you safely reached me, and i am delighted at it.
Les Miserables	my health is not bad, and yet i grow thinner every day.
Les Miserables	good-by, my paper is running out and compels me to break off.
Les Miserables	a thousand kind regards from your baptistine.
Les Miserables	"p.s.
Les Miserables	your little nephew is delightful: do you know that he is nearly five years of age?
Les Miserables	yesterday he saw a horse pass with knee-caps on, and he said, 'what has he got on his knees?'
Les Miserables	he is such a dear child.
Les Miserables	his little brother drags an old broom about the room like a coach, and cries, 'hu!'"
Les Miserables	as may be seen from this letter, the two women managed to yield to the bishop's ways, with the genius peculiar to woman, who comprehends a man better than he does himself.
Les Miserables	the bishop of d----, beneath the candid, gentle air which never broke down, at times did grand, bold, and magnificent things, without even appearing to suspect the fact.
Les Miserables	they trembled, but let him alone.
Les Miserables	at times madame magloire would hazard a remonstrance beforehand, but never during or after the deed.
Les Miserables	they never troubled him either by word or sign when he had once begun an affair.
Les Miserables	at certain moments, without his needing to mention the fact, or perhaps when he was not conscious of it, so perfect was his simplicity, they vaguely felt that he was acting episcopally, and at such times they were only two shadows in the house.
Les Miserables	they served him passively, and if disappearance were obedience, they disappeared.
Les Miserables	they knew, with an admirable intuitive delicacy, that certain attentions might vex him, and hence, though they might believe him in peril, they understood, i will not say his thoughts, but his nature, and no longer watched over him.
Les Miserables	they intrusted him to god.
Les Miserables	moreover, baptistine said, as we have just read, that her brother's death would be her death.
Les Miserables	madame magloire did not say so, but she knew it.
Les Miserables	chapter x.   the bishop faces a new light.
Les Miserables	at a period rather later than the date of the letter just quoted he did a thing which the whole town declared to be even more venturesome than his trip in the mountains among the bandits.
Les Miserables	a man lived alone in the country near d----: this man, let us out with the great word at once, was an ex-conventionalist, of the name of g----.
Les Miserables	people talked about him in the little world of d---- with a species of horror.
Les Miserables	a conventionalist, only think of that!
Les Miserables	those men existed at the time when people "thou-ed" one another and were called citizens.
Les Miserables	this man was almost a monster: he had not voted for the king's death, but had done all but that, and was a quasi-regicide.
Les Miserables	how was it that this man had not been tried by court-martial, on the return of the legitimate princes?
Les Miserables	they need not have cut his head off, for clemency is all right and proper, but banishment for life would have been an example, and so on.
Les Miserables	moreover, he was an atheist, like all those men.
Les Miserables	it was the gossip of geese round a vulture.
Les Miserables	and was this g---- a vulture?
Les Miserables	yes, if he might be judged by his ferocious solitude.
Les Miserables	as he had not voted the king's death, he was not comprised in the decree of exile, and was enabled to remain in france.
Les Miserables	he lived about three miles from the town, far from every village, every road, in a nook of a very wild valley.
Les Miserables	he had there, so it was said, a field, a hut, a den.
Les Miserables	he had no neighbors, not even passers-by; since he had lived in the valley the path leading to it had become overgrown with grass.
Les Miserables	people talked of the spot as of the hangman's house.
Les Miserables	yet the bishop thought of it, and from time to time gazed at a spot on the horizon where a clump of trees pointed out the old conventionalist's valley, and said "there is a soul there alone," and he added to himself, "i owe him a visit."
Les Miserables	but, let us confess it, this idea, which at the first blush was natural, seemed to him after a moment's reflection strange and impossible, almost repulsive.
Les Miserables	for, in his heart, he shared the general impression, and the conventionalist inspired him, without his being able to account for it, with that feeling which is the border line of hatred, and which is so well expressed by the word "estrangement."
Les Miserables	still the shepherd ought not to keep aloof from a scabby sheep; but then what a sheep it was!
Les Miserables	the good bishop was perplexed; at times he started in that direction, but turned back.
Les Miserables	one day a rumor spread in the town, that a shepherd boy who waited on g---- in his den, had come to fetch a doctor: the old villain was dying, paralysis was overpowering him, and he could not last out the night.
Les Miserables	happy release!
Les Miserables	some added.
Les Miserables	the bishop took his stick, put on his overcoat to hide his well-worn cassock, as well as to protect him against the night breeze which would soon rise, and set out.
Les Miserables	the sun had almost attained the horizon when the bishop reached the excommunicated spot.
Les Miserables	he perceived with a certain heart-beating that he was close to the wild beast's den.
Les Miserables	he strode across a ditch, clambered over a hedge, entered a neglected garden, and suddenly perceived the cavern behind some shrubs.
Les Miserables	it was a low, poor-looking hut, small and clean, with a vine nailed over the front.
Les Miserables	in front of the door an old white-haired man, seated in a worn-out wheel-chair, was smiling in the sun.
Les Miserables	by his side stood a boy, who handed him a pot of milk.
Les Miserables	while the bishop was looking at him the old man uplifted his voice.
Les Miserables	"thanks," he said, "i want nothing further," and his smile was turned from the sun to rest on the boy.
Les Miserables	the bishop stepped forward, and at the noise of his footsteps the seated man turned his head, and his face expressed all the surprise it is possible to feel after a long life.
Les Miserables	"since i have lived here," he said, "you are the first person who has come to me.
Les Miserables	who may you be, sir?"
Les Miserables	the bishop answered, "my name is bienvenu myriel."
Les Miserables	"i have heard that name uttered.
Les Miserables	are you not he whom the peasants call monseigneur welcome?"
Les Miserables	"i am."
Les Miserables	the old man continued, with a half-smile, "in that case you are my bishop?"
Les Miserables	"a little."
Les Miserables	"come in, sir."
Les Miserables	the conventionalist offered his hand to the bishop, but the bishop did not take it--he confined himself to saying,--  "i am pleased to see that i was deceived.
Les Miserables	you certainly do not look ill."  "i am about to be cured, sir," the old man said; then after a pause he added, "i shall be dead in three hours.
Les Miserables	i am a bit of a physician, and know in what way the last hour comes.
Les Miserables	yesterday only my feet were cold; to-day the chill reached my knees; now i can feel it ascending to my waist, and when it reaches the heart i shall stop.
Les Miserables	the sun is glorious, is it not?
Les Miserables	i had myself wheeled out in order to take a farewell glance at things.
Les Miserables	you can talk to me, for it does not weary me.
Les Miserables	you have done well to come and look at a dying man, for it is proper that there should be witnesses.
Les Miserables	people have their fancies, and i should have liked to go on till dawn.
Les Miserables	but i know that i can hardly last three hours.
Les Miserables	it will be night, but, after all, what matter?
Les Miserables	finishing is a simple affair, and daylight is not necessary for it.
Les Miserables	be it so, i will die by star-light."
Les Miserables	then he turned to the lad:  "go to bed.
Les Miserables	you sat up the other night, and must be tired."
Les Miserables	the boy went into the cabin; the old man looked after him, and added, as if speaking to himself,--  "while he is sleeping i shall die; the two slumbers can keep each other company."
Les Miserables	the bishop was not so moved as we might imagine he would be.
Les Miserables	he did not think that he saw god in this way of dying: and--let us out with it, as the small contradictions of great hearts must also be indicated--he, who at times laughed so heartily at his grandeur, was somewhat annoyed at not being called monseigneur, and was almost tempted to reply, citizen.
Les Miserables	he felt an inclination for coarse familiarity, common enough with doctors and priests, but to which he was not accustomed.
Les Miserables	this man after all, this conventionalist, this representative of the people, had been a mighty one of the earth: for the first time in his life, perhaps, the bishop felt disposed to sternness.
Les Miserables	the republican, in the mean while, regarded him with modest cordiality, in which, perhaps, could be traced that humility which is so becoming in a man who is on the point of returning to the dust.
Les Miserables	the bishop, on his side, though he generally guarded against curiosity, which according to him was akin to insult, could not refrain from examining the conventionalist with an attention which, as it did not emanate from sympathy, would have pricked his conscience in the case of any other man.
Les Miserables	the conventionalist produced the effect upon him of being beyond the pale of the law, even the law of charity.
Les Miserables	g----, calm, almost upright, and possessing a sonorous voice, was one of those grand octogenarians who are the amazement of the physiologist.
Les Miserables	the revolution possessed many such men, proportioned to the age.
Les Miserables	the thoroughly tried man could be seen in him, and, though so near his end, he had retained all the signs of health.
Les Miserables	there was something which would disconcert death in his bright glance, his firm accent, and the robust movement of his shoulders: azrael, the mohammedan angel of the tomb, would have turned back fancying that he had mistaken the door.
Les Miserables	g---- seemed to be dying because he wished to do so; there was liberty in his agony, and his legs alone, by which the shadows clutched him, were motionless.
Les Miserables	while the feet were dead and cold, the head lived with all the power of life and appeared in full light.
Les Miserables	g---- at this awful moment resembled the king in the oriental legend, flesh above and marble below.
Les Miserables	the bishop sat down on a stone and began rather abruptly:--  "i congratulate you," he said, in the tone people employ to reprimand; "_at least_ you did not vote the king's death."
Les Miserables	the republican did not seem to notice the covert bitterness of this remark, _at least_; he replied, without a smile on his face,--  "do not congratulate me, sir: i voted the death of the tyrant."
Les Miserables	it was the accent of austerity opposed to that of sternness.
Les Miserables	"what do you mean?"
Les Miserables	the bishop continued.
Les Miserables	"i mean that man has a tyrant, ignorance, and i voted for the end of that tyrant which engendered royalty, which is the false authority, while knowledge is the true authority.
Les Miserables	man must only be governed by knowledge."
Les Miserables	"and by his conscience," the bishop added.
Les Miserables	"that is the same thing.
Les Miserables	conscience is the amount of innate knowledge we have in us."
Les Miserables	monseigneur welcome listened in some surprise to this language, which was very novel to him.
Les Miserables	the republican continued,--  "as for louis xvi.
Les Miserables	i said no.
Les Miserables	i do not believe that i have the right to kill a man, but i feel the duty of exterminating a tyrant, and i voted for the end of the tyrant.
Les Miserables	that is to say, for the end of prostitution for women; the end of slavery for men; and the end of night for children.
Les Miserables	in voting for the republic i voted for all this: i voted for fraternity, concord, the dawn!
Les Miserables	i aided in the overthrow of errors and prejudices, and such an overthrow produces light; we hurled down the old world, and that vase of wretchedness, by being poured over the human race, became an urn of joy."
Les Miserables	"mingled joy," said the bishop.
Les Miserables	"you might call it a troubled joy, and now, after that fatal return of the past which is called 1814, a departed joy.
Les Miserables	alas!
Les Miserables	the work was incomplete, i grant; we demolished the ancient régime in facts, but were not able to suppress it completely in ideas.
Les Miserables	it is not sufficient to destroy abuses, but morals must also be modified.
Les Miserables	though the mill no longer exists, the wind still blows."
Les Miserables	"you demolished: it may be useful, but i distrust a demolition complicated with passion."
Les Miserables	"right has its passion, sir bishop, and that passion is an element of progress.
Les Miserables	no matter what may be said, the french revolution is the most powerful step taken by the human race since the advent of christ.
Les Miserables	it may be incomplete, but it was sublime.
Les Miserables	it softened minds, it calmed, appeased, and enlightened, and it spread civilization over the world.
Les Miserables	the french revolution was good, for it was the consecration of humanity."
Les Miserables	the bishop could not refrain from muttering,--"yes?
Les Miserables	'93!"
Les Miserables	the republican drew himself up with almost mournful solemnity, and shouted, as well as a dying man could shout,--  "ah!
Les Miserables	there we have it!
Les Miserables	i have been waiting for that.
Les Miserables	a cloud had been collecting for fifteen hundred years, and at the end of that period it burst: you are condemning the thunder-clap."
Les Miserables	the bishop, without perhaps confessing it to himself, felt that the blow had gone home; still he kept a good countenance, and answered,--  "the judge speaks in the name of justice; the priest speaks in that of pity, which is only a higher form of justice.
Les Miserables	a thunder-clap must not deceive itself."
Les Miserables	and he added as he looked fixedly at the conventionalist,--  "and louis xvii.?"
Les Miserables	the republican stretched forth his hand and seized the bishop's arm.
Les Miserables	"louis xvii.
Les Miserables	let us consider.
Les Miserables	whom do you weep for?
Les Miserables	is it the innocent child?
Les Miserables	in that case i weep with you.
Les Miserables	is it the royal child?
Les Miserables	in that case i must ask leave to reflect.
Les Miserables	for me, the thought of the brother of cartouche, an innocent lad, hung up under the armpits in the place de grève until death ensued, for the sole crime of being cartouche's brother, is not less painful than the grandson of louis xv., the innocent boy martyrized in the temple tower for the sole crime of being the grandson of louis xv."
Les Miserables	"i do not like such an association of names, sir," said the bishop.
Les Miserables	"louis xv.?
Les Miserables	cartouche?
Les Miserables	on behalf of which do you protest?"
Les Miserables	there was a moment's silence; the bishop almost regretted having come, and yet felt himself vaguely and strangely shaken.
Les Miserables	the conventionalist continued,--  "ah!
Les Miserables	sir priest, you do not like the crudities of truth, but christ loved them; he took a scourge and swept the temple.
Les Miserables	his lightning lash was a rough discourser of truths.
Les Miserables	when he exclaimed, 'suffer little children to come unto me,' he made no distinction among them.
Les Miserables	he made no difference between the dauphin of barabbas and the dauphin of herod.
Les Miserables	innocence is its own crown, and does not require to be a highness; it is as august in rags as when crowned with _fleurs de lis_."
Les Miserables	"that is true," said the bishop in a low voice.
Les Miserables	"you have named louis xvii.," the conventionalist continued; "let us understand each other.
Les Miserables	shall we weep for all the innocents, martyrs, and children of the lowest as of the highest rank?
Les Miserables	i am with you there, but as i said, in that case we must go back beyond '93, and begin our tears before louis xvii.
Les Miserables	i will weep over the children of the kings with you, provided that you weep with me over the children of the people."
Les Miserables	"i weep for all," said the bishop.
Les Miserables	"equally!"
Les Miserables	g---- exclaimed; "and if the balance must be uneven, let it be on the side of the people, as they have suffered the longest."
Les Miserables	there was again a silence, which the republican broke.
Les Miserables	he rose on his elbow, held his chin with his thumb and forefinger, as a man does mechanically when he is interrogating and judging, and fixed on the bishop a glance full of all the energy of approaching death.
Les Miserables	it was almost an explosion.
Les Miserables	"yes, sir; the people have suffered for a long time.
Les Miserables	but let me ask why you have come to question and speak to me about louis xvii.?
Les Miserables	i do not know you.
Les Miserables	ever since i have been in this country i have lived here alone, never setting my foot across the threshold, and seeing no one but the boy who attends to me.
Les Miserables	your name, it is true, has vaguely reached me, and i am bound to say that it was pronounced affectionately, but that means nothing, for clever people have so many ways of making the worthy, simple folk believe in them.
Les Miserables	by the bye, i did not hear the sound of your coach; you doubtless left it down there behind that clump of trees at the cross roads.
Les Miserables	i do not know you, i tell you; you have informed me that you are the bishop, but that teaches me nothing as to your moral character.
Les Miserables	in a word--i repeat my question, who are you?
Les Miserables	you are a bishop, that is to say, a prince of the church, one of those gilded, escutcheoned annuitants who have fat prebends--the bishopric of d----, with 15,000 francs income, 10,000 francs fees, or a total of 25,000 francs,--who have kitchens, liveries, keep a good table, and eat water-fowl on a friday; who go about, with lackeys before and behind, in a gilded coach, in the name of the saviour who walked barefoot!
Les Miserables	you are a prelate; you have, like all the rest, income, palace, horses, valets, a good table, and like all the rest you enjoy them: that is all very well, but it says either too much or too little; it does not enlighten me as to your intrinsic and essential value when you come with the probable intention of bringing me wisdom.
Les Miserables	to whom am i speaking--who are you?"
Les Miserables	the bishop bowed his head, and answered, "i am a worm."
Les Miserables	"a worm in a carriage!"
Les Miserables	the republican growled.
Les Miserables	it was his turn to be haughty, the bishop's to be humble; the latter continued gently,--  "be it so, sir.
Les Miserables	but explain to me how my coach, which is a little way off behind the trees, my good table, and the water-fowl i eat on friday, my palace, my income, and my footmen, prove that pity is not a virtue, that clemency is not a duty, and that '93 was not inexorable."
Les Miserables	the republican passed his hand over his forehead, as if to remove a cloud.
Les Miserables	"before answering you," he said, "i must ask you to forgive me.
Les Miserables	i was in the wrong, sir, for you are in my house and my guest.
Les Miserables	you discuss my ideas, and i must restrict myself to combating your reasoning.
Les Miserables	your wealth and enjoyments are advantages which i have over you in the debate, but courtesy bids me not employ them.
Les Miserables	i promise not to do so again."
Les Miserables	"i thank you," said the bishop.
Les Miserables	g---- continued: "let us return to the explanation you asked of me.
Les Miserables	where were we?
Les Miserables	what was it you said, that '93 was inexorable?"
Les Miserables	"yes, inexorable," the bishop said; "what do you think of marat clapping his hands at the guillotine?"
Les Miserables	"what do you think of bossuet singing a te deum over the dragonnades?"
Les Miserables	the response was harsh, but went to its mark with the rigidity of a minié bullet.
Les Miserables	the bishop started, and could not parry it, but he was hurt by this way of mentioning bossuet.
Les Miserables	the best minds have their fetishes, and at times feel vaguely wounded by any want of respect on the part of logic.
Les Miserables	the conventionalist was beginning to gasp; that asthma which is mingled with the last breath affected his voice; still he retained perfect mental clearness in his eyes.
Les Miserables	he continued,--  "let us say a few words more on this head.
Les Miserables	beyond the revolution, which, taken in its entirety, is an immense human affirmation, '93, alas, is a reply.
Les Miserables	you consider it inexorable, but what was the whole monarchy?
Les Miserables	carrier is a bandit, but what name do you give to montrevel?
Les Miserables	fouquier tainville is a scoundrel, but what is your opinion about lamoignon-bâville?
Les Miserables	maillard is frightful, but what of saulx-tavannes, if you please?
Les Miserables	father duchêne is ferocious, but what epithet will you allow me for père letellier?
Les Miserables	jourdan coupe-tête is a monster, but less so than the marquis de louvois.
Les Miserables	i pity marie antoinette, archduchess and queen, but i also pity the poor huguenot woman who, in 1685, while suckling her child, was fastened, naked to the waist, to a stake, while her infant was held at a distance.
Les Miserables	her breast was swollen with milk, her heart with agony; the babe, hungry and pale, saw that breast and screamed for it, and the hangman said to the wife, mother, and nurse, 'abjure!'
Les Miserables	giving her the choice between the death of her infant and the death of her conscience.
Les Miserables	what do you say of this punishment of tantalus adapted to a woman?
Les Miserables	remember this carefully, sir, the french revolution had its reasons, and its wrath will be absolved by the future.
Les Miserables	its result is a better world; and a caress for the human race issues from its most terrible blows.
Les Miserables	i must stop, for the game is all in my favor--besides, i am dying."
Les Miserables	and ceasing to regard the bishop, the republican finished his thought with the following few calm words,--  "yes, the brutalities of progress are called revolutions, but when they are ended, this fact is recognized; the human race has been chastised, but it has moved onwards."
Les Miserables	the republican did not suspect that he had carried in turn every one of the bishop's internal intrenchments.
Les Miserables	one still remained, however, and from this, the last resource of monseigneur's resistance, came this remark, in which all the roughness of the commencement was perceptible.
Les Miserables	"progress must believe in god, and the good cannot have impious servants.
Les Miserables	a man who is an atheist is a bad guide for the human race."
Les Miserables	the ex-representative of the people did not reply.
Les Miserables	he trembled, looked up to the sky, and a tear slowly collected in his eye.
Les Miserables	when the lid was full the tear ran down his livid cheek, and he said in a low, shaking voice, as if speaking to himself,--  "oh thou!
Les Miserables	oh ideal!
Les Miserables	thou alone existest!"
Les Miserables	the bishop had a sort of inexpressible commotion; after a silence the old man raised a finger to heaven and said,--  "the infinite is.
Les Miserables	it is there.
Les Miserables	if the infinite had not a me, the i would be its limit; it would not be infinite; in other words, it would not be.
Les Miserables	but it is.
Les Miserables	hence it has a me.
Les Miserables	this i of the infinite is god."
Les Miserables	the dying man uttered these words in a loud voice, and with a shudder of ecstasy as if he saw some one.
Les Miserables	when he had spoken his eyes closed, for the effort had exhausted him.
Les Miserables	it was evident that he had lived in one minute the few hours left him.
Les Miserables	the supreme moment was at hand.
Les Miserables	the bishop understood it; he had come here as a priest, and had gradually passed from extreme coldness to extreme emotion; he looked at these closed eyes, he took this wrinkled and chilly hand and bent down over the dying man.
Les Miserables	"this hour is god's.
Les Miserables	would you not consider it matter of regret if we had met in vain?"
Les Miserables	the republican opened his eyes again; a gravity which suggested the shadow of death was imprinted on his countenance.
Les Miserables	[illustration: "'your blessing,' said the bishop, and knelt down"]  "monsieur le bishop," he said, with a slowness produced perhaps more by the dignity of the soul than by failing of his strength, "i have spent my life in meditation, contemplation, and study.
Les Miserables	i was sixty years of age when my country summoned me and ordered me to interfere in its affairs.
Les Miserables	i obeyed.
Les Miserables	there were abuses, and i combated them; tyranny, and i destroyed it; rights and principles, and i proclaimed and confessed them; the territory was invaded, and i defended it; france was menaced, and i offered her my chest; i was not rich, and i am poor.
Les Miserables	i was one of the masters of the state; the bank cellars were so filled with specie that it was necessary to prop up the walls, which were ready to burst through the weight of gold and silver, but i dined in the rue de l'arbre sec, at two-and-twenty sous a head.
Les Miserables	i succored the oppressed.
Les Miserables	i relieved the suffering.
Les Miserables	i tore up the altar cloth, it is true, but it was to stanch the wounds of the country.
Les Miserables	i ever supported the onward march of the human race towards light, and i at times resisted pitiless progress.
Les Miserables	when opportunity served, i protected my adversaries, men of your class.
Les Miserables	and there is at peteghem in flanders, on the same site where the merovingian kings had their summer palace, a monastery of urbanists, the abbey of st. claire en beaulieu, which i saved in 1793. i did my duty according to my strength, and what good i could.
Les Miserables	after which i was driven out, tracked, pursued, persecuted, maligned, mocked, spat upon, accursed, and proscribed.
Les Miserables	for many years i have felt that persons believed they had a right to despise me.
Les Miserables	my face has been held accursed by the poor ignorant mob, and, while hating no one, i accepted the isolation of hatred.
Les Miserables	now, i am eighty-six years of age and on the point of death; what have you come to ask of me?"
Les Miserables	"your blessing!"
Les Miserables	said the bishop, and knelt: down.
Les Miserables	when the bishop raised his head again, the conventionalist's countenance had become august: he had just expired.
Les Miserables	the bishop returned home absorbed in the strangest thoughts, and spent the whole night in prayer.
Les Miserables	on the morrow curious worthies tried to make him talk about g---- the republican, but he only pointed to heaven.
Les Miserables	from this moment he redoubled his tenderness and fraternity for the little ones and the suffering.
Les Miserables	any allusion to "that old villain of a g----" made him fall into a singular reverie; no one could say that the passing of that mind before his, and the reflection that great conscience cast upon his, had not something to do with this approach to perfection.
Les Miserables	this "pastoral visit" nearly created a stir among the small local coteries.
Les Miserables	"was it a bishop's place to visit the death-bed of such a man?
Les Miserables	it was plain that he had no conversion to hope for, for all these revolutionists are relapsed!
Les Miserables	then why go?
Les Miserables	what had he to see there?
Les Miserables	he must have been very curious to see the fiend carry off a soul."
Les Miserables	one day a dowager, of the impertinent breed which believes itself witty, asked him this question, "monseigneur, people are asking when your grandeur will have the red cap?"
Les Miserables	"oh, oh!"
Les Miserables	the bishop answered, "that is an ominous color.
Les Miserables	fortunately those who despise it in a cap venerate it in a hat."
Les Miserables	chapter xi.
Les Miserables	a restriction.
Les Miserables	we should run a strong risk of making a mistake were we to conclude from this that monseigneur welcome was "a philosophic bishop," or "a patriotic curé."
Les Miserables	his meeting, which might almost be called his conjunction, with the conventionalist g---- produced in him a sort of amazement, which rendered him more gentle than ever.
Les Miserables	that was all.
Les Miserables	though monseigneur was anything rather than a politician, this is perhaps the place to indicate briefly what was his attitude in the events of that period, supposing that monseigneur ever dreamed of having an attitude.
Les Miserables	we will, therefore, go back for a few years.
Les Miserables	a short time after m. myriel's elevation to the episcopate, the emperor made him a baron, simultaneously with some other bishops.
Les Miserables	the arrest of the pope took place, as is well known, on the night of july 5, 1809, at which time m. myriel was called by napoleon to the synod of french and italian bishops convened at paris.
Les Miserables	this synod was held at notre dame and assembled for the first time on june 15, 1811, under the presidency of cardinal fesch.
Les Miserables	m. myriel was one of the ninety-five bishops convened, but he was only present at one session and three or four private conferences.
Les Miserables	as bishop of a mountain diocese, living so near to nature in rusticity and poverty, it seems that he introduced among these eminent personages ideas which changed the temperature of the assembly.
Les Miserables	he went back very soon to d----, and when questioned about this hurried return, he replied, "i was troublesome to them.
Les Miserables	the external air came in with me and i produced the effect of an open door upon them."
Les Miserables	another time he said, "what would you have?
Les Miserables	those messeigneurs are princes, while i am only a poor peasant bishop."
Les Miserables	the fact is, that he displeased: among other strange things he let the following remarks slip out, one evening when he was visiting one of his most influential colleagues: "what fine clocks!
Les Miserables	what splendid carpets!
Les Miserables	what magnificent liveries!
Les Miserables	you must find all that very troublesome?
Les Miserables	oh!
Les Miserables	i should not like to have such superfluities to yell incessantly in my ears: there are people who are hungry; there are people who are cold; there are poor, there are poor."
Les Miserables	let us remark parenthetically, that a hatred of luxury would not be an intelligent hatred, for it would imply a hatred of the arts.
Les Miserables	still in churchmen any luxury beyond that connected with their sacred office is wrong, for it seems to reveal habits which are not truly charitable.
Les Miserables	an opulent priest is a paradox, for he is bound to live with the poor.
Les Miserables	now, can a man incessantly both night and day come in contact with distress, misfortune, and want, without having about him a little of that holy wretchedness, like the dust of toil?
Les Miserables	can we imagine a man sitting close to a stove and not feeling hot?
Les Miserables	can we imagine a workman constantly toiling at a furnace, and have neither a hair burned, a nail blackened, nor a drop of perspiration, nor grain of soot on his face?
Les Miserables	the first proof of charity in a priest, in a bishop especially, is poverty.
Les Miserables	this was doubtless the opinion of the bishop of d----.
Les Miserables	we must not believe either that he shared what we might call the "ideas of the age" on certain delicate, points; he mingled but slightly in the theological questions of the moment, in which church and state are compromised; but had he been greatly pressed we fancy he would have been found to be ultramontane rather than gallican.
Les Miserables	as we are drawing a portrait, and do not wish to conceal anything, we are forced to add that he was frigid toward the setting napoleon.
Les Miserables	from 1813 he adhered to or applauded all hostile demonstrations, he refused to see him when he passed through on his return from elba, and abstained from ordering public prayers for the emperor during the hundred days.
Les Miserables	besides his sister, mlle.
Les Miserables	baptistine, he had two brothers, one a general, the other a prefect.
Les Miserables	he wrote very frequently to both of them.
Les Miserables	for some time he owed the former a grudge, because the general, who at the time of the landing at cannes held a command in provence, put himself at the head of twelve hundred men and pursued the emperor as if he wished to let him escape.
Les Miserables	his correspondence was more affectionate with the other brother, the ex-prefect, a worthy, honest man, who lived retired at paris.
Les Miserables	monseigneur welcome, therefore, also had his hour of partisan spirit, his hour of bitterness, his cloud.
Les Miserables	the shadow of the passions of the moment fell athwart this gentle and great mind, which was occupied by things eternal.
Les Miserables	certainly such a man would have deserved to have no political opinions.
Les Miserables	pray let there be no mistake as to our meaning: we do not confound what are called "political opinions" with the grand aspiration for progress, with that sublime, patriotic, democratic and human faith, which in our days must be the foundation of all generous intelligence.
Les Miserables	without entering into questions which only indirectly affect the subject of this book, we say, it would have been better had monseigneur welcome not been a royalist, and if his eye had not turned away, even for a moment, from that serene contemplation, in which the three pure lights of truth, justice, and charity are seen beaming above the fictions and hatreds of this world, and above the stormy ebb and flow of human affairs.
Les Miserables	while allowing that god had not created monseigneur welcome for political functions, we could have understood and admired a protest in the name of justice and liberty, a proud opposition, a perilous and just resistance offered to napoleon, all-powerful.
Les Miserables	but conduct which pleases us towards those who are rising, pleases us less towards those who are falling.
Les Miserables	we only like the contest so long as there is danger; and, in any case, only the combatants from the beginning have a right to be the exterminators at the end.
Les Miserables	a man who has not been an obstinate accuser during prosperity must be silent when the crash comes; the denouncer of success is the sole legitimate judge of the fell.
Les Miserables	for our part, when providence interferes and strikes we let it do so.
Les Miserables	1812 begins to disarm us; in 1813 the cowardly rupture of silence by the taciturn legislative corps, emboldened by catastrophes, could only arouse indignation; in 1814, in the presence of the traitor marshals, in the presence of that senate, passing from one atrocity to another, and insulting after deifying, and before the idolaters kicking their idol and spitting on it, it was a duty to turn one's head away; in 1815, as supreme disasters were in the air, as france had a shudder of their sinister approach, as waterloo, already open before napoleon could be vaguely distinguished, the dolorous acclamation offered by the army and the people had nothing laughable about it, and--leaving the despot out of the question--a heart like the bishop of d----'s ought not to have misunderstood how much there was august and affecting in this close embrace between a great nation and a great man on the verge of an abyss.
Les Miserables	with this exception, the bishop was in all things just, true, equitable, intelligent, humble, and worthy; beneficent, and benevolent, which is another form of beneficence.
Les Miserables	he was a priest, a sage, and a man.
Les Miserables	even in the political opinions with which we have reproached him, and which we are inclined to judge almost severely, we are bound to add that he was tolerant and facile, more so perhaps than the writer of these lines.
Les Miserables	the porter of the town hall had been appointed by the emperor; he was an ex-non-commissioned officer of the old guard, a legionary of austerlitz, and as bonapartist as the eagle.
Les Miserables	this poor fellow now and then made thoughtless remarks, which the law of that day qualified as seditious.
Les Miserables	from the moment when the imperial profile disappeared from the legion of honor, he never put on his uniform again, that he might not be obliged, as he said, to bear his cross.
Les Miserables	he had himself devotedly removed the imperial effigy from the cross which napoleon had given him with his own hands, and though this made a hole he would not let anything be put in its place.
Les Miserables	"sooner die," he would say, "than wear the three frogs on my heart."
Les Miserables	he was fond of ridiculing louis xviii.
Les Miserables	aloud.
Les Miserables	"the old gouty fellow with his english gaiters, let him be off to prussia with his salsifies."
Les Miserables	it delighted him thus to combine in one imprecation the two things he hated most, england and prussia.
Les Miserables	he went on thus till he lost his place, and then he was starving in the street with wife and children.
Les Miserables	the bishop sent for him, gave him a gentle lecturing, and appointed him beadle to the cathedral.
Les Miserables	in nine years, through his good deeds and gentle manners, monseigneur welcome had filled the town of d---- with a sort of tender and filial veneration.
Les Miserables	even his conduct to napoleon had been accepted, and, as it were, tacitly pardoned, by the people, an honest weak flock of sheep, who adored their emperor but loved their bishop.
Les Miserables	chapter xii.
Les Miserables	monseigneur's solitude.
Les Miserables	there is nearly always round a bishop a squad of little abbés, as there is a swarm of young officers round a general.
Les Miserables	they are what that delightful st. francis de sales calls somewhere "sucking priests."
Les Miserables	every career has its aspirants, who pay their respects to those who have reached the goal; there is not a power without its following, not a fortune without its court.
Les Miserables	the seekers for a future buzz round the splendid present.
Les Miserables	every metropolitan has his staff: every bishop who is at all influential has his patrol of seminarist cherubim, who go the rounds, maintain order in the episcopal palace, and mount guard round monseigneur's smile.
Les Miserables	pleasing a bishop is a foot in the stirrup for a sub-deaconry; after all, a man must make his way, and apostles do not despise canonries.
Les Miserables	in the same way as there are "gros bonnets," otherwhere, there are large mitres in the church.
Les Miserables	they are bishops who stand well with the court, well endowed, clever, favorites of society, who doubtless know how to pray, but also how to solicit, not scrupulous about having a whole diocese waiting in their ante-rooms, connecting links between the sacristy and diplomacy, more abbés than priests, rather prelates than bishops.
Les Miserables	happy the man who approaches them!
Les Miserables	as they stand in good credit they shower around them, on the obsequious and their favored, and on all the youth who know the art of pleasing, fat livings, prebends, archdeaconries, chaplaincies, and cathedral appointments, while waiting for episcopal dignities.
Les Miserables	while themselves advancing, they cause their satellites to progress, and it is an entire solar system moving onwards.
Les Miserables	their beams throw a purple hue over their suite, and their prosperity is showered over the actors behind the scenes in nice little bits of promotions.
Les Miserables	the larger the patron's diocese, the larger the favorite's living.
Les Miserables	and then there is rome.
Les Miserables	a bishop who contrives to become an archbishop, an archbishop who manages to become a cardinal, takes you with him as a conclavist; you enter the rota, you have the pallium, you are an auditor, a chamberlain, a monsignore, and from grandeur to eminence there is but a step, and between eminence and holiness there is only the smoke of the balloting tickets.
Les Miserables	every cassock can dream of the tiara.
Les Miserables	the priest is in our days the only man who can regularly become a king, and what a king!
Les Miserables	the supreme king!
Les Miserables	hence what a hotbed of longings is a seminary!
Les Miserables	how many blushing choristers, how many young abbés, have on their head perrette's milk-jar!
Les Miserables	how easily ambition calls itself a profession!
Les Miserables	and perhaps it does so in good faith and in self-deception, for it is so unworldly.
Les Miserables	monseigneur welcome, humble, poor, and out of the world, was not counted among the large mitres.
Les Miserables	this was visible in the utter absence of young priests around him.
Les Miserables	we have seen that at paris "he did not take," and not an aspirant tried to cling to this solitary old man; not the most youthful ambition tried to flourish in his shade.
Les Miserables	his canons and vicars were good old men, walled up like him in this diocese which had no issue to the cardinal's hat, and who resembled their bishop with this difference, that they were finished while he was completed.
Les Miserables	the impossibility of growing up near monseigneur welcome was so well felt, that young priests whom he ordained at once obtained letters commendatory to the archbishop of aix, or auch, and went off at score.
Les Miserables	for, after all, we repeat, men wish to be pushed upward.
Les Miserables	a saint who lives in a state of excessive self-denial is a dangerous neighbor, he might possibly communicate to you by contagion an incurable poverty, a stiffening of the joints useful for advancement, and, in a word, more renunciation than you care for: and such scabby virtue is shunned.
Les Miserables	hence came the isolation of monseigneur welcome.
Les Miserables	we live in the midst of a gloomy society.
Les Miserables	succeed,--such is the teaching which falls drop by drop from the corruption hanging over us.
Les Miserables	success is a very hideous thing, and its resemblance with merit deceives men.
Les Miserables	for the herd, success has nearly the same profile as supremacy.
Les Miserables	success, that twin brother of talent, has a dupe,--history.
Les Miserables	tacitus and juvenal alone grumble at it.
Les Miserables	in our days an almost official philosophy wears the livery of success, and waits in its ante-room.
Les Miserables	succeed, that is the theory, for prosperity presupposes capacity.
Les Miserables	win in the lottery and you are a clever man, for he who triumphs is revered.
Les Miserables	all you want is to be born under a fortunate star.
Les Miserables	have luck and you will have the rest, be fortunate and you will be thought a great man; leaving out five or six immense exceptions, which form the lustre of an age, contemporary admiration is blear-eyedness.
Les Miserables	gilding is gold, and it does you no harm to be any one so long as you are the parvenu.
Les Miserables	the mob is an old narcissus, adoring itself and applauding the mob.
Les Miserables	that enormous faculty by which a man is a moses, æschylus, dante, michael angelo, or napoleon, the multitude decrees broadcast and by acclamation to any one who attains his object, no matter in what.
Les Miserables	let a notary transfigure himself into a deputy; a false corneille produce tiridates; an eunuch contrive to possess a harem; a military prudhomme accidentally gain the decisive battle of an age; an apothecary invent cardboard soles for the army of the sambre-et-meuse, and make out of the cardboard sold for leather an income of 400,000 francs a year; a pedler espouse usury and put it to bed with seven or eight millions, of which he is the father and she the mother; a preacher become a bishop by his nasal twang; let the steward of a good family be so rich on leaving service that he is made chancellor of the exchequer--and men will call it genius, in the same way as they call mousqueton's face beauty and claude's mien majesty.
Les Miserables	they confound with the constellations of profundity the stars which the duck's feet make in the soft mud of the pond.
Les Miserables	chapter xiii.
Les Miserables	what he believed.
Les Miserables	it is not our business to gauge the bishop of d---- from an orthodox point of view.
Les Miserables	in the presence of such a soul we only feel inclined to respect.
Les Miserables	the conscience of the just man must be believed on its word; besides, certain natures granted, we admit the possibility of the development of all the beauties of human virtue in a creed differing from our own.
Les Miserables	what did he think of this dogma or that mystery?
Les Miserables	these heart-secrets are only known to the tomb which souls enter in a state of nudity.
Les Miserables	what we are certain of is, that he never solved difficulties of faith by hypocrisy.
Les Miserables	it is impossible for the diamond to rot.
Les Miserables	he believed as much as he possibly could, and would frequently exclaim, "i believe in the father."
Les Miserables	he also derived from his good deeds that amount of satisfaction which suffices the conscience, and which whispers to you, "you are with god."
Les Miserables	what we think it our duty to note is that, beyond his faith, he had an excess of love.
Les Miserables	it was through this, _quia multum amavit_, that he was considered vulnerable by "serious men," "grave persons," and "reasonable people," those favorite phrases of our melancholy world in which selfishness is under the guidance of pedantry.
Les Miserables	what was this excess of love.
Les Miserables	it was a serene benevolence, spreading over men, as we have already indicated, and on occasion extending even to things.
Les Miserables	he loved without disdain, and was indulgent to god's creation.
Les Miserables	every man, even the best, has in him an unreflecting harshness, which he reserves for animals, but the bishop of d---- had not this harshness, which is, however, peculiar to many priests.
Les Miserables	he did not go so far as the brahmin, but seemed to have meditated on the words of ecclesiastes--"who knoweth the spirit of the beast that goeth downward to the earth?"
Les Miserables	an ugly appearance, a deformity of instinct, did not trouble him or render him indignant; he was moved, almost softened, by them.
Les Miserables	it seemed as if he thoughtfully sought, beyond apparent life, for the cause, the explanation, or the excuse.
Les Miserables	he examined without anger, and with the eye of a linguist deciphering a palimpsest, the amount of chaos which still exists in nature.
Les Miserables	this reverie at times caused strange remarks to escape from him.
Les Miserables	one morning he was in his garden and fancied himself alone; but his sister was walking behind, though unseen by him.
Les Miserables	he stopped and looked at something on the ground.
Les Miserables	it was a large black, hairy, horrible spider.
Les Miserables	his sister heard him mutter, "poor brute, it is not thy fault."
Les Miserables	why should we not repeat this almost divine childishness of goodness?
Les Miserables	it may be puerile, but of such were the puerilities of st. francis d'assisi and marcus aurelius.
Les Miserables	one day he sprained himself because he did not wish to crush an ant.
Les Miserables	such was the way in which this just man lived: at times he fell asleep in his garden, and then nothing could be more venerable.
Les Miserables	monseigneur welcome had been formerly, if we may believe the stories about his youth and even his manhood, a passionate, perhaps violent man.
Les Miserables	his universal mansuetude was less a natural instinct than the result of a grand conviction, which had filtered through life into his heart, and slowly dropped into it thought by thought, for in a character, as in a rock, there may be waterholes.
Les Miserables	such hollows, however, are ineffaceable, such formations indestructible.
Les Miserables	in 1815, as we think we have said, he reached his seventy-fifth year, but did not seem sixty.
Les Miserables	he was not tall, and had a tendency to stoutness, which he strove to combat by long walks; he stood firmly, and was but very slightly built.
Les Miserables	but these are details from which we will not attempt to draw any conclusion, for gregory xvi.
Les Miserables	at the age of eighty was erect and smiling, which did not prevent him being a bad priest.
Les Miserables	monseigneur welcome had what people call "a fine head," which was so amiable that its beauty was forgotten.
Les Miserables	when he talked with that infantine gayety which was one of his graces you felt at your ease by his side, and joy seemed to emanate from his whole person.
Les Miserables	his fresh, ruddy complexion, and all his white teeth, which he had preserved and displayed when he laughed, gave him that open facile air which makes you say of an aged man, "he is a worthy person."
Les Miserables	that, it will be remembered, was the effect he produced on napoleon.
Les Miserables	at the first glance, and when you saw him for the first time, he was in reality only a worthy man, but if you remained some hours in his company, and saw him in thought, he became gradually transfigured and assumed something imposing; his wide and serious brow, already august through the white hair, became also august through meditation; majesty was evolved from the goodness; though the latter did not cease to gleam, you felt the same sort of emotion as you would if you saw a smiling angel slowly unfold his wings without ceasing to smile.
Les Miserables	an inexpressible respect gradually penetrated you and ascended to your head, and you felt that you had before you one of those powerful, well-bred, and indulgent souls whose thoughts are so great that they cannot but be gentle.
Les Miserables	as we have seen, prayer, celebration of the mass, almsgiving, consoling the afflicted, tilling a patch of ground, frugality, hospitality, self-denial, confidence, study, and labor, filled every day of his life.
Les Miserables	_filled_ is the exact word, and certainly the bishop's day was full of good thoughts, good words, and good actions.
Les Miserables	still, it was not complete.
Les Miserables	if cold or wet weather prevented him from spending an hour or two in the garden before going to bed after the two females had retired, it seemed as it were a species of rite of his to prepare himself for sleep by meditation, in the presence of the grand spectacle of the heavens by night.
Les Miserables	at times, even at an advanced hour of night, if the women were not asleep, they heard him slowly pacing the walks.
Les Miserables	he was then alone with himself, contemplative, peaceful, adoring, comparing the serenity of his heart with that of ether, affected in the darkness by the visible splendor of the constellations, and the invisible splendor of god, and opening his soul to thoughts which fall from the unknown.
Les Miserables	at such moments, offering up his heart at the hour when the nocturnal flowers offer up their perfumes, he could not have said himself, possibly, what was passing in his mind; but he felt something fly out of him and something descend into him.
Les Miserables	he dreamed of the grandeur and presence of god; of future eternity, that strange mystery; of past eternity, that even stranger mystery; of all the infinities which buried themselves before his eyes in all directions: and without seeking to comprehend the incomprehensible, he gazed at it.
Les Miserables	he did not study god; he was dazzled by him.
Les Miserables	he considered this magnificent concourse of atoms which reveals forces, creates individualities in unity, proportions in space, innumerability in the infinite, and through light produces beauty.
Les Miserables	such a concourse incessantly takes place, and is dissolved again, and hence come life and death.
Les Miserables	he would sit down on a wood bench with his back against a rickety trellis, and gaze at the stars through the stunted sickly profiles of his fruit trees.
Les Miserables	this quarter of an acre, so poorly planted, and so encumbered with sheds and out-houses, was dear to him, and was sufficient for him.
Les Miserables	what more was wanting to this aged man, who divided the leisure of his life, which knew so little leisure, between gardening by day and contemplation by night?
Les Miserables	was not this limited enclosure with the sky for its roof sufficient for him to be able to adore god by turns in his most delicious and most sublime works?
Les Miserables	was not this everything, in fact?
Les Miserables	and what could be desired beyond?
Les Miserables	a small garden to walk about in, and immensity to dream in; at his feet, what can be cultivated and gathered; over his head, what can be studied and meditated; a few flowers on the earth, and all the stars in the heavens.
Les Miserables	chapter xiv.
Les Miserables	what he thought.
Les Miserables	one last word.
Les Miserables	as these details might, especially at the present day, and to employ an expression which is now fashionable, give the bishop of d---- a certain "pantheistic" physiognomy, and cause it to be believed, either to his praise or blame, that he had in him one of those personal philosophies peculiar to our age, which germinate sometimes in solitary minds, and grow until they take the place of religion, we must lay stress on the fact that not one of the persons who knew monseigneur welcome believed himself authorized in thinking anything of the sort.
Les Miserables	what enlightened this man was his heart, and his wisdom was the product of the light which emanates from it.
Les Miserables	he had no systems, but abundance of deeds.
Les Miserables	abstruse speculations contain vertigo, and nothing indicates that he ventured his mind amid the apocalypses.
Les Miserables	the apostle may be bold, but the bishop must be timid.
Les Miserables	he probably refrained from going too deep into certain problems reserved to some extent for great and terrible minds.
Les Miserables	there is a sacred horror beneath the portals of the enigma; the dark chasms gape before you, but something tells you that you must not enter: woe to him who penetrates.
Les Miserables	geniuses, in the profundities of abstraction and pure speculation, being situated, so to speak, above dogmas, propose their ideas to god; their prayer audaciously offers a discussion, and their adoration interrogates.
Les Miserables	this is direct religion, full of anxiety and responsibility for the man who attempts to carry the escarpment by storm.
Les Miserables	human meditation has no limits; at its own risk and peril it analyzes and produces its own bedazzlement; we might almost say that, through a species of splendid reaction, it dazzles nature with it.
Les Miserables	the mysterious world around us gives back what it receives, and it is probable that the contemplators are contemplated.
Les Miserables	however this may be, there are in the world men--are they men?--who distinctly perceive on the horizon of dreamland the heights of the absolute, and have the terrible vision of the mountain of the infinite.
Les Miserables	monseigneur welcome was not one of these men, for he was not a genius.
Les Miserables	he would have feared these sublimities, on which even very great men, like swedenborg and pascal, fell in their insanity.
Les Miserables	assuredly, such powerful reveries have their utility, and by these arduous routes ideal perfection is approached, but he took a short-cut,--the gospel.
Les Miserables	he did not attempt to convert his chasuble into elijah's cloak, he cast no beam of the future over the gloomy heaving of events; there was nothing of the prophet or the magus about him.
Les Miserables	this humble soul loved, that was all.
Les Miserables	it is probable that he expanded prayer into a superhuman aspiration; but a man can no more pray too much than he can love too much, and if it were a heresy to pray further than the text, st theresa and st jérôme would be heretics.
Les Miserables	he bent down over all that groaned and all that expiated; the universe appeared to him an immense malady; he felt a fever everywhere; he heard the panting of suffering all around him, and without trying to solve the enigma, he sought to heal the wound.
Les Miserables	the formidable spectacle of created things developed tenderness in him; he was solely engaged in finding for himself and arousing in others the best way of pitying and relieving.
Les Miserables	existence was to this good and rare priest a permanent subject of sorrow seeking for consolation.
Les Miserables	there are some men who toil to extract gold, but he labored to extract pity; the universal wretchedness was his mine.
Les Miserables	sorrow all around was only an opportunity for constant kindness.
Les Miserables	"love one another" he declared to be complete; he wished for nothing more, and that was his entire doctrine.
Les Miserables	one day the senator, who believed himself a "philosopher," said to the bishop: "just look at the spectacle of the world; all are fighting, and the strongest man is the cleverest.
Les Miserables	your 'love one another' is nonsense."
Les Miserables	"well," monseigneur welcome replied, without discussion, "if it be nonsense, the soul must shut itself up in it like the pearl in the oyster."
Les Miserables	he consequently shut himself up in it, lived in it, was absolutely satisfied with it, leaving on one side those prodigious questions which attract and terrify, the unfathomable perspectives of the abstract, the precipices of metaphysics, all those depths which for the apostle converge in god, for the atheist in nothingness: destiny, good, and evil, the war of being against being, human consciousness, the pensive somnambulism of the animal, transformation through death, the recapitulation of existences which the grave contains, the incomprehensible grafting of successive loves on the enduring me, essence, substance, the nil and ens nature, liberty, necessity; in a word, he avoided all the gloomy precipices over which the gigantic archangels of the human mind bend, the formidable abysses which lucretius, manou, st. paul, and dante contemplate with that flashing eye which seems, in regarding infinity, to make stars sparkle in it.
Les Miserables	monseigneur welcome was simply a man who accepted mysterious questions without scrutinizing, disturbing them, or troubling his own mind, and who had in his soul a grave respect for the shadow.
Les Miserables	book ii.
Les Miserables	the fall.
Les Miserables	chapter i.   the close of a day's march.
Les Miserables	at the beginning of october, 1815, and about an hour before sunset, a man travelling on foot entered the little town of d----.
Les Miserables	the few inhabitants, who were at the moment at their windows or doors, regarded this traveller with a species of inquietude.
Les Miserables	it would be difficult to meet a wayfarer of more wretched appearance; he was a man of middle height, muscular and robust, and in the full vigor of life.
Les Miserables	he might be forty-six to forty-eight years of age.
Les Miserables	a cap with a leather peak partly concealed his sunburnt face, down which the perspiration streamed.
Les Miserables	his shirt of coarse yellow calico, fastened at the neck by a small silver anchor, allowed his hairy chest to be seen; he had on a neck-cloth twisted like a rope, trousers of blue ticking worn and threadbare, white at one knee and torn at the other; an old gray ragged blouse patched at one elbow with a rag of green cloth; on his back a large new well-filled and well-buckled knapsack, and a large knotty stick in his hand.
Les Miserables	his stockingless feet were thrust into iron-shod shoes, his hair was clipped, and his beard long.
Les Miserables	perspiration, heat, travelling on foot, and the dust, added something sordid to his wretched appearance.
Les Miserables	his hair was cut close and yet was bristling, for it was beginning to grow a little, and did not seem to have been cut for some time.
Les Miserables	no one knew him; he was evidently passing through the town.
Les Miserables	where did he come from?
Les Miserables	the south perhaps, the sea-board, for he made his entrance into d---- by the same road napoleon had driven along seven months previously when going from cannes to paris.
Les Miserables	the man must have been walking all day, for he seemed very tired.
Les Miserables	some women in the old suburb at the lower part of the town had seen him halt under the trees on the gassendi boulevard, and drink from the fountain at the end of the walk.
Les Miserables	he must have been very thirsty, for the children that followed him saw him stop and drink again at the fountain on the market-place.
Les Miserables	on reaching the corner of the rue poichevert, he turned to the left and proceeded to the mayor's office.
Les Miserables	he went in and came out again a quarter of an hour after.
Les Miserables	a gendarme was sitting on the stone bench near the door, on which general drouot had mounted on march 4th, to read to the startled town-folk of d---- the proclamation of the gulf of juan.
Les Miserables	the man doffed his cap and bowed humbly to the gendarme; the latter, without returning his salute, looked at him attentively, and then entered the office.
Les Miserables	there was at that time at d---- a capital inn, with the sign of the cross of colbas.
Les Miserables	this inn was kept by a certain jacquin labarre, a man highly respected in the town for his relationship to another labarre, who kept the three dolphins at grenoble, and had served in the guides.
Les Miserables	when the emperor landed, many rumors were current in the country about the three dolphins; it was said that general bertrand, in the disguise of a wagoner, had stopped there several times in the month of january, and distributed crosses of honor to the soldiers, and handsful of napoleons to the towns-people.
Les Miserables	the fact was that the emperor on entering grenoble refused to take up his quarters at the prefecture; he thanked the mayor, and said, "i am going to a worthy man whom i know," and he went to the three dolphins.
Les Miserables	the glory of the grenoble labarre was reflected for a distance of five-and-twenty leagues on the labarre of the cross of colbas.
Les Miserables	the towns-people said of him, "he is cousin to the one at grenoble."
Les Miserables	the man proceeded to this inn, which was the best in the town, and entered the kitchen, the door of which opened on the street.
Les Miserables	all the ovens were heated, and a large fire blazed cheerily in the chimney.
Les Miserables	the host, who was at the same time head-cook, went from the hearth to the stew-pans, very busy in attending to a dinner intended for the carriers, who could be heard singing and talking noisily in an adjoining room.
Les Miserables	any one who has travelled knows that no people feed so well as carriers.
Les Miserables	a fat marmot, flanked by white-legged partridges and grouse, was turning on a long spit before the fire; while two large carp from lake lauzet and an alloz trout were baking in the ovens.
Les Miserables	the landlord, on hearing the door open and a stranger enter, said, without raising his eyes from his stew-pans,--  "what do you want, sir?"
Les Miserables	"supper and a bed," the man replied.
Les Miserables	"nothing easier," said mine host.
Les Miserables	at this moment he looked up, took in the stranger's appearance at a glance, and added, "on paying."
Les Miserables	the man drew a heavy leathern purse from the pocket of his blouse, and replied,--  "i have money."
Les Miserables	"in that case i am at your service," said the host.
Les Miserables	the man returned the purse to his pocket, took off his knapsack, placed it on the ground near the door, kept his stick in his hand, and sat down on a low stool near the fire.
Les Miserables	d---- is in the mountains, and the evenings there are cold in october.
Les Miserables	while going backwards and forwards the landlord still inspected his guest.
Les Miserables	"will supper be ready soon?"
Les Miserables	the man asked.
Les Miserables	"directly."
Les Miserables	while the new-comer had his back turned to warm himself, the worthy landlord took a pencil from his pocket, and then tore off the corner of an old newspaper which lay on a small table near the window.
Les Miserables	on the white margin he wrote a line or two, folded up the paper, and handed it to a lad who seemed to serve both as turnspit and page.
Les Miserables	the landlord whispered a word in the boy's ear, and he ran off in the direction of the mayor's house.
Les Miserables	the traveller had seen nothing of all this, and he asked again whether supper would be ready soon.
Les Miserables	the boy came back with the paper in his hand, and the landlord eagerly unfolded it, like a man who is expecting an answer.
Les Miserables	he read it carefully, then shook his head, and remained thoughtful for a moment.
Les Miserables	at last he walked up to the traveller, who seemed plunged in anything but a pleasant reverie.
Les Miserables	"i cannot make room for you, sir," he said.
Les Miserables	the man half turned on his stool.
Les Miserables	"what do you mean?
Les Miserables	are you afraid i shall bilk you?
Les Miserables	do you want me to pay you in advance?
Les Miserables	i have money, i tell you."
Les Miserables	"it is not that"  "what is it, then?"
Les Miserables	"you have money."
Les Miserables	"yes," said the man.
Les Miserables	"but i have not a spare bed-room."
Les Miserables	the man continued quietly: "put me in the stables."
Les Miserables	"i cannot."
Les Miserables	"why?"
Les Miserables	"the horses take up all the room."
Les Miserables	"well," the man continued, "a corner in the loft and a truss of straw: we will see to that after supper."
Les Miserables	"i cannot give you any supper."
Les Miserables	this declaration, made in a measured but firm tone, seemed to the stranger serious.
Les Miserables	he rose.
Les Miserables	"nonsense, i am dying of hunger.
Les Miserables	i have been on my legs since sunrise, and have walked twelve leagues.
Les Miserables	i can pay, and demand food."
Les Miserables	"i have none," said the landlord.
Les Miserables	the man burst into a laugh, and turned to the chimney and the oven.
Les Miserables	"nothing!
Les Miserables	why, what is all this?"
Les Miserables	"all this is ordered."
Les Miserables	"by whom?"
Les Miserables	"by the carriers."
Les Miserables	"how many are there of them?"
Les Miserables	"twelve."
Les Miserables	"there is enough food here for twenty."
Les Miserables	the man sat down again, and said without raising his voice,--  "i am at an inn, i am hungry, and so shall remain."
Les Miserables	the landlord then stooped down, and whispered with an accent which made him start, "be off with you!"
Les Miserables	the stranger at this moment was thrusting some logs into the fire with the ferule of his stick, but he turned quickly, and as he was opening his mouth to reply, the landlord continued in the same low voice: "come, enough of this.
Les Miserables	do you wish me to tell you your name?
Les Miserables	it is jean valjean.
Les Miserables	now, do you wish me to tell you who you are?
Les Miserables	on seeing you come in i suspected something, so i sent to the police office, and this is the answer i received.
Les Miserables	can you read?"
Les Miserables	while saying this, he handed the stranger the paper which had travelled from the inn to the office and back again.
Les Miserables	the man took a glance at it, and mine host continued after a moment's silence,--  "i am accustomed to be polite with everybody.
Les Miserables	be off."
Les Miserables	the man stooped, picked up his knapsack, and went off.
Les Miserables	he walked along the high street hap-hazard, keeping close to the houses like a sad and humiliated man.
Les Miserables	he did not look back once; had he done so he would have seen the landlord of the cross of colbas in his doorway surrounded by all his guests and the passers-by, talking eagerly and pointing to him: and judging from the looks of suspicion and terror, he might have guessed that ere long his arrival would be the event of the whole town.
Les Miserables	he saw nothing of all this, for men who are oppressed do not look back, as they know only too well that an evil destiny is following them.
Les Miserables	he walked on thus for a long time, turning down streets he did not know, and forgetting his fatigue, as happens in sorrow.
Les Miserables	all at once he was sharply assailed by hunger: night was approaching, and he looked round to see whether he could not discover a shelter.
Les Miserables	the best inn was closed against him, and he sought some very humble pot-house, some wretched den.
Les Miserables	at this moment a lamp was lit at the end of the street, and a fir-branch hanging from an iron bar stood out on the white twilight sky.
Les Miserables	he went towards it: it was really a pot-house.
Les Miserables	the stranger stopped for a moment and looked through the window into the low tap-room, which was lighted up by a small lamp on the table and a large fire on the hearth.
Les Miserables	some men were drinking, and the landlord was warming himself; over the flames bubbled a caldron hanging from an iron hook.
Les Miserables	this pot-house, which is also a sort of inn, has two entrances, one on the street, the other opening on a small yard full of manure.
Les Miserables	the traveller did not dare enter by the street door: he slipped into the yard, stopped once again, and then timidly raised the latch and opened the door.
Les Miserables	"who's there?"
Les Miserables	the landlord asked.
Les Miserables	"some one who wants a supper and bed."
Les Miserables	"very good.
Les Miserables	they are to be had here."
Les Miserables	he went in, and all the topers turned to look at him; they examined him for some time while he was taking off his knapsack.
Les Miserables	said the landlord to him, "here is a fire; supper is boiling in the pot: come and warm yourself, comrade."
Les Miserables	he sat down in the ingle and stretched out his feet, which were swollen with fatigue.
Les Miserables	a pleasant smell issued from the caldron.
Les Miserables	all that could be distinguished of his face under his cap-peak assumed a vague appearance of comfort blended with the other wretched appearance which the habit of suffering produces.
Les Miserables	it was, moreover, a firm, energetic, and sad profile; the face was strangely composed, for it began by appearing humble and ended by becoming severe.
Les Miserables	his eyes gleamed under his brows, like a fire under brushwood.
Les Miserables	one of the men seated at the table was a fishmonger, who, before entering the pot-house, had gone to put up his horse in labarre's stables.
Les Miserables	accident willed it, that on the same morning he had met this ill-looking stranger walking between bras d'asse and--(i have forgotten the name, but i fancy it is escoublon).
Les Miserables	now, on meeting him, the man, who appeared very fatigued, had asked the fishmonger to give him a lift, which had only made him go the faster.
Les Miserables	this fishmonger had been half an hour previously one of the party surrounding jacquin labarre, and had told his unpleasant encounter in the morning to the people at the cross of colbas.
Les Miserables	he made an imperceptible sign to the landlord from his seat, and the latter went up to him, and they exchanged a few whispered words.
Les Miserables	the man had fallen back into his reverie.
Les Miserables	the landlord went up to the chimney, laid his hand sharply on the man's shoulder, and said to him,--  "you must be off from here."
Les Miserables	the stranger turned and replied gently, "ah, you know?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"i was turned out of the other inn."
Les Miserables	"and so you will be out of this."
Les Miserables	"where would you have me go?"
Les Miserables	"somewhere else."
Les Miserables	the man took his knapsack and stick and went away.
Les Miserables	as he stepped out, some boys who had followed him from the cross of colbas, and seemed to have been waiting for him, threw stones at him.
Les Miserables	he turned savagely, and threatened them with his stick, and the boys dispersed like a flock of birds.
Les Miserables	he passed in front of the prison, and pulled the iron bell-handle; a wicket was opened.
Les Miserables	"mr. jailer," he said, as he humbly doffed his cap, "would you be kind enough to open the door and give me a nights lodging?"
Les Miserables	a voice answered, "a prison is not an inn; get yourself arrested, and then i will open the door."
Les Miserables	the man entered a small street, in which there are numerous gardens, some of them being merely enclosed with hedges, which enliven the street.
Les Miserables	among these gardens and hedges he saw a single-storeyed house, whose window was illuminated, and he looked through the panes as he had done at the pot-house.
Les Miserables	it was a large white-washed room, with a bed with printed chintz curtains, and a cradle in a corner, a few chairs, and a double-barrelled gun hanging on the wall.
Les Miserables	a table was laid for supper in the middle of the room; a copper lamp lit up the coarse white cloth, the tin mug glistening like silver and full of wine, and the brown smoking soup-tureen.
Les Miserables	at this table was seated a man of about forty years of age, with a hearty, open face, who was riding a child on his knee.
Les Miserables	by his side a woman, still young, was suckling another child.
Les Miserables	the father was laughing, the children were laughing, and the mother was smiling.
Les Miserables	the stranger stood for a moment pensively before this gentle and calming spectacle; what was going on within him?
Les Miserables	it would be impossible to say, but it is probable that he thought that this joyous house would prove hospitable, and that where he saw so much happiness he might find a little pity.
Les Miserables	he tapped very slightly on a window pane, but was not heard; he tapped a second time, and he heard the woman say, "husband, i fancy i can hear some one knocking."
Les Miserables	"no," the husband answered.
Les Miserables	he tapped a third time.
Les Miserables	the husband rose, took the lamp, and walked to the front door.
Les Miserables	he was a tall man, half peasant, half artisan; he wore a huge, leathern apron, which came up to his left shoulder, and on which he carried a hammer, a red handkerchief, a powder-flask, and all sorts of things, which his belt held like a pocket.
Les Miserables	as he threw back his head, his turned-down shirt-collar displayed his full neck, white and bare.
Les Miserables	he had thick eye-brows, enormous black whiskers, eyes flush with his head, a bull-dog lower jaw, and over all this that air of being at home, which is inexpressible.
Les Miserables	"i beg your pardon, sir," the traveller said, "but would you, for payment, give me a plateful of soup and a corner to sleep in in your garden outhouse?"
Les Miserables	"who are you?"
Les Miserables	the owner of the cottage asked.
Les Miserables	the man answered, "i have come from puy moisson, i have walked the whole day.
Les Miserables	could you do it,--for payment of course?"
Les Miserables	"i would not refuse," the peasant answered, "to lodge any respectable person who paid.
Les Miserables	but why do you not go to the inn?"
Les Miserables	"there is no room there."
Les Miserables	"nonsense!
Les Miserables	that is impossible; it is neither market nor fair day.
Les Miserables	have you been to labarre's?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"well?"
Les Miserables	the traveller continued, with some hesitation, "i do not know why, but he refused to take me in."
Les Miserables	"have you been to what is his name, in the rue de chauffaut?"
Les Miserables	the stranger's embarrassment increased; he stammered, "he would not take me in either."
Les Miserables	the peasant's face assumed a suspicious look, he surveyed the new comer from head to foot, and all at once exclaimed with a sort of shudder,--  "can you be the man?..."
Les Miserables	he took another look at the stranger, placed the lamp on the table, and took down his gun.
Les Miserables	on hearing the peasant say "can you be the man?"
Les Miserables	his wife had risen, taken her two children in her arms, and hurriedly sought refuge behind her husband, and looked in horror at the stranger as she muttered, "the villain!"
Les Miserables	all this took place in less time than is needed to imagine it.
