Les Miserables	midnight was striking as the bishop returned from the garden to his room, and a few minutes later everybody was asleep in the small house.
Les Miserables	chapter vi.
Les Miserables	jean valjean.
Les Miserables	toward the middle of the night jean valjean awoke.
Les Miserables	he belonged to a poor peasant family of la brie.
Les Miserables	in his childhood he had not been taught to read, and when he was of man's age he was a wood-lopper at faverolles.
Les Miserables	his mother's name was jeanne mathieu, his father's jean valjean or vlajean, probably a sobriquet and a contraction of _voilà jean._ jean valjean possessed a pensive but not melancholy character, which is peculiar to affectionate natures; but altogether he was a dull, insignificant fellow, at least apparently.
Les Miserables	he had lost father and mother when still very young: the latter died of a badly-managed milk fever; the former, a pruner like himself, was killed by a fall from a tree.
Les Miserables	all that was left jean valjean was a sister older than himself, a widow with seven children, boys and girls.
Les Miserables	this sister brought jean valjean up, and so long as her husband was alive she supported her brother.
Les Miserables	when the husband died, the oldest of the seven children was eight years of age, the youngest, one, while jean valjean had just reached his twenty-fifth year; he took the place of the father, and in his turn supported the sister who had reared him.
Les Miserables	this was done simply as a duty, and even rather roughly by jean valjean; and his youth was thus expended in hard and ill-paid toil.
Les Miserables	he was never known to have had a sweetheart, for he had no time for love-making.
Les Miserables	at night he came home tired, and ate his soup without saying a word.
Les Miserables	his sister, mother jeanne, while he was eating, often took out of his porringer the best part of his meal, the piece of meat, the slice of bacon, or the heart of the cabbage, to give it to one of her children; he, still eating, bent over the table with his head almost in the soup, and his long hair falling round his porringer and hiding his eyes, pretended not to see it, and let her do as she pleased.
Les Miserables	there was at faverolles, not far from the valjeans' cottage, on the other side of the lane, a farmer's wife called marie claude.
Les Miserables	the young valjeans, who were habitually starving, would go at times and borrow in their mother's name a pint of milk from marie claude, which they drank behind a hedge or in some corner, tearing the vessel from each other so eagerly that the little girls spilt the milk over their aprons.
Les Miserables	their mother, had she been aware of this fraud, would have severely corrected the delinquents, but jean valjean, coarse and rough though he was, paid marie claude for the milk behind his sister's back, and the children were not punished.
Les Miserables	he earned in the pruning season eighteen sous a day, and besides hired himself out as reaper, laborer, neat-herd, and odd man.
Les Miserables	he did what he could; his sister worked too, but what could she do with seven children?
Les Miserables	it was a sad group, which wretchedness gradually enveloped and choked.
Les Miserables	one winter was hard, and jean had no work to do, and the family had no bread.
Les Miserables	no bread, literally none, and seven children!
Les Miserables	one sunday evening, maubert isabeau, the baker in the church square at faverolles, was just going to bed when he heard a violent blow dealt the grating in front of his shop.
Les Miserables	he arrived in time to see an arm passed through a hole made by a fist through the grating and window pane; the arm seized a loaf, and carried it off.
Les Miserables	isabeau ran out hastily; the thief ran away at his hardest, but the baker caught him and stopped him.
Les Miserables	the thief had thrown away the loaf, but his arm was still bleeding; it was jean valjean.
Les Miserables	this took place in 1795. jean valjean was brought before the courts of the day, charged "with burglary committed with violence at night, in an inhabited house."
Les Miserables	he had a gun, was a splendid shot, and a bit of a poacher, and this injured him.
Les Miserables	there is a legitimate prejudice against poachers, for, like smugglers, they trench very closely on brigandage.
Les Miserables	still we must remark that there is an abyss between these classes and the hideous assassins of our cities: the poacher lives in the forest; the smuggler in the mountains and on the sea.
Les Miserables	cities produce ferocious men, because they produce corrupted men; the forest, the mountain, and the sea produce savage men, but while they develop their ferocious side, they do not always destroy their human part.
Les Miserables	jean valjean was found guilty, and the terms of the code were precise.
Les Miserables	there are in our civilization formidable hours; they are those moments in which penal justice pronounces a shipwreck.
Les Miserables	what a mournful minute is that in which society withdraws and consummates the irreparable abandonment of a thinking being!
Les Miserables	jean valjean was sentenced to five years at the galleys.
Les Miserables	on april 22d, 1796, men were crying in the streets of paris the victory of montenotte, gained by the general-in-chief of the army of italy, whom the message of the directory to the five hundred, of the 2 floréal, year iv., calls buona-parte; and on the same day a heavy gang was put in chains at bicetre, and jean valjean formed part of the chain.
Les Miserables	an ex-jailer of the prison, who is now nearly ninety years of age, perfectly remembers the wretched man, who was chained at the end of the fourth cordon, in the north angle of the court-yard.
Les Miserables	he was seated on the ground like the rest, and seemed not at all to understand his position, except that it was horrible.
Les Miserables	it is probable that he also saw something excessive through the vague ideas of an utterly ignorant man.
Les Miserables	while the bolt of his iron collar was being riveted with heavy hammer-blows behind his head, he wept, tears choked him, and prevented him from speaking, and he could only manage to say from time to time: "i was a wood-cutter at faverolles."
Les Miserables	then, while still continuing to sob, he raised his right hand, and lowered it gradually seven times, as if touching seven uneven heads in turn, and from this gesture it could be guessed that whatever the crime he had committed, he had done it to feed and clothe seven children.
Les Miserables	he started for toulon, and arrived there after a journey of twenty-seven days in a cart, with the chain on his neck.
Les Miserables	at toulon he was dressed in the red jacket.
Les Miserables	all that had hitherto been his life, even to his name, was effaced.
Les Miserables	he was no longer jean valjean, but no.
Les Miserables	24,601. what became of his sister?
Les Miserables	what became of the seven children?
Les Miserables	who troubles himself about that?
Les Miserables	what becomes of the spray of leaves when the stem of the young tree has been cut at the foot?
Les Miserables	it is always the same story.
Les Miserables	these poor living beings, these creatures of god, henceforth without support, guide, or shelter, went off hap-hazard, and gradually buried themselves in that cold fog in which solitary destinies are swallowed up, that mournful gloom in which so many unfortunates disappear during the sullen progress of the human race.
Les Miserables	they left their country; what had once been their steeple forgot them; what had once been their hedge-row forgot them; and after a few years' stay in the bagne, jean valjean himself forgot them.
Les Miserables	in that heart where there had once been a wound there was now a scar: that was all.
Les Miserables	he only heard about his sister once during the whole time he spent at toulon; it was, i believe, toward the end of the fourth year of his captivity, though i have forgotten in what way the information reached him.
Les Miserables	she was in paris, living in the rue du geindre, a poor street, near st. sulpice, and had only one child with her, the youngest, a boy.
Les Miserables	where were the other six?
Les Miserables	perhaps she did not know herself.
Les Miserables	every morning she went to a printing-office, no.
Les Miserables	3, rue du sabot, where she was a folder and stitcher; she had to be there at six in the morning, long before daylight in winter.
Les Miserables	in the same house as the printing-office there was a day-school, to which she took the little boy, who was seven years of age, but as she went to work at six and the school did not open till seven o'clock, the boy was compelled to wait in the yard for an hour, in winter,--an hour of night in the open air.
Les Miserables	the boy was not allowed to enter the printing-office, because it was said that he would be in the way.
Les Miserables	the workmen as they passed in the morning saw the poor little fellow seated on the pavement, and often sleeping in the darkness, with his head on his satchel.
Les Miserables	when it rained, an old woman, the portress, took pity on him; she invited him into her den, where there were only a bed, a spinning-wheel, and two chairs, when the little fellow fell asleep in a corner, clinging to the cat, to keep him warm.
Les Miserables	this is what jean valjean was told; it was a momentary flash, as it were a window suddenly opened in the destiny of the beings he had loved, and then all was closed again; he never heard about them more.
Les Miserables	nothing reached him from them; he never saw them again, never met them, and we shall not come across them in the course of this melancholy narrative.
Les Miserables	toward the end of this fourth year, jean valjean's turn to escape arrived, and his comrades aided him as they always do in this sorrowful place.
Les Miserables	he escaped and wandered about the fields at liberty for two days: if it is liberty to be hunted down; to turn ones head at every moment; to start at the slightest sound; to be afraid of everything,--of a chimney that smokes, a man who passes, a barking dog, a galloping horse, the striking of the hour, of day because people see, of night because they do not see, of the highway, the path, the thicket, and even sleep.
Les Miserables	on the evening of the second day he was recaptured; he had not eaten or slept for six-and-thirty hours.
Les Miserables	the maritime tribunal added three years to his sentence for his crime, which made it eight years.
Les Miserables	in the sixth year, it was again his turn to escape; he tried, but could not succeed.
Les Miserables	he was missing at roll-call, the gun was fired, and at night the watchman found him hidden under the keel of a ship that was building, and he resisted the _garde chiourme_, who seized him.
Les Miserables	escape and rebellion: this fact, foreseen by the special code, was punished by an addition of five years, of which two would be spent in double chains.
Les Miserables	thirteen years.
Les Miserables	in his tenth year his turn came again, and he took advantage of it, but succeeded no better: three years for this new attempt, or sixteen years in all.
Les Miserables	finally, i think it was during his thirteenth year that he made a last attempt, and only succeeded so far as to be recaptured in four hours: three years for these four hours, and a total of nineteen years.
Les Miserables	in october, 1815, he was liberated; he had gone in in 1796 for breaking a window and stealing a loaf.
Les Miserables	let us make room for a short parenthesis.
Les Miserables	this is the second time that, during his essays on the penal question and condemnation by the law, the author of this book has come across a loaf as the starting point of the disaster of a destiny.
Les Miserables	claude gueux stole a loaf, and so did jean valjean, and english statistics prove that in london four robberies out of five have hunger as their immediate cause.
Les Miserables	jean valjean entered the bagne sobbing and shuddering: he left it stoically.
Les Miserables	he entered it in despair: he came out of it gloomy.
Les Miserables	what had taken place in this soul?
Les Miserables	chapter vii.
Les Miserables	a desperate man's heart.
Les Miserables	society must necessarily look at these things, because they are created by it.
Les Miserables	he was, as we have said, an ignorant man, but he was not weak-minded.
Les Miserables	the natural light was kindled within him, and misfortune, which also has its brightness, increased the little daylight there was in this mind.
Les Miserables	under the stick and the chain in the dungeon, when at work, beneath the torrid sun of the bagne, or when lying on the convict's plank, he reflected.
Les Miserables	he constituted himself a court, and began by trying himself.
Les Miserables	he recognized that he was not an innocent man unjustly punished; he confessed to himself that he had committed an extreme and blamable action; that the loaf would probably not have been refused him had he asked for it; that in any case it would have been better to wait for it, either from pity or from labor, and that it was not a thoroughly unanswerable argument to say, "can a man wait when he is hungry?"
Les Miserables	that, in the first place, it is very rare for a man to die literally of hunger; next, that, unhappily or happily, man is so made that he can suffer for a long time and severely, morally and physically, without dying; that hence he should have been patient; that it would have been better for the poor little children; that it was an act of madness for him, a wretched weak man, violently to collar society and to imagine that a man can escape from wretchedness by theft; that in any case the door by which a man enters infamy is a bad one by which to escape from wretchedness; and, in short, that he had been in the wrong.
Les Miserables	then he asked himself if he were the only person who had been in the wrong in his fatal history?
Les Miserables	whether, in the first place, it was not a serious thing that he, a workman, should want for work; that he, laborious as he was, should want for bread?
Les Miserables	whether, next, when the fault was committed and confessed, the punishment had not been ferocious and excessive, and whether there were not more abuse on the side of the law in the penalty than there was on the side of the culprit in the crime?
Les Miserables	whether there had not been an excessive weight in one of the scales, that one in which expiation lies?
Les Miserables	whether the excess of punishment were not the effacement of the crime, and led to the result of making a victim of the culprit, a creditor of the debtor, and definitively placing the right on the side of the man who had violated it?
Les Miserables	whether this penalty, complicated by excessive aggravations for attempted escapes, did not eventually become a sort of attack made by the stronger on the weaker, a crime of society committed on the individual, a crime which was renewed every day, and had lasted for nineteen years?
Les Miserables	he asked himself if human society could have the right to make its members equally undergo, on one side, its unreasonable improvidence, on the other its pitiless foresight, and to hold a man eternally between a want and an excess, want of work and excess of punishment?
Les Miserables	whether it were not exorbitant that society should treat thus its members who were worst endowed in that division of property which is made by chance, and consequently the most worthy of indulgence?
Les Miserables	these questions asked and solved, he passed sentence on society and condemned it--to his hatred.
Les Miserables	he made it responsible for the fate he underwent, and said to himself that he would not hesitate to call it to account some day.
Les Miserables	he declared that there was no equilibrium between the damage he had caused and the damage caused him; and he came to the conclusion that his punishment was not an injustice, but most assuredly an iniquity.
Les Miserables	wrath may be wild and absurd; a man may be wrongly irritated; but he is only indignant when he has some show of reason somewhere.
Les Miserables	jean valjean felt indignant.
Les Miserables	and then, again, human society had never done him aught but harm, he had only seen its wrathful face, which is called its justice, and shows itself to those whom it strikes.
Les Miserables	men had only laid hands on him to injure him, and any contact with them had been a blow to him.
Les Miserables	never, since his infancy, since his mother and his sister, had he heard a kind word or met a friendly look.
Les Miserables	from suffering after suffering, he gradually attained the conviction that life was war, and that in this war he was the vanquished.
Les Miserables	as he had no other weapon but his hatred, he resolved to sharpen it in the bagne and take it with him when he left.
Les Miserables	there was at toulon a school for the chain-gang, kept by the ignorantin brethren, who imparted elementary instruction to those wretches who were willing to learn.
Les Miserables	he was one of the number, and went to school at the age of forty, where he learned reading, writing, and arithmetic; he felt that strengthening his mind was strengthening his hatred.
Les Miserables	in certain cases, instruction and education may serve as allies to evil.
Les Miserables	it is sad to say, that after trying society which had caused his misfortunes, he tried providence, who had made society, and condemned it also.
Les Miserables	hence, during these nineteen years of torture and slavery, this soul ascended and descended at the same time; light entered on one side and darkness on the other.
Les Miserables	as we have seen, jean valjean was not naturally bad, he was still good when he arrived at the bagne.
Les Miserables	he condemned society then, and felt that he was growing wicked; he condemned providence, and felt that he was growing impious.
Les Miserables	here it is difficult not to meditate for a moment.
Les Miserables	is human nature thus utterly transformed?
Les Miserables	can man, who is created good by god, be made bad by man?
Les Miserables	can the soul be entirely remade by destiny, and become evil if the destiny be evil?
Les Miserables	can the heart be deformed, and contract incurable ugliness and infirmity under the pressure of disproportionate misfortune, like the spine beneath too low a vault?
Les Miserables	is there not in every human soul, was there not in that of jean valjean especially, a primary spark, a divine element, incorruptible in this world, and immortal for the other, which good can develop, illumine, and cause to glisten splendidly, and which evil can never entirely extinguish?
Les Miserables	these are grave and obscure questions, the last of which every physiologist would unhesitatingly have answered in the negative, had he seen at toulon, in those hours of repose which were for jean valjean hours of reverie, this gloomy, stern, silent, and pensive galley-slave--the pariah of the law which regarded men passionately--the condemned of civilization, who regarded heaven with severity--seated with folded arms on a capstan bar, with the end of his chain thrust into his pocket to prevent it from dragging.
Les Miserables	we assuredly do not deny that the physiological observer would have seen there an irremediable misery; he would probably have pitied this patient of the law, but he would not have even attempted a cure: he would have turned away from the caverns he noticed in this soul, and, like dante at the gates of the inferno, he would have effaced from this existence that word which god, however, has written on the brow of every man: _hope!_  was this state of his soul, which we have attempted to analyze, as perfectly clear to jean valjean as we have tried to render it to our readers?
Les Miserables	did jean valjean see after their formation, and had he seen distinctly as they were formed, all the elements of which his moral wretchedness was composed?
Les Miserables	had this rude and unlettered man clearly comprehended the succession of ideas by which he had step by step ascended and descended to the gloomy views which had for so many years been the inner horizon of his mind?
Les Miserables	was he really conscious of all that had taken place in him and all that was stirring in him?
Les Miserables	this we should not like to assert, and, indeed, we are not inclined to believe it.
Les Miserables	there was too much ignorance in jean valjean for a considerable amount of vagueness not to remain, even after so much misfortune; at times he did not even know exactly what he experienced.
Les Miserables	jean valjean was in darkness; he suffered in darkness, and he hated in darkness.
Les Miserables	he lived habitually in this shadow, groping like a blind man and a dreamer; at times he was attacked, both internally and externally, by a shock of passion, a surcharge of suffering, a pale and rapid flash which illumined his whole soul, and suddenly made him see all around, both before and behind him, in the glare of a frightful light, the hideous precipices and gloomy perspective of his destiny.
Les Miserables	when the flash had passed, night encompassed him again, and where was he?
Les Miserables	he no longer knew.
Les Miserables	the peculiarity of punishments of this nature, in which nought but what is pitiless, that is to say brutalizing, prevails, is gradually, and by a species of stupid transfiguration, to transform a man into a wild beast, at times a ferocious beast.
Les Miserables	jean valjean's attempted escapes, successive and obstinate, would be sufficient to prove the strange work carried on by the law upon a human soul; he would have renewed these attempts, so utterly useless and mad, as many times as the opportunity offered itself, without dreaming for a moment of the result, or the experiments already made.
Les Miserables	he escaped impetuously like the wolf that finds its cage open.
Les Miserables	instinct said to him, "run away;" reasoning would have said to him, "remain;" but in the presence of so violent a temptation, reason disappeared and instinct alone was left.
Les Miserables	the brute alone acted, and when he was recaptured the new severities inflicted on him only served to render him more wild.
Les Miserables	one fact we must not omit mentioning is that he possessed a physical strength with which no one in the bagne could compete.
Les Miserables	in turning a capstan, jean valjean was equal to four men; he frequently raised and held on his back enormous weights, and took the place at times of that instrument which is called a jack, and was formerly called _orgueil_, from which, by the way, the rue montorgueil derived its name.
Les Miserables	his comrades surnamed him jean the jack.
Les Miserables	once when the balcony of the town hall at toulon was being repaired, one of those admirable caryatides of puget's which support the balcony, became loose and almost fell.
Les Miserables	jean valjean, who was on the spot, supported the statue with his shoulder, and thus gave the workmen time to come up.
Les Miserables	his suppleness even exceeded his vigor.
Les Miserables	some convicts, who perpetually dream of escaping, eventually make a real science of combined skill and strength; it is the science of the muscles.
Les Miserables	a full course of mysterious statics is daily practised by the prisoners, those eternal enviers of flies and birds.
Les Miserables	swarming up a perpendicular, and finding a resting-place where a projection is scarcely visible, was child's play for jean valjean.
Les Miserables	given a corner of a wall, with the tension of his back and hams, with his elbows and heels clinging to the rough stone, he would hoist himself as if by magic to a third story, and at times would ascend to the very roof of the bagne.
Les Miserables	he spoke little and never laughed; it needed some extreme emotion to draw from him, once or twice a year, that mournful convict laugh, which is, as it were, the echo of fiendish laughter.
Les Miserables	to look at him, he seemed engaged in continually gazing at something terrible.
Les Miserables	he was, in fact, absorbed.
Les Miserables	through the sickly perceptions of an incomplete nature and a crushed intellect, he saw confusedly that a monstrous thing was hanging over him.
Les Miserables	in this obscure and dull gloom through which he crawled, wherever he turned his head and essayed to raise his eye, he saw, with a terror blended with rage, built up above him, with frightfully scarped sides, a species of terrific pile of things, laws, prejudices, men, and facts, whose outline escaped him, whose mass terrified him, and which was nothing else but that prodigious pyramid which we call civilization.
Les Miserables	he distinguished here and there in this heaving and shapeless conglomeration--at one moment close to him, at another on distant and inaccessible plateaux--some highly illumined group;--here the jailer and his stick, there the gendarme and his sabre, down below the mitred archbishop, and on the summit, in a species of sun, the crowned and dazzling emperor.
Les Miserables	it seemed to him as if this distant splendor, far from dissipating his night, only rendered it more gloomy and black.
Les Miserables	all these laws, prejudices, facts, men, and things, came and went above him, in accordance with the complicated and mysterious movement which god imprints on civilization, marching over him, and crushing him with something painful in its cruelty and inexorable in its indifférence.
Les Miserables	souls which have fallen into the abyss of possible misfortune, hapless men lost in the depths of those limbos into which people no longer look, and the reprobates of the law, feel on their heads the whole weight of the human society which is so formidable for those outside it, so terrific for those beneath it.
Les Miserables	in this situation, jean valjean thought, and what could be the nature of his reverie?
Les Miserables	if the grain of corn had its thoughts, when ground by the mill-stone, it would doubtless think as did jean valjean.
Les Miserables	all these things, realities full of spectres, phantasmagorias full of reality, ended by creating for him a sort of internal condition which is almost inexpressible.
Les Miserables	at times, in the midst of his galley-slave toil, he stopped and began thinking; his reason, at once riper and more troubled than of yore, revolted.
Les Miserables	all that had happened appeared to him absurd; all that surrounded him seemed to him impossible.
Les Miserables	he said to himself that it was a dream; he looked at the overseer standing a few yards from him, and he appeared to him a phantom, until the phantom suddenly dealt him a blow with a stick.
Les Miserables	visible nature scarce existed for him; we might almost say with truth, that for jean valjean there was no sun, no glorious summer-day, no brilliant sky, no fresh april dawn; we cannot describe the gloomy light which illumined his soul.
Les Miserables	in conclusion, to sum up all that can be summed up in what we have indicated, we will confine ourselves to establishing the fact that in nineteen years, jean valjean, the inoffensive wood-cutter of faverolles, and the formidable galley-slave of toulon, had become, thanks to the manner in which the bagne had fashioned him, capable of two sorts of bad actions: first, a rapid, unreflecting bad deed, entirely instinctive, and a species of reprisal for the evil he had suffered; and, secondly, of a grave, serious evil deed, discussed conscientiously and meditated with the false ideas which such a misfortune can produce.
Les Miserables	his premeditations passed through the three successive phases which natures of a certain temperament can alone undergo,--reasoning, will, and obstinacy.
Les Miserables	he had for his motives habitual indignation, bitterness of soul, the profound feeling of iniquities endured, and reaction even against the good, the innocent, and the just, if such exist.
Les Miserables	the starting-point, like the goal, of all his thoughts, was hatred of human law; that hatred, which, if it be not arrested in its development by some providential incident, becomes within a given time a hatred of society, then a hatred of the human race, next a hatred of creation, and which is expressed by a vague, incessant, and brutal desire to injure some one, no matter whom.
Les Miserables	as we see, it was not unfairly that the passport described jean valjean as a highly dangerous man.
Les Miserables	year by year this soul had become more and more withered, slowly but fatally.
Les Miserables	a dry soul must have a dry eye, and on leaving the bagne, nineteen years had elapsed since he had shed a tear.
Les Miserables	chapter viii.
Les Miserables	the wave and the darkness.
Les Miserables	man overboard!
Les Miserables	what of it?
Les Miserables	the ship does not stop.
Les Miserables	the wind is blowing, and this dark ship has a course which she must keep.
Les Miserables	she goes right on.
Les Miserables	the man disappears, then appears again.
Les Miserables	he goes down and again comes up to the surface; he shouts, he holds up his arms, but they do not hear him.
Les Miserables	the ship, shivering under the storm, has all she can do to take care of herself.
Les Miserables	the sailors and the passengers can no longer even see the drowning man; his luckless head is only a speck in the vastness of the waves.
Les Miserables	his cries of despair sound through the depths.
Les Miserables	what a phantom that is,--that sail, fast disappearing from view!
Les Miserables	he gazes after it; his eyes are fixed upon it with frenzy.
Les Miserables	it is disappearing, it is fading from sight, it is growing smaller and smaller.
Les Miserables	only just now he was there; he was one of the crew; he was going and coming on the deck with the rest; he had his share of air and sun; he was a living man.
Les Miserables	what, then, has happened?
Les Miserables	he has slipped, he has fallen; it is all over with him.
Les Miserables	he is in the huge waves.
Les Miserables	there is nothing now under his feet but death and sinking.
Les Miserables	the fearful waves, torn and frayed by the wind, surround him; the swells of the abyss sweep him along; all the crests of the waves are blown about his head; a crowd of waves spit upon him; uncertain gulfs half swallow him; every time he plunges down he catches a glimpse of precipices black as night; frightful, unknown seaweeds seize him, tie his feet, drag him down to them.
Les Miserables	he feels that he is becoming a part of the abyss, of the foam; the waves throw him from one to another; he tastes the bitterness; the cowardly ocean has given itself up to drowning him; the vastness sports with his agony.
Les Miserables	all this water seems to be hate.
Les Miserables	still he struggles.
Les Miserables	he tries to save himself, to keep himself up; he strikes out, he swims.
Les Miserables	he, this pitiful force, at once exhausted, is matched against the inexhaustible.
Les Miserables	where is the ship now?
Les Miserables	way down there, barely visible in the pale obscurity of the horizon.
Les Miserables	the squalls hum about him, the wave-crests wash over him.
Les Miserables	he raises his eyes, and sees only the lividness of the clouds.
Les Miserables	in his death struggle he takes part in the madness of the sea.
Les Miserables	he is tortured by this madness.
Les Miserables	he hears sounds, strange to man, which seem to come from beyond the earth, and from some terrible world outside.
Les Miserables	there are birds in the clouds, just as there are angels above human griefs, but what can they do for him?
Les Miserables	there is one, flying, singing, and hovering, while he has the death-rattle in his throat.
Les Miserables	he feels himself buried at the same time by these two infinites, the ocean and the heavens; the one a tomb, the other a shroud.
Les Miserables	night falls; he has been swimming now for hours; his strength has reached its end; this ship, this far-off thing where there were men, is blotted from his sight; he is alone in the fearful gulf of twilight; he sinks, he braces himself, he writhes, he feels below him the roving monsters of the invisible.
Les Miserables	he cries aloud.
Les Miserables	"there are no longer any men here."
Les Miserables	"where is god?"
Les Miserables	he calls "somebody!"
Les Miserables	"somebody!"
Les Miserables	he keeps on calling.
Les Miserables	nothing on the horizon; nothing in heaven.
Les Miserables	he implores the waste of waters, the wave, the seaweed, the rock; it is deaf.
Les Miserables	he supplicates the tempest; the pitiless tempest obeys only the infinite.
Les Miserables	around him is darkness, mist, solitude, the stormy and unreasoning tumult, the boundless rolling of the wild waters.
Les Miserables	in him is horror and weariness.
Les Miserables	under him the abyss.
Les Miserables	there is nothing to rest on.
Les Miserables	he thinks of what will happen to his body in the boundless shades.
Les Miserables	the infinite cold benumbs him.
Les Miserables	his hands shrivel; they clutch and find nothing.
Les Miserables	winds, clouds, whirlwinds, puffs, useless stars.
Les Miserables	what is he to do?
Les Miserables	in despair, he gives up.
Les Miserables	worn out as he is, he makes up his mind to die, he abandons himself, he lets himself go, he relaxes himself, and there he is rolling forever into the dismal depths in which he is swallowed up.
Les Miserables	oh, implacable course of human society!
Les Miserables	what a loss of men and of souls on the way!
Les Miserables	ocean into which falls all that the law lets fall.
Les Miserables	wicked vanishing of help!
Les Miserables	oh, moral death!
Les Miserables	the sea is the pitiless social night into which the penal law thrusts its condemned; the sea is boundless wretchedness.
Les Miserables	the soul, swept with the stream into this gulf, may be drowned.
Les Miserables	who will bring it to life again?
Les Miserables	chapter ix.
Les Miserables	new wrongs.
Les Miserables	when the hour for quitting the bagne arrived, when jean valjean heard in his ear the unfamiliar words "you are free," the moment seemed improbable and extraordinary, and a ray of bright light, of the light of the living, penetrated to him; but it soon grew pale.
Les Miserables	jean valjean had been dazzled by the idea of liberty, and had believed in a new life, but he soon saw that it is a liberty to which a yellow passport is granted.
Les Miserables	and around this there was much bitterness; he had calculated that his earnings, during his stay at the bagne, should have amounted to 171 francs.
Les Miserables	we are bound to add that he had omitted to take into his calculations the forced rest of sundays and holidays, which, during nineteen years, entailed a diminution of about 24 francs.
Les Miserables	however this might be, the sum was reduced, through various local stoppages, to 109 francs, 15 sous, which were paid to him when he left the bagne.
Les Miserables	he did not understand it all, and fancied that he had been robbed.
Les Miserables	on the day after his liberation, he saw at grasse men in front of a distillery of orange-flower water,--men unloading bales; he offered his services, and as the work was of a pressing nature, they were accepted.
Les Miserables	he set to work; he was intelligent, powerful, and skilful, and his master appeared satisfied.
Les Miserables	while he was at work a gendarme passed, noticed him, asked for his paper, and he was compelled to show his yellow pass.
Les Miserables	this done, jean valjean resumed his toil.
Les Miserables	a little while previously he had asked one of the workmen what he earned for his day's work, and the answer was 30 sous.
Les Miserables	at night, as he was compelled to start again the next morning, he went to the master of the distillery and asked for payment; the master did not say a word, but gave him 15 sous, and when he protested, the answer was, "that is enough for you."
Les Miserables	he became pressing, the master looked him in the face and said, "mind you don't get into prison."
Les Miserables	here again he regarded himself as robbed; society, the state, by diminishing his earnings, had robbed him wholesale; now it was the turn of the individual to commit retail robbery.
Les Miserables	liberation is not deliverance; a man may leave the bagne, but not condemnation.
Les Miserables	we have seen what happened to him at grasse, and we know how he was treated at d----.
Les Miserables	chapter x.  the man awake.
Les Miserables	as two o'clock pealed from the cathedral bell, jean valjean awoke.
Les Miserables	what aroused him was that the bed was too comfortable, for close on twenty years he had not slept in a bed, and though he had not undressed, the sensation was too novel not to disturb his sleep.
Les Miserables	he had been asleep for more than four hours, and his weariness had worn off; and he was accustomed not to grant many hours to repose.
Les Miserables	he opened his eyes and looked into the surrounding darkness, and then he closed them again to go to sleep once more.
Les Miserables	when many diverse sensations have agitated a day, and when matters preoccupy the mind, a man may sleep, but he cannot go to sleep again.
Les Miserables	sleep comes more easily than it returns, and this happened to jean valjean.
Les Miserables	as he could not go to sleep again, he began thinking.
Les Miserables	it was one of those moments in which the ideas that occupy the mind are troubled, and there was a species of obscure oscillation in his brain.
Les Miserables	his old recollections and immediate recollections crossed each other, and floated confusedly, losing their shape, growing enormously, and then disappearing suddenly, as if in troubled and muddy water.
Les Miserables	many thoughts occurred to him, but there was one which constantly reverted and expelled all the rest.
Les Miserables	this thought we will at once describe; he had noticed the six silver forks and spoons and the great ladle which madame magloire put on the table.
Les Miserables	this plate overwhelmed him; it was there, a few yards from him.
Les Miserables	when he crossed the adjoining room to reach the one in which he now was, the old servant was putting it in a small cupboard at the bed-head,--he had carefully noticed this cupboard; it was on the right as you came in from the dining-room.
Les Miserables	the plate was heavy and old, the big soup-ladle was worth at least 200 francs, or double what he had earned in nineteen years, though it was true that he would have earned more had not the officials robbed him.
Les Miserables	his mind oscillated for a good hour, in these fluctuations with which a struggle was most assuredly blended.
Les Miserables	when three o'clock struck he opened his eyes, suddenly sat up, stretched out his arms, and felt for his knapsack which he had thrown into a corner of the alcove, then let his legs hang, and felt himself seated on the bed-side almost without knowing how.
Les Miserables	he remained for a while thoughtfully in this attitude, which would have had something sinister about it, for any one who had seen him, the only wakeful person in the house.
Les Miserables	all at once he stooped, took off his shoes, then resumed his thoughtful posture, and remained motionless.
Les Miserables	in the midst of this hideous meditation, the ideas which we have indicated incessantly crossed his brain, entered, went out, returned, and weighed upon him; and then he thought, without knowing why, and with the mechanical obstinacy of reverie, of a convict he had known at the bagne, of the name of brevet, whose trousers were only held up by a single knitted brace.
Les Miserables	the draught-board design of that brace incessantly returned to his mind.
Les Miserables	he remained in this situation, and would have probably remained so till sunrise, had not the clock struck the quarter or the half-hour.
Les Miserables	it seemed as if this stroke said to him, to work!
Les Miserables	he rose, hesitated for a moment and listened; all was silent in the house, and he went on tip-toe to the window, through which he peered.
Les Miserables	the night was not very dark; there was a full moon, across which heavy clouds were chased by the wind.
Les Miserables	this produced alternations of light and shade, and a species of twilight in the room; this twilight, sufficient to guide him, but intermittent in consequence of the clouds, resembled that livid hue produced by the grating of a cellar over which people are continually passing.
Les Miserables	on reaching the window, jean valjean examined it; it was without bars, looked on the garden, and was only closed, according to the fashion of the country, by a small peg.
Les Miserables	he opened it, but as a cold sharp breeze suddenly entered the room, he closed it again directly.
Les Miserables	he gazed into the garden with that attentive glance which studies rather than looks, and found that it was enclosed by a white-washed wall, easy to climb over.
Les Miserables	beyond it he noticed the tops of trees standing at regular distances, which proved that this wall separated the garden from a public walk.
Les Miserables	after taking this glance, he walked boldly to the alcove, opened his knapsack, took out something which he laid on the bed, put his shoes in one of the pouches, placed the knapsack on his shoulders, put on his cap, the peak of which he pulled over his eyes, groped for his stick, which he placed in the window nook, and then returned to the bed, and took up the object he had laid on it.
Les Miserables	it resembled a short iron bar, sharpened at one of its ends.
Les Miserables	it would have been difficult to distinguish in the darkness for what purpose this piece of iron had been fashioned; perhaps it was a lever, perhaps it was a club.
Les Miserables	by daylight it could have been seen that it was nothing but a miners candlestick.
Les Miserables	the convicts at that day were sometimes employed in extracting rock from the lofty hills that surround toulon, and it was not infrequent for them to have mining tools at their disposal.
Les Miserables	the miner's candlesticks are made of massive steel, and have a point at the lower end, by which they are dug into the rock.
Les Miserables	he took the bar in his right hand, and holding his breath and deadening his footsteps he walked towards the door of the adjoining room, the bishop's as we know.
Les Miserables	on reaching this door he found it ajar--the bishop had not shut it.
Les Miserables	chapter xi.
Les Miserables	what he did.
Les Miserables	jean valjean listened, but there was not a sound; he pushed the door with the tip of his finger lightly, and with the furtive restless gentleness of a cat that wants to get in.
Les Miserables	the door yielded to the pressure, and made an almost imperceptible and silent movement, which slightly widened the opening.
Les Miserables	he waited for a moment, and then pushed the door again more boldly.
Les Miserables	it continued to yield silently, and the opening was soon large enough for him to pass through.
Les Miserables	but there was near the door a small table which formed an awkward angle with it, and barred the entrance.
Les Miserables	jean valjean noticed the difficulty: the opening must be increased at all hazards.
Les Miserables	he made up his mind, and pushed the door a third time, more energetically still.
Les Miserables	this time there was a badly-oiled hinge, which suddenly uttered a hoarse prolonged cry in the darkness.
Les Miserables	jean valjean started; the sound of the hinge smote his ear startlingly and formidably, as if it had been the trumpet of the day of judgment.
Les Miserables	in the fantastic exaggerations of the first minute, he almost imagined that this hinge had become animated, and suddenly obtained a terrible vitality and barked like a dog to warn and awaken the sleepers.
Les Miserables	he stopped, shuddering and dismayed, and fell back from tip-toes on his heels.
Les Miserables	he felt the arteries in his temples beat like two forge hammers, and it seemed to him that his breath issued from his lungs with the noise of the wind roaring out of a cavern.
Les Miserables	he fancied that the horrible clamor of this irritated hinge must have startled the whole house like the shock of an earthquake; the door he opened had been alarmed and cried for help; the old man would rise, the two aged females would shriek, and assistance would arrive within a quarter of an hour, the town would be astir, and the gendarmerie turned out.
Les Miserables	for a moment he believed himself lost.
Les Miserables	he remained where he was, petrified like the pillar of salt, and not daring to make a movement.
Les Miserables	a few minutes passed, during which the door remained wide open.
Les Miserables	he ventured to look into the room, and found that nothing had stirred.
Les Miserables	he listened; no one was moving in the house, the creaking of the rusty hinge had not awakened any one.
Les Miserables	the first danger had passed, but still there was fearful tumult within him.
Les Miserables	but he did not recoil, he had not done so even when he thought himself lost; he only thought of finishing the job as speedily as possible, and entered the bed-room.
Les Miserables	the room was in a state of perfect calmness; here and there might be distinguished confused and vague forms, which by day were papers scattered over the table, open folios, books piled on a sofa, an easy-chair covered with clothes, and a priedieu, all of which were at this moment only dark nooks and patches of white.
Les Miserables	jean valjean advanced cautiously and carefully, and avoided coming into collision with the furniture.
Les Miserables	he heard from the end of the room the calm and regular breathing of the sleeping bishop.
Les Miserables	suddenly he stopped, for he was close to the bed; he had reached it sooner than he anticipated.
Les Miserables	nature at times blends her effects and scenes with our actions, with a species of gloomy and intelligent design, as if wishing to make us reflect.
Les Miserables	for nearly half an hour a heavy cloud had covered the sky, but at the moment when jean valjean stopped at the foot of the bed, this cloud was rent asunder as if expressly, and a moonbeam passing through the tall window suddenly illumined the bishop's pale face.
Les Miserables	he was sleeping peacefully, and was wrapped up in a long garment of brown wool, which covered his arms down to the wrists.
Les Miserables	his head was thrown back on the pillow in the easy attitude of repose, and his hand, adorned with the pastoral ring, and which had done so many good deeds, hung out of bed.
Les Miserables	his entire face was lit up by a vague expression of satisfaction, hope, and beatitude--it was more than a smile and almost a radiance.
Les Miserables	he had on his forehead the inexpressible reflection of an invisible light, for the soul of a just man contemplates a mysterious heaven during sleep.
Les Miserables	a reflection of this heaven was cast over the bishop, but it was at the same time a luminous transparency, for the heaven was within him, and was conscience.
Les Miserables	at the moment when the moonbeam was cast over this internal light, the sleeping bishop seemed to be surrounded by a glory, which was veiled, however, by an ineffable semi-light.
Les Miserables	the moon in the heavens, the slumbering landscape, the quiet house, the hour, the silence, the moment, added something solemn and indescribable to this man's venerable repose, and cast a majestic and serene halo round his white hair and closed eyes, his face in which all was hope and confidence, his aged head, and his infantine slumbers.
Les Miserables	there was almost a divinity in this unconsciously august man.
Les Miserables	jean valjean was standing in the shadow with his crow-bar in his hand, motionless and terrified by this luminous old man.
Les Miserables	he had never seen anything like this before, and such confidence horrified him.
Les Miserables	the moral world has no greater spectacle than this,--a troubled, restless conscience, which is on the point of committing a bad action, contemplating the sleep of a just man.
Les Miserables	this sleep in such isolation, and with a neighbor like himself, possessed a species of sublimity which he felt vaguely, but imperiously.
Les Miserables	no one could have said what was going on within him, not even himself.
Les Miserables	in order to form any idea of it we must imagine what is the most violent in the presence of what is gentlest.
Les Miserables	even in his face nothing could have been distinguished with certainty, for it displayed a sort of haggard astonishment.
Les Miserables	he looked at the bishop, that was all, but what his thoughts were it would be impossible to divine; what was evident was, that he was moved and shaken, but of what nature was this emotion?
Les Miserables	his eye was not once removed from the old man, and the only thing clearly revealed by his attitude and countenance was a strange indecision.
Les Miserables	it seemed as if he were hesitating between two abysses, the one that saves and the one that destroys; he was ready to dash out the bishop's brains or kiss his hand.
Les Miserables	at the expiration of a few minutes his left arm slowly rose to his cap, which he took off; then his arm fell again with the same slowness, and jean valjean recommenced his contemplation, with his cap in his left hand, his crow-bar in his right, and his hair standing erect on his savage head.
Les Miserables	the bishop continued to sleep peacefully beneath this terrific glance.
Les Miserables	a moonbeam rendered the crucifix over the mantel-piece dimly visible, which seemed to open its arms for both, with a blessing for one and a pardon for the other.
Les Miserables	all at once jean valjean put on his cap again, then walked rapidly along the bed, without looking at the bishop, and went straight to the cupboard.
Les Miserables	he raised his crow-bar to force the lock, but as the key was in it, he opened it, and the first thing he saw was the plate-basket, which he seized.
Les Miserables	he hurried across the room, not caring for the noise he made, re-entered the oratory, opened the window, seized his stick, put the silver in his pocket, threw away the basket, leaped into the garden, bounded over the wall like a tiger, and fled.
Les Miserables	chapter xii.
Les Miserables	the bishop at work.
Les Miserables	the next morning at sunrise monseigneur welcome was walking about the garden, when madame magloire came running toward him in a state of great alarm.
Les Miserables	"monseigneur, monseigneur!"
Les Miserables	she screamed, "does your grandeur know where the plate-basket is?"
Les Miserables	"yes," said the bishop.
Les Miserables	"the lord be praised," she continued; "i did not know what had become of it."
Les Miserables	the bishop had just picked up the basket in a flower-bed, and now handed it to madame magloire.
Les Miserables	"here it is," he said.
Les Miserables	"well!"
Les Miserables	she said, "there is nothing in it; where is the plate?"
Les Miserables	"ah!"
Les Miserables	the bishop replied, "it is the plate that troubles your mind.
Les Miserables	well, i do not know where that is."
Les Miserables	"good lord!
Les Miserables	it is stolen, and that man who came last night is the robber."
Les Miserables	in a twinkling madame magloire had run to the oratory, entered the alcove, and returned to the bishop.
Les Miserables	he was stooping down and looking sorrowfully at a cochlearia, whose stem the basket had broken.
Les Miserables	he raised himself on hearing madame magloire scream,--  "monseigneur, the man has gone!
Les Miserables	the plate is stolen!"
Les Miserables	while uttering this exclamation her eyes fell on a corner of the garden, where there were signs of climbing; the coping of the wall had been torn away.
Les Miserables	"that is the way he went!
Les Miserables	he leaped into cochefilet lane.
Les Miserables	oh, what an outrage!
Les Miserables	he has stolen our plate."
Les Miserables	the bishop remained silent for a moment, then raised his earnest eyes, and said gently to madame magloire,--  "by the way, was that plate ours?"
Les Miserables	madame magloire was speechless; there was another interval of silence, after which the bishop continued,--  "madame magloire, i had wrongfully held back this silver, which belonged to the poor.
Les Miserables	who was this person?
Les Miserables	evidently a poor man."
Les Miserables	"good gracious!"
Les Miserables	madame magloire continued; "i do not care for it, nor does mademoiselle, but we feel for monseigneur.
Les Miserables	with what will monseigneur eat now?"
Les Miserables	the bishop looked at her in amazement.
Les Miserables	"why, are there not pewter forks to be had?"
Les Miserables	madame magloire shrugged her shoulders.
Les Miserables	"pewter smells!"
Les Miserables	"then iron!"
Les Miserables	madame magloire made an expressive grimace.
Les Miserables	"iron tastes."
Les Miserables	"well, then," said the bishop, "wood!"
Les Miserables	a few minutes later he was breakfasting at the same table at which jean valjean sat on the previous evening.
Les Miserables	while breakfasting monseigneur welcome gayly remarked to his sister, who said nothing, and to madame magloire, who growled in a low voice, that spoon and fork, even of wood, are not required to dip a piece of bread in a cup of milk.
Les Miserables	"what an idea!"
Les Miserables	madame magloire said, as she went backwards and forwards, "to receive a man like that, and lodge him by one's side.
Les Miserables	and what a blessing it is that he only stole!
Les Miserables	oh, lord!
Les Miserables	the mere thought makes a body shudder."
Les Miserables	as the brother and sister were leaving the table there was a knock at the door.
Les Miserables	"come in," said the bishop.
Les Miserables	the door opened, and a strange and violent group appeared on the threshold.
Les Miserables	three men were holding a fourth by the collar.
Les Miserables	the three men were gendarmes, the fourth was jean valjean.
Les Miserables	a corporal, who apparently commanded the party, came in and walked up to the bishop with a military salute.
Les Miserables	"monseigneur," he said.
Les Miserables	at this word jean valjean, who was gloomy and crushed, raised his head with a stupefied air.
Les Miserables	"'monseigneur,'" he muttered; "then he is not the curé."
Les Miserables	"silence!"
Les Miserables	said a gendarme.
Les Miserables	"this gentleman is monseigneur the bishop."
Les Miserables	in the mean while monseigneur welcome had advanced as rapidly as his great age permitted.
Les Miserables	"ah!
Les Miserables	there you are," he said, looking at jean valjean.
Les Miserables	"i am glad to see you.
Les Miserables	why, i gave you the candlesticks too, which are also silver, and will fetch you 200 francs.
Les Miserables	why did you not take them away with the rest of the plate?"
Les Miserables	jean valjean opened his eyes, and looked at the bishop with an expression which no human language could render.
Les Miserables	"monseigneur," the corporal said; "what this man told us was true then?
Les Miserables	we met him, and as he looked as if he were running away, we arrested him.
Les Miserables	he had this plate--"  "and he told you," the bishop interrupted, with a smile, "that it was given to him by an old priest at whose house he passed the night?
Les Miserables	i see it all.
Les Miserables	and you brought him back here?
Les Miserables	that is a mistake."
Les Miserables	"in that case," the corporal continued, "we can let him go?"
Les Miserables	"of course," the bishop answered.
Les Miserables	the gendarmes loosed their hold of jean valjean, who tottered back.
Les Miserables	"is it true that i am at liberty?"
Les Miserables	he said, in an almost inarticulate voice, and as if speaking in his sleep.
Les Miserables	"yes, you are let go; don't you understand?"
Les Miserables	said a gendarme.
Les Miserables	"my friend," the bishop continued, "before you go take your candlesticks."
Les Miserables	he went to the mantel-piece, fetched the two candlesticks, and handed them to jean valjean.
Les Miserables	the two females watched him do so without a word, without a sign, without a look that could disturb the bishop.
Les Miserables	jean valjean was trembling in all his limbs; he took the candlesticks mechanically, and with wandering looks.
Les Miserables	"now," said the bishop, "go in peace.
Les Miserables	by the bye, when you return, my friend, it is unnecessary to pass through the garden, for you can always enter, day and night, by the front door, which is only latched."
Les Miserables	then, turning to the gendarmes, he said,--  "gentlemen, you can retire."
Les Miserables	they did so.
Les Miserables	jean valjean looked as if he were on the point of fainting; the bishop walked up to him, and said in a low voice,--  "never forget that you have promised me to employ this money in becoming an honest man."
Les Miserables	jean valjean, who had no recollection of having promised anything, stood silent.
Les Miserables	the bishop, who had laid a stress on these words, continued solemnly,--  "jean valjean, my brother, you no longer belong to evil, but to good.
Les Miserables	i have bought your soul of you.
Les Miserables	i withdraw it from black thoughts and the spirit of perdition, and give it to god."
Les Miserables	chapter xiii.
Les Miserables	little gervais.
Les Miserables	jean valjean left the town as if running away; he walked hastily across the fields, taking the roads and paths that offered themselves, without perceiving that he was going round and round.
Les Miserables	he wandered thus the entire morning, and though he had eaten nothing, he did not feel hungry.
Les Miserables	he was attacked by a multitude of novel sensations; he felt a sort of passion, but he did not know with whom.
Les Miserables	he could not have said whether he was affected or humiliated; at times a strange softening came over him, against which he strove, and to which he opposed the hardening of the last twenty years.
Les Miserables	this condition offended him, and he saw with alarm that the species of frightful calmness, which the injustice of his misfortune had produced, was shaken within him.
Les Miserables	he asked himself what would take its place; at times he would have preferred being in prison and with the gendarmes, and that things had not happened thus; for that would have agitated him less.
Les Miserables	although the season was advanced, there were still here and there in the hedges a few laggard flowers, whose smell recalled childhood's memories as he passed them.
Les Miserables	these recollections were almost unendurable, for it was so long since they had recurred to him.
Les Miserables	indescribable thoughts were thus congregated within him the whole day through.
Les Miserables	when the sun was setting, and lengthening on the ground the shadow of the smallest pebble, jean valjean was sitting behind a bush in a large tawny and utterly-deserted plain.
Les Miserables	there were only the alps on the horizon, there was not even the steeple of a distant village.
Les Miserables	jean valjean might be about three leagues from d----, and a path that crossed the plain ran a few paces from the bushes.
Les Miserables	in the midst of this meditation, which would have contributed no little in rendering his rags startling to any one who saw him, he heard a sound of mirth.
Les Miserables	he turned his head and saw a little savoyard about ten years of age coming along the path, with his hurdy-gurdy at his side and his dormouse-box on his back.
Les Miserables	he was one of those gentle, merry lads who go about from place to place, displaying their knees through the holes in their trousers.
Les Miserables	while singing the lad stopped every now and then to play at pitch and toss with some coins he held in his hand, which were probably his entire fortune.
Les Miserables	among these coins was a two-franc piece.
Les Miserables	the lad stopped by the side of the bushes without seeing jean valjean, and threw up the handful of sous, all of which he had hitherto always caught on the back of his hand.
Les Miserables	this time the two-franc piece fell, and rolled up to jean valjean, who placed his foot upon it.
Les Miserables	but the boy had looked after the coin, and seen him do it; he did not seem surprised, but walked straight up to the man.
Les Miserables	it was an utterly deserted spot; as far as eye could extend there was no one on the plain or the path.
Les Miserables	nothing was audible, save the faint cries of a swarm of birds of passage passing through the sky, at an immense height.
Les Miserables	the boy had his back turned to the sun, which wove golden threads in his hair, and suffused jean valjean's face with a purpled, blood-red hue.
Les Miserables	"sir," the little savoyard said, with that childish confidence which is composed of ignorance and innocence, "my coin?"
Les Miserables	"what is your name?"
Les Miserables	jean valjean said.
Les Miserables	"little gervais, sir."
Les Miserables	"be off," said jean valjean.
Les Miserables	"give me my coin, if you please, sir."
Les Miserables	jean valjean hung his head, but said nothing.
Les Miserables	the boy began again,--  "my two-franc piece, sir."
Les Miserables	jean valjean's eye remained fixed on the ground.
Les Miserables	"my coin," the boy cried, "my silver piece, my money."
Les Miserables	it seemed as if jean valjean did not hear him, for the boy seized the collar of his blouse and shook him, and at the same time made an effort to remove the iron-shod shoe placed on his coin.
Les Miserables	"i want my money, my forty-sous piece."
Les Miserables	the boy began crying, and jean valjean raised his head.
Les Miserables	he was still sitting on the ground, and his eyes were misty.
Les Miserables	he looked at the lad with a sort of amazement, then stretched forth his hand to his stick, and shouted in a terrible voice, "who is there?"
Les Miserables	"i, sir," the boy replied.
Les Miserables	"little gervais; give me back my two francs, if you please.
Les Miserables	take away your foot, sir, if you please."
Les Miserables	then he grew irritated, though so little, and almost threatening.
Les Miserables	"come, will you lift your foot?
Les Miserables	lift it, i say!"
Les Miserables	"ah, it is you still," said jean valjean, and springing up, with his foot still held on the coin, he added, "will you be off or not?"
Les Miserables	the startled boy looked at him, then began trembling from head to foot, and after a few moments of stupor ran off at full speed, without daring to look back or utter a cry.
Les Miserables	still, when he had got a certain distance, want of breath forced him to stop, and jean valjean could hear him sobbing.
Les Miserables	in a few minutes the boy had disappeared.
Les Miserables	the sun had set, and darkness collected around jean valjean.
Les Miserables	he had eaten nothing all day, and was probably in a fever.
Les Miserables	he had remained standing and not changed his attitude since the boy ran off.
Les Miserables	his breath heaved his chest at long and unequal intervals, his eye, fixed ten or twelve yards ahead, seemed to be studying with profound attention the shape of an old fragment of blue earthenware which had fallen in the grass.
Les Miserables	suddenly he started, for he felt the night chill; he pulled his cap over his forehead, mechanically tried to cross and button his blouse, made a step, and stooped to pick up his stick.
Les Miserables	at this moment he perceived the two-franc piece, which his foot had half buried in the turf, and which glistened among the pebbles.
Les Miserables	it had the effect of a galvanic shock upon him.
Les Miserables	"what is this?"
Les Miserables	he muttered.
Les Miserables	he fell back three paces, then stopped, unable to take his eye from the spot his foot had trodden a moment before, as if the thing glistening there in the darkness had an open eye fixed upon him.
Les Miserables	in a few moments he dashed convulsively at the coin, picked it up, and began looking out into the plain, while shuddering like a straying wild beast which is seeking shelter.
Les Miserables	he saw nothing, night was falling, the plain was cold and indistinct, and heavy purple mists rose in the twilight.
Les Miserables	he set out rapidly in a certain direction, the one in which the lad had gone.
Les Miserables	after going some thirty yards he stopped, looked and saw nothing; then he shouted with all his strength, "little gervais, little gervais!"
Les Miserables	he was silent, and waited, but there was no response.
Les Miserables	the country was deserted and gloomy, and he was surrounded by space.
Les Miserables	there was nothing but a gloom in which his gaze was lost, and a stillness in which his voice was lost.
Les Miserables	an icy breeze was blowing, and imparted to things around a sort of mournful life.
Les Miserables	the bushes shook their little thin arms with incredible fury; they seemed to be threatening and pursuing some one.
Les Miserables	he walked onwards and then began running, but from time to time he stopped, and shouted in the solitude with a voice the most formidable and agonizing that can be imagined: "little gervais, little gervais!"
Les Miserables	assuredly, if the boy had heard him, he would have felt frightened, and not have shown himself; but the lad was doubtless a long way off by this time.
Les Miserables	the convict met a priest on horseback, to whom he went up and said,--  "monsieur le curé, have you seen a lad pass?"
Les Miserables	"no," the priest replied.
Les Miserables	"a lad of the name of 'little gervais?'"
Les Miserables	"i have seen nobody."
Les Miserables	the convict took two five-franc pieces from his pouch and handed them to the priest.
Les Miserables	"monsieur le curé, this is for your poor.
Les Miserables	he was a boy of about ten years of age, with a dormouse, i think, and a hurdy-gurdy,--a savoyard, you know."
Les Miserables	"i did not see him."
Les Miserables	"can you tell me if there is any one of the name of little gervais in the villages about here?"
Les Miserables	"if it is as you say, my good fellow, the lad is a stranger.
Les Miserables	many of them pass this way."
Les Miserables	jean valjean violently took out two other five-franc pieces, which he gave the priest.
Les Miserables	"for your poor," he said; then added wildly, "monsieur l'abbé, have me arrested: i am a robber."
Les Miserables	the priest urged on his horse, and rode away in great alarm, while jean valjean set off running in the direction he had first taken.
Les Miserables	he went on for a long distance, looking, calling, and shouting, but he met no one else.
Les Miserables	twice or thrice he ran across the plain to something that appeared to him to be a person lying or sitting down; but he only found heather, or rocks level with the ground.
Les Miserables	at last he stopped at a spot where three paths met; the moon had risen; he gazed afar, and called out for the last time, "little gervais, little gervais, little gervais!"
Les Miserables	his shout died away in the mist, without even awakening an echo.
Les Miserables	he muttered again, "little gervais," in a weak and almost inarticulate voice, but it was his last effort.
Les Miserables	his knees suddenly gave way under him as if an invisible power were crushing him beneath the weight of a bad conscience.
Les Miserables	he fell exhausted on a large stone, with his hand tearing his hair, his face between his knees, and shrieked: "i am a scoundrel!"
Les Miserables	then his heart melted, and he began to weep; it was the first time for nineteen years.
Les Miserables	when jean valjean quitted the bishop's house he was lifted out of his former thoughts, and could not account for what was going on within him.
Les Miserables	he stiffened himself against the angelic deeds and gentle words of the old man: "you have promised me to become an honest man.
Les Miserables	i purchase your soul; i withdraw it from the spirit of perverseness and give it to god."
Les Miserables	this incessantly recurred to him, and he opposed to this celestial indulgence that pride which is within us as the fortress of evil.
Les Miserables	he felt indistinctly that this priest's forgiveness was the greatest and most formidable assault by which he had yet been shaken; that his hardening would be permanent if he resisted this clemency; that if he yielded he must renounce that hatred with which the actions of other men had filled his soul during so many years, and which pleased him; that this time he must either conquer or be vanquished, and that the struggle, a colossal and final struggle, had begun between his wickedness and that man's goodness.
Les Miserables	in the presence of all these gleams he walked on like a drunken man.
Les Miserables	while he went on thus with haggard eye, had he any distinct perception of what the result of his adventure at d---- might be?
Les Miserables	did he hear all that mysterious buzzing which warns or disturbs the mind at certain moments of life?
Les Miserables	did a voice whisper in his ear that he had just gone through the solemn hour of his destiny, that no middle way was now left him, and that if he were not henceforth the best of men he would be the worst; that he must now ascend higher than the bishop, or sink lower than the galley-slave; that if he wished to be good he must become an angel, and if he wished to remain wicked that he must become a monster?
Les Miserables	here we must ask again the question we previously asked, did he confusedly receive any shadow of all this into his mind?
Les Miserables	assuredly, as we said, misfortune educates the intellect, still it is doubtful whether jean valjean was in a state to draw the conclusions we have formed.
Les Miserables	if these ideas reached him, he had a glimpse of them rather than saw them, and they only succeeded in throwing him into an indescribable and almost painful trouble.
Les Miserables	on leaving that shapeless black thing which is called the bagne the bishop had hurt his soul, in the same way as a too brilliant light would have hurt his eyes on coming out of darkness.
Les Miserables	the future life, the possible life, which presented itself to him, all pure and radiant, filled him with tremor and anxiety, and he really no longer knew how matters were.
Les Miserables	like an owl that suddenly witnessed a sunrise the convict had been dazzled and, as it were, blinded by virtue.
Les Miserables	one thing which he did not suspect is certain, however, that he was no longer the same man; all was changed in him, and it was no longer in his power to get rid of the fact that the bishop had spoken to him and taken his hand.
Les Miserables	while in this mental condition he met little gervais, and robbed him of his two francs: why did he so?
Les Miserables	assuredly he could not explain it.
Les Miserables	was it a final, and as it were supreme, effort of the evil thought he had brought from the bagne, a remainder of impulse, a result of what is called in statics "acquired force"?
Les Miserables	it was so, and was perhaps also even less than that.
Les Miserables	let us say it simply, it was not he who robbed, it was not the man, but the brute beast that through habit and instinct stupidly placed its foot on the coin, while the intellect was struggling with such novel and extraordinary sensations.
Les Miserables	when the intellect woke again and saw this brutish action, jean valjean recoiled with agony and uttered a cry of horror.
Les Miserables	it was a curious phenomenon, and one only possible in his situation, that, in robbing the boy of that money, he committed a deed of which he was no longer capable.
Les Miserables	however this may be, this last bad action had a decisive effect upon him: it suddenly darted through the chaos which filled his mind and dissipated it, placed on one side the dark mists, on the other the light, and acted on his soul, in its present condition, as certain chemical re-agents act upon a troubled mixture, by precipitating one element and clarifying another.
Les Miserables	at first, before even examining himself or reflecting, he wildly strove to find the boy again and return him his money; then, when he perceived that this was useless and impossible, he stopped in despair.
Les Miserables	at the moment when he exclaimed, "i am a scoundrel!"
Les Miserables	he had seen himself as he really was, and was already so separated from himself that he fancied himself merely a phantom, and that he had there before him, in flesh and blood, his blouse fastened round his hips, his knapsack full of stolen objects on his back, with his resolute and gloomy face and his mind full of hideous schemes, the frightful galley-slave, jean valjean.
Les Miserables	as we have remarked, excessive misfortune had made him to some extent a visionary, and this therefore was a species of vision.
Les Miserables	he really saw that jean valjean with his sinister face before him, and almost asked himself who this man who so horrified him was.
Les Miserables	his brain was in that violent and yet frightfully calm stage when the reverie is so deep that it absorbs reality.
Les Miserables	he contemplated himself, so to speak, face to face, and at the same time he saw through this hallucination a species of light which he at first took for a torch.
Les Miserables	on looking more attentively at this light which appeared to his conscience, he perceived that it had a human shape and was the bishop.
Les Miserables	his conscience examined in turn the two men standing before him, the bishop and jean valjean.
Les Miserables	by one of those singular effects peculiar to an ecstasy of this nature, the more his reverie was prolonged, the taller and more brilliant the bishop appeared, while jean valjean grew less and faded out of sight.
Les Miserables	at length he disappeared and the bishop alone remained, who filled the wretched man's soul with a magnificent radiance.
Les Miserables	jean valjean wept for a long time, and sobbed with more weakness than a woman, more terror than a child.
Les Miserables	while he wept the light grew brighter in his brain,--an extraordinary light, at once ravishing and terrible.
Les Miserables	his past life, his first fault, his long expiation, his external brutalization, his internal hardening, his liberation, accompanied by so many plans of vengeance, what had happened at the bishop's, the last thing he had done, the robbery of the boy, a crime the more cowardly and monstrous because it took place after the bishop's forgiveness, --all this recurred to him, but in a light which he had never before seen.
Les Miserables	he looked at his life, and it appeared to him horrible; at his soul, and it appeared to him frightful.
Les Miserables	still a soft light was shed over both, and he fancied that he saw satan by the light of paradise.
Les Miserables	how many hours did he weep thus?
Les Miserables	what did he do afterwards?

Les Miserables	these parisians came, one from toulouse, the second from limoges, the third from cahors, the fourth from montauban, but they were students, and thus parisians; for studying in paris is being born in paris.
Les Miserables	these young men were insignificant, four every-day specimens, neither good nor bad, wise nor ignorant, geniuses nor idiots, and handsome with that charming aprilia which is called twenty years.
Les Miserables	they were four oscars, for at that period arthurs did not yet exist.
Les Miserables	"burn for him the perfumes of arabia," the romance said; "oscar is advancing, i am about to see him."
Les Miserables	people had just emerged from ossian: the elegant world was scandinavian and caledonian, the english style was not destined to prevail till a later date, and the first of the arthurs, wellington, had only just won the battle of waterloo.
Les Miserables	the names of these oscars were félix tholomyès, of toulouse; listolier, of cahors; fameuil, of limoges; and blachevelle, of montauban.
Les Miserables	of course each had a mistress; blachevelle loved favourite, so called because she had been to england; listolier adored dahlia, who had taken the name of a flower for her _nom de guerre_; fameuil idolized zéphine, an abridgment of josephine; while tholomyès had fantine, called the blonde, owing to her magnificent suncolored hair.
Les Miserables	favourite, dahlia, zéphine, and fantine were four exquisitely pretty girls, still to some extent workwomen.
Les Miserables	they had not entirely laid down the needle, and though unsettled by their amourettes, they still had in their faces a remnant of the serenity of toil, and in their souls that flower of honesty, which in a woman survives the first fall.
Les Miserables	one of the four was called the young one, because she was the youngest, and one called the old one, who was only three-and-twenty.
Les Miserables	to conceal nothing, the three first were more experienced, more reckless, and had flown further into the noise of life than fantine the blonde, who was still occupied with her first illusion.
Les Miserables	dahlia, zéphine, and especially favourite, could not have said the same.

Les Miserables	poverty and coquettishness are two fatal counsellors: one scolds, the other flatters, and the poor girls of the lower classes have them whispering in both ears.
Les Miserables	badly-guarded souls listen, and hence come the falls they make, and the stones hurled at them.

Les Miserables	she met her father from time to time, and he bowed to her.
Les Miserables	one morning, an old woman with a hypocritical look came into her room and said, "do you not know me, miss?"

Les Miserables	a sage and a philosopher are two persons; and what proves it is that, after making all reservations for these little irregular households, favourite, zéphine, and dahlia were philosophic girls, and fantine a prudent girl.
Les Miserables	prudent, it will be said, and tholomyès?
Les Miserables	solomon would reply, that love forms part of wisdom.

Les Miserables	she was the only one of the four who was addressed familiarly by one man alone.
Les Miserables	fantine was one of those beings who spring up from the dregs of the people; issuing from the lowest depths of the social darkness, she had on her forehead the stamp of the anonymous and the unknown.
Les Miserables	she was born at m. sur m.; of what parents, who could say?
Les Miserables	she had never known either father or mother.
Les Miserables	she called herself fantine, and why fantine?
Les Miserables	she was never known by any other name.
Les Miserables	at the period of her birth, the directory was still in existence.
Les Miserables	she had no family name, as she had no family; and no christian name, as the church was abolished.
