Les Miserables	she was called little fantine, and no one knew any more.
Les Miserables	this human creature came into the world in that way.
Les Miserables	at the age of ten, fantine left the town, and went into service with farmers in the neighborhood.
Les Miserables	at the age of fifteen she went to paris, "to seek her fortune."
Les Miserables	fantine was pretty and remained pure as long as she could.
Les Miserables	she was a charming blonde, with handsome teeth; she had gold and pearls for her dower, but the gold was on her head, and the pearls in her mouth.
Les Miserables	she worked for a livelihood; and then she loved, still for the sake of living, for the heart is hungry too.
Les Miserables	she loved tholomyès; it was a pastime for him, but a passion with her.
Les Miserables	the streets of the quartier latin, which are thronged with students and grisettes, saw the beginning of this dream.
Les Miserables	fantine, in the labyrinth of the pantheon hill, where so many adventures are fastened and unfastened, long shunned tholomyès, but in such a way as to meet him constantly.
Les Miserables	there is a manner of avoiding which resembles seeking,--in a word, the eclogue was played.
Les Miserables	blachevelle, listolier, and fameuil formed a sort of group, of which tholomyès was the head, for it was he who had the wit.
Les Miserables	tholomyès was the antique old student; he was rich, for he had an income of 4000 francs a year, a splendid scandal on the montagne st. geneviève.
Les Miserables	tholomyès was a man of the world, thirty years of age, and in a bad state of preservation.
Les Miserables	he was wrinkled and had lost teeth, and he had an incipient baldness, of which he himself said without sorrow: "the skull at thirty, the knee at forty."
Les Miserables	he had but a poor digestion, and one of his eyes was permanently watery.
Les Miserables	but in proportion as his youth was extinguished, his gayety became brighter; he substituted jests for his teeth, joy for his hair, irony for his health, and his weeping eye laughed incessantly.
Les Miserables	he was battered, but still flowering.
Les Miserables	his youth had beaten an orderly retreat, and only the fire was visible.
Les Miserables	he had had a piece refused at the vaudeville theatre, and wrote occasional verses now and then.
Les Miserables	in addition, he doubted everything in a superior way, which is a great strength in the eyes of the weak.
Les Miserables	hence, being ironical and bald, he was the leader.
Les Miserables	we wonder whether irony, is derived from the english word "iron"?
Les Miserables	one day tholomyès took the other three aside, made an oracular gesture, and said,--  "it is nearly a year that fantine, dahlia, zéphine, and favorite have been asking us to give them a surprise, and we promised solemnly to do so.

Les Miserables	our beauties incessantly say to me, "tholomyès, when will you be delivered of your surprise?"
Les Miserables	at the same time our parents are writing to us, so let us kill two birds with one stone.
Les Miserables	the moment appears to me to have arrived, so let us talk it over."

Les Miserables	an _estaminet_ full of smoke presenting itself, they went in, and the remainder of their conference was lost in the tobacco clouds.
Les Miserables	the result of the gloom was a brilliant pleasure excursion, that took place on the following sunday, to which the four young men invited the girls.
Les Miserables	chapter iii.
Les Miserables	four to four.
Les Miserables	it is difficult to form an idea at the present day of what a pleasure party of students and grisettes was four-and-forty years ago.
Les Miserables	paris has no longer the same environs; the face of what may be termed circum-parisian life has completely changed during half a century; where there was the old-fashioned coach, there is a railway-carriage; where there was the fly-boat, there is now the steamer; people talk of fécamp as people did in those days of st. cloud.
Les Miserables	paris of 1862 is a city which has france for its suburbs.
Les Miserables	the four couples conscientiously accomplished all the rustic follies possible at that day.
Les Miserables	it was a bright warm summer day; they rose at five o'clock; then they went to st. cloud in the stage-coach, looked at the dry cascade, and exclaimed, "that must be grand when there is water;" breakfasted at the tête noire, where castaing had not yet put up, ran at the ring in the quincunx of the great basin, ascended into the diogenes lantern, gambled for macaroons at the roulette board by the sêvres bridge, culled posies at puteaux, bought reed-pipes at neuilly, ate apple tarts everywhere, and were perfectly happy.
Les Miserables	the girls prattled and chattered like escaped linnets; they were quite wild, and every now and then gave the young men little taps.
Les Miserables	oh, youthful intoxication of life!
Les Miserables	adorable years!
Les Miserables	the wing of the dragon-fly rustles.
Les Miserables	oh, whoever you may be, do you remember?
Les Miserables	have you ever walked in the woods, removing the branches for the sake of the pretty head that comes behind you?
Les Miserables	have you laughingly stepped on a damp slope, with a beloved woman who holds your hand, and cries, "oh, my boots, what a state they are in!"
Les Miserables	let us say at once, that the merry annoyance of a shower was spared the happy party, although favourite had said on starting, with a magisterial and maternal air, "the slugs are walking about the paths; that is a sign of rain, children."
Les Miserables	all four were pretty madcaps.
Les Miserables	a good old classic poet, then renowned, m. le chevalier de labouisse, a worthy man who had an eléanore, wandering that day under the chestnut-trees of st. cloud, saw them pass at about ten in the morning, and exclaimed, "there is one too many," thinking of the graces.
Les Miserables	favourite, the girl who was three-and-twenty and the old one, ran in front under the large green branches, leaped over ditches, strode madly across bushes, and presided over the gayety with the spirit of a young fawn.
Les Miserables	zéphine and dahlia, whom accident had created as a couple necessary to enhance each other's beauty by contrast, did not separate, though more through a coquettish instinct than through friendship, and leaning on one another, assumed english attitudes; the first "keepsakes" had just come out, melancholy was culminating for women, as byronism did at a later date for men, and the hair of the tender sex was beginning to become dishevelled.
Les Miserables	zéphine and dahlia had their hair in rolls.
Les Miserables	listolier and fameuil, who were engaged in a discussion about their professors, were explaining to fantine the difference there was between m. delvincourt and m. blondeau.
Les Miserables	blachevelle seemed to have been created expressly to carry favourite's faded shabby shawl on sundays.
Les Miserables	tholomyès came last; he was very gay, but there was something commanding in his joviality; his principal ornament was nankeen trousers, cut in the shape of elephant's legs, with leathern straps; he had a mighty rattan worth 200 francs in his hand, and, as he was quite reckless, a strange thing called a cigar in his mouth; nothing being sacred to him, he smoked.
Les Miserables	"that tholomyès is astounding," the others were wont to say with veneration.
Les Miserables	"what trousers!
Les Miserables	what energy!"
Les Miserables	as for fantine she was the personification of joy.
Les Miserables	her splendid teeth had evidently been made for laughter by nature.
Les Miserables	she carried in her hand, more willingly than on her head, her little straw bonnet, with its long streamers.
Les Miserables	her thick, light hair, inclined to float, and which had to be done up continually, seemed made for the flight of galatea under the willows.
Les Miserables	her rosy lips prattled enchantingly; the corners of her mouth voluptuously raised, as in the antique masks of erigone, seemed to encourage boldness; but her long eyelashes, full of shade, were discreetly lowered upon the seductiveness of the lower part of the face, as if to command respect.
Les Miserables	her whole toilet had something of song and sunshine about it; she had on a dress of mauve barége, little buskin slippers, whose strings formed an x on her fine, open-worked stockings, and that sort of muslin spencer, a marseillais invention, whose name of canezou, a corrupted pronunciation of _quinze août_ at the cannebière, signifies fine weather and heat.
Les Miserables	the three others, who were less timid, as we said, bravely wore low-necked dresses, which in summer are very graceful and attractive, under bonnets covered with flowers; but by the side of this bold dress, fantine's canezou, with its transparency, indiscretion, and reticences, at once concealing and displaying, seemed a provocative invention of decency; and the famous court of love, presided over by the vicomtesse de cette with the sea-green eyes, would have probably bestowed the prize for coquettishness on this canezou, which competed for that of chastity.
Les Miserables	the simplest things are frequently the cleverest.
Les Miserables	dazzling from a front view, delicate from a side view, with dark blue eyes, heavy eye-lids, arched and small feet, wrists and ankles admirably set on, the white skin displaying here and there the azure arborescences of the veins, with a childish fresh cheek, the robust neck of the æginetan juno shoulders, apparently modelled by couston, and having in their centre a voluptuous dimple, visible through the muslin; a gayety tempered by reverie; a sculptural and exquisite being,--such was fantine; you could trace beneath the ribbons and finery a statue, and inside the statue a soul.
Les Miserables	fantine was beautiful, without being exactly conscious of it.
Les Miserables	those rare dreamers, the mysterious priests of the beautiful, who silently confront everything with perfection, would have seen in this little work-girl the ancient sacred euphony, through the transparency of parisian grace!
Les Miserables	this girl had blood in her, and had those two descriptions of beauty which are the style and the rhythm.
Les Miserables	the style is the form of the ideal; the rhythm is its movement.
Les Miserables	we have said that fantine was joy itself; she was also modesty.
Les Miserables	any one who watched her closely would have seen through all this intoxication of youth, the season, and love, an invincible expression of restraint and modesty.
Les Miserables	she remained slightly astonished, and this chaste astonishment distinguishes psyche from venus.
Les Miserables	fantine had the long white delicate fingers of the vestal, who stirs up the sacred fire with a golden bodkin.
Les Miserables	though she had refused nothing, as we shall soon see, to tholomyès, her face, when in repose, was supremely virginal; a species of stern and almost austere dignity suddenly invaded it at certain hours, and nothing was so singular and affecting as to see gayety so rapidly extinguished on it, and contemplation succeed cheerfulness without any transition.
Les Miserables	this sudden gravity, which was at times sternly marked, resembled the disdain of a goddess.
Les Miserables	her forehead, nose, and chin offered that equilibrium of outline which is very distinct from the equilibrium of proportion, and produces the harmony of the face; in the characteristic space between the base of the nose and the upper lip, she had that imperceptible and charming curve, that mysterious sign of chastity, which made barbarossa fall in love with a diana found in the ruins of iconium.
Les Miserables	love is a fault; be it so; but fantine was innocence floating on the surface of the fault.
Les Miserables	chapter iv.
Les Miserables	tholomyès sings a spanish song.
Les Miserables	the whole of this day seemed to be composed of dawn; all nature seemed to be having a holiday, and laughing.
Les Miserables	the pastures of st. cloud exhaled perfumes; the breeze from the seine vaguely stirred the leaves; the branches gesticulated in the wind; the bees were plundering the jessamine; a madcap swarm of butterflies settled down on the ragwort, the clover, and the wild oats; there was in the august park of the king of france a pack of vagabonds, the birds.
Les Miserables	the four happy couples enjoyed the sun, the fields, the flowers, and the trees.
Les Miserables	and in this community of paradise, the girls, singing, talking, dancing, chasing butterflies, picking bind-weed, wetting their stockings in the tall grass, fresh, madcap, not bad, all received kisses from all the men, every now and then, save fantine, enveloped in her vague resistance, dreamy and shy, and who was in love.
Les Miserables	"you always look strange," favourite said to her.
Les Miserables	such passings-by of happy couples are a profound appeal to life and nature, and bring caresses and light out of everything.
Les Miserables	once upon a time there was a fairy, who made fields and trees expressly for lovers; hence the eternal hedge-school of lovers, which incessantly recommences, and will last so long as there are bushes and scholars.
Les Miserables	hence the popularity of spring among thinkers; the patrician and the knifegrinder, the duke and the limb of the law, people of the court and people of the city, as they were called formerly, are all subjects of this fairy.
Les Miserables	people laugh and seek each other; there is the brilliancy of an apotheosis in the air, for what a transfiguration is loving!
Les Miserables	notary's clerks are gods.
Les Miserables	and then the little shrieks, pursuits in the grass, waists caught hold of, that chattering which is so melodious, that adoration which breaks out in the way of uttering a word, cherries torn from lips,--all this is glorious!
Les Miserables	people believe that it will never end; philosophers, poets, artists, regard these ecstasies, and know not what to do, as they are so dazzled by them.
Les Miserables	the departure for cythera!
Les Miserables	exclaims watteau; lancret, the painter of the middle classes, regards his cits flying away in the blue sky; diderot stretches out his arms to all these amourettes, and d'urfé mixes up druids with them.
Les Miserables	after breakfast the four couples went to see, in what was then called the king's square, a plant newly arrived from the indies, whose name we have forgotten, but which at that time attracted all paris to st. cloud; it was a strange and pretty shrub, whose numerous branches, fine as threads and leafless, were covered with a million of small white flowers giving it the appearance of a head of hair swarming with flowers; there was always a crowd round it, admiring it.
Les Miserables	after inspecting the shrub, tholomyès exclaimed, "i will pay for donkeys;" and after making a bargain with the donkey-man, they returned by vauvres and issy.
Les Miserables	at the latter place an incident occurred; the park, a national estate held at this time by bourguin the contractor, was accidentally open.
Les Miserables	they passed through the gates, visited the wax hermit in his grotto, and tried the mysterious effect of the famous cabinet of mirrors, a lascivious trap, worthy of a satyr who had become a millionnaire.
Les Miserables	they bravely pulled the large swing, fastened to the two chestnut-trees celebrated by the abbé de bernis.
Les Miserables	while swinging the ladies in turn, which produced, amid general laughter, a flying of skirts by which greuze would have profited, the toulousian tholomyès, who was somewhat of a spaniard, as toulouse is the cousin of tolosa, sang to a melancholy tune the old gallega, which was probably inspired by the sight of a pretty girl swinging between two trees,--  "soy tie badajoz amor me llama toda mi alma es en mis ojos porque enseflas a tus piernas."
Les Miserables	fantine alone declined to swing.
Les Miserables	"i do not like people to be so affected," favourite muttered rather sharply.
Les Miserables	on giving up the donkeys there was fresh pleasure; the seine was crossed in a boat, and from passy they walked to the barrière de l'étoile.
Les Miserables	they had been afoot since five in the morning; but no matter!
Les Miserables	"there is no such thing as weariness on sunday," said favourite; "on sundays fatigue does not work."
Les Miserables	at about three o'clock, the four couples, wild with delight, turned into the montagnes busses, a singular building, which at that time occupied the heights of beaujon, and whose winding line could be seen over the trees of the champs élysées.
Les Miserables	from time to time favourite exclaimed,--  "where's the surprise?
Les Miserables	i insist on the surprise."
Les Miserables	"have patience," tholomyès answered.
Les Miserables	chapter v.   at bombarda's.
Les Miserables	the russian mountain exhausted, they thought about dinner, and the radiant eight, at length somewhat weary, put into the cabaret bombarda, an offshoot established in the champs élysées by that famous restaurateur bombarda, whose sign could be seen at that time at the rue de rivoli by the side of the delorme passage.
Les Miserables	a large but ugly room, with an alcove and a bed at the end (owing to the crowded state of the houses on sundays they were compelled to put up with it); two windows from which the quay and river could be contemplated through the elm-trees; a magnificent autumn sun illumining the windows; two tables, on one of them a triumphal mountain of bottles, mixed up with hats and bonnets, at the other four couples joyously seated round a mass of dishes, plates, bottles, and glasses, pitchers of beer, mingled with wine-bottles; but little order on the table, and some amount of disorder under it.
Les Miserables	"ils faisaient sous la table un bruit, un trique-trac de pieds épouvantable,"  as molière says.
Les Miserables	such was the state of the pastoral which began at 5 a.m.; at half-past 4 p.m. the sun was declining and appetite was satisfied.
Les Miserables	the champs élysées, full of sunshine and crowd, were nought but light and dust, two things of which glory is composed.
Les Miserables	the horses of marly, those neighing marbles, reared amid a golden cloud.
Les Miserables	carriages continually passed along; a squadron of splendid guards, with the trumpeter at their head, rode down the neuilly avenue; the white flag, tinged with pink by the setting sun, floated above the dome of the tuileries.
Les Miserables	the place de la concorde, which had again become the place louis xv., was crowded with merry promenaders.
Les Miserables	many wore a silver _fleur de lys_ hanging from a black moiré ribbon, which, in 1817, had not entirely disappeared from the buttonholes.
Les Miserables	here and there, in the midst of applauding crowds, little girls were singing a royalist _bourrée,_ very celebrated at that time, intended to crush the hundred days, and which had a chorus of,--  "rendez nous notre père de gand, rendez vous notre père."
Les Miserables	heaps of suburbans, dressed in their sunday clothes, and some wearing _fleur de lys_ like the cits, were scattered over the squares, playing at quintain or riding in roundabouts; others were drinking; some who were printers' apprentices wore paper caps, and their laughter was the loudest.
Les Miserables	all was radiant; it was a time of undeniable peace, and of profound royalist security; it was a period when a private and special report of anglès, prefect of police to the king, terminated with these lines: "all things duly considered, sire, there is nothing to fear from these people.
Les Miserables	they are as careless and indolent as cats, and though the lower classes in the provinces are stirring, those in paris are not so.
Les Miserables	they are all little men, sire, and it would take two of them to make one of your grenadiers.
Les Miserables	there is nothing to fear from the populace of the capital.
Les Miserables	it is remarkable that their height has decreased during the last fifty years, and the people of the suburbs of paris are shorter than they were before the revolution.
Les Miserables	they are not dangerous, and, in a word, are good-tempered _canaille._"  prefects of police do not believe it possible that a cat can be changed into a lion; it is so, however, and that is the miracle of the people of paris.
Les Miserables	the cat, so despised by count anglès, possessed the esteem of the old republics; it was the incarnation of liberty in their eyes, and as if to serve as a pendant to the minerva apteros of the piræus, there was on the public square of corinth a colossal bronze statue of a cat.
Les Miserables	the simple police of the restoration had too favorable an opinion of the people of paris, and they were not such good-tempered _canaille_ as they were supposed to be.
Les Miserables	the parisian is to the french-man what the athenian is to the greek; no one sleeps sounder than he; no one is more frankly frivolous and idle than he; no one can pretend to forget so well as he,--but he must not be trusted; he is suited for every species of nonchalance, but when there is a glory as the result, he is admirable for every sort of fury.
Les Miserables	give him a pike and he will make august 10; give him a musket, and you will have austerlitz.
Les Miserables	he is the support of napoleon, and the resource of danton.
Les Miserables	if the country is in danger, he enlists; if liberty is imperilled, he tears up the pavement.
Les Miserables	his hair, full of wrath, is epical, his blouse assumes the folds of a chlamys.
Les Miserables	take care; for of the first rue grenétat he comes to be will make caudine forks.
Les Miserables	if the hour strikes, this suburban grows, the little man looks in a terrible manner, his breath becomes a tempest, and from his weak chest issues a blast strong enough to uproot the alps.
Les Miserables	it was through the parisian suburban that the revolution, joined with armies, conquered europe.
Les Miserables	he sings, and that forms his delight; proportion his song to his nature, and you shall see!
Les Miserables	so long as he has no burden but the carmagnole, he will merely overthrow louis xvi.
Les Miserables	; but make him sing the marseillaise, and he will deliver the world.
Les Miserables	after writing this note on the margin of count anglès' report, we will return to our four couples.
Les Miserables	the dinner, as we said, was drawing to a close.
Les Miserables	chapter vi.
Les Miserables	in which people adore each other.
Les Miserables	love talk and table talk are equally indescribable, for the first is a cloud, the second smoke.
Les Miserables	fantine and dahlia were humming a tune, tholomyès was drinking, zéphine laughing, fantine smiling, listolier was blowing a penny trumpet bought at st. cloud, favourite was looking tenderly at blachevelle and saying,--  "blachevelle, i adore you."

Les Miserables	it is easy to see that he wants to be an actor, and i am fond of actors.
Les Miserables	so soon as he comes in, his mother says,--'oh, good heavens!
Les Miserables	my tranquillity is destroyed: he is going to begin to shout; my dear boy, you give me a headache;' because he goes about the house, into the garrets as high as he can get, and sings and declaims, so that he can be heard from the streets!
Les Miserables	he already earns 20 sous a day in a lawyer's office.
Les Miserables	he is the son of an ex-chorister at st. jacques du haut pas.
Les Miserables	ah!
Les Miserables	he adores me to such a pitch that one day when he saw me making batter for pancakes, he said to me, 'mamselle, make fritters of your gloves, and i will eat them.'
Les Miserables	only artists are able to say things like that.
Les Miserables	ah!
Les Miserables	he is very good-looking, and i feel as if i am about to fall madly in love with the little fellow.

Les Miserables	it has done nothing but rain all the summer: the wind annoys me, blachevelle is excessively mean, there are hardly any green peas in the market, one does not know what to eat; i have the spleen, as the english say, for butter is so dear; and then it is horrifying that we are dining in a room with a bed in it, and that disgusts me with life."
Les Miserables	chapter vii.
Les Miserables	the wisdom of tholomyès.
Les Miserables	at length, when all were singing noisily, or talking all together, tholomyès interfered.
Les Miserables	"let us not talk hap-hazard or too quickly," he exclaimed; "we must meditate if we desire to be striking; too much improvisation stupidly empties the mind.
Les Miserables	gentlemen, no haste; let us mingle majesty with our gayety, eat contemplatively, and let _festina lente_ be our rule.
Les Miserables	we must not hurry.
Les Miserables	look at the spring; if it goes ahead too fast it is floored, that is to say, nipped by frost.

Les Miserables	no zeal, gentlemen; grimaud de la reynière is of the same opinion as talleyrand."

Les Miserables	this poor pun produced the effect of a stone thrown into a pond.
Les Miserables	the marquis de montcalm was a celebrated royalist at that day.
Les Miserables	all the frogs were silent.
Les Miserables	"my friends," tholomyès shouted with the accent of a man who is recapturing his empire, "recover yourselves: too great stupor should not greet this pun which has fallen from the clouds, for everything that falls in such a manner is not necessarily worthy of enthusiasm and respect.
Les Miserables	far be from me to insult puns: i honor them according to their deserts, and no more.
Les Miserables	all the most august, sublime, and charming in humanity and perhaps beyond humanity have played upon words.
Les Miserables	christ made a pun on saint peter, moses on isaac, æschylus on polynices, and cleopatra on octavius.
Les Miserables	and note the fact that cleopatra's pun preceded the battle of actium, and that, were it not for that pun, no one would know the town of toryne, a greek word signifying a potladle.

Les Miserables	brethren, i repeat, no zeal, no row, no excess, even in witticisms, gayeties, merriments, and playing upon words.
Les Miserables	listen to me, for i possess the prudence of amphiaralis and the baldness of cæsar; there should be a limit even to the rebus.

Les Miserables	there should be a limit even to dinners; you are fond of apple-puffs, ladies, but no abuse; even in the matter of apple-puffs, good sense and art are needed.
Les Miserables	gluttony chastises the glutton.
Les Miserables	_gula punit gulax_.
Les Miserables	indigestion was sent into the world to read a lecture to our stomachs; and, bear this in mind, each of our passions, even love, has a stomach which must not be filled too full.
Les Miserables	in all things, we must write betimes the word _finis_, we must restrain ourselves when it becomes urgent, put a bolt on our appetites, lock up our fancy, and place ourselves under arrest.
Les Miserables	the wise man is he who knows how, at a given moment, to arrest himself.
Les Miserables	place some confidence in me: it does not follow because i know a little law, as my examinations prove; because i have supported a thesis in latin as to the mode in which torture was applied at rome at the time when munatius demens was _quæstor parricidæ_; and because i am going to be a doctor at law, as it seems,--it does not necessarily follow, i say, that i am an ass.
Les Miserables	i recommend to you moderation in your desires.
Les Miserables	as truly as my name is félix tholomyès, i am speaking the truth.
Les Miserables	happy the man who, when the hour has struck, forms an heroic resolve, and abdicates like sylla or origen."
Les Miserables	favourite was listening with profound attention.
Les Miserables	"félix!"
Les Miserables	she said, "what a pretty name; i like it.
Les Miserables	it is latin, and means happy."
Les Miserables	tholomyès continued,--  "gentlemen, be suspicious of women; woe to the man who surrenders himself to a woman's fickle heart; woman is perfidious and tortuous, and detests the serpent from professional jealousy.
Les Miserables	it is the shop opposite."

Les Miserables	"i hope so!"
Les Miserables	"then be jolly."

Les Miserables	"glory to wine!

Les Miserables	pardon, ladies, that is spanish, and the proof, señoras, is this: as the country is, so is the measure.
Les Miserables	the arroba of castille contains sixteen quarts, the cantaro of alicante twelve, the almuda of the canary isles twenty-five, the cuartino of the balearic isles twenty-six, and czar peter's boot thirty.

Les Miserables	i say nothing of fantine, for she is a dreamer, pensive and sensitive; she is a phantom, having the form of a nymph, and the modesty of a nun, who has strayed into the life of a grisette, but takes shelter in illusions, and who sings, prays, and looks at the blue sky, without exactly knowing what she sees or what she does, and who, with her eyes fixed on heaven, wanders about a garden in which there are more birds than ever existed.
Les Miserables	o fantine, be aware of this fact: i, tholomyès, am an illusion--why, the fair girl of chimeræ is not even listening to me!
Les Miserables	all about her is freshness, suavity, youth, and sweet morning brightness.

Les Miserables	girls are incurable about wedlock; and all that we sages may say will not prevent waistcoat-makers and shoebinders from dreaming of husbands loaded with diamonds.

Les Miserables	you have only one fault, o women, and that is nibbling sugar.
Les Miserables	o rodent sex, your pretty little white teeth adore sugar.
Les Miserables	now, listen to this: sugar is a salt, and salts are of a drying nature, and sugar is the most drying of all salts.
Les Miserables	it pumps out the fluidity of the blood through the veins; this produces first coagulation and then solidifying of the blood; from this come tubercles in the lungs, and thence death.
Les Miserables	hence do not nibble sugar, and you will live.
Les Miserables	i now turn to my male hearers: gentlemen, make conquests.
Les Miserables	rob one another of your well-beloved ones remorselessly; change partners, for, in love there are no friends.
Les Miserables	whenever there is a pretty woman, hostilities are opened; there is no quarter, but war to the knife!
Les Miserables	a pretty woman is a _casus belli_ and a flagrant offence.
Les Miserables	all the invasions of history were produced by petticoats; for woman is the lawful prey of man.
Les Miserables	romulus carried off the sabine women, william the saxon women, and cæsar the roman women.

Les Miserables	at the same time the other three gentlemen struck up to a doleful air one of those studio-songs, as destitute of sense as the motion of a tree or the sound of the wind, which are composed extemporaneously, either in rhyme or prose, which spring up from the smoke of pipes, and fly away with it.

Les Miserables	since men can still be unreasonable, i return thanks to the immortal gods.
Les Miserables	men lie, but they laugh: they affirm, but they doubt: and something unexpected issues from the syllogism.
Les Miserables	this is grand: there are still in the world human beings who can joyously open and shut the puzzle-box of paradox.
Les Miserables	this wine, ladies, which you are drinking so calmly, is madeira, you must know, grown at coural das freiras, which is three hundred and seventeen _toises_ above the sea level.
Les Miserables	attention while drinking!
Les Miserables	three hundred and seventeen _toises_, and m. bombarda, the magnificent restaurateur, lets you have these three hundred and seventeen _toises_ for four francs, fifty centimes."
Les Miserables	tholomyès drained his glass and then continued:  "honor to bombarda!
Les Miserables	he would be equal to memphis of elephanta if he could ladle me up an almeh, and to thygelion of cheronea if he could procure me an hetæra!
Les Miserables	for, ladies, there were bombardas in greece and egypt, as apuleius teaches us.
Les Miserables	alas!
Les Miserables	ever the same thing and nothing new: nothing is left unpublished in the creation of the creator.
Les Miserables	'nothing new under the sun,' says solomon: _amor omnibus idem_, and carabine gets into the st. cloud fly-boat with carabin, just as aspasia embarked with pericles aboard the samos fleet.
Les Miserables	one last word: do you know who aspasia was, ladies?
Les Miserables	although she lived at a time when women had no soul, she was a soul: a soul of a pink and purple hue, hotter than fire, and fresher than the dawn.
Les Miserables	aspasia was a creature in whom the two extremes of woman met.
Les Miserables	she was a prostituted goddess: socrates _plus_ manon lescaut."
Les Miserables	tholomyès, when started, would hardly have been checked, had not a horse fallen in the street at this very moment.
Les Miserables	through the shock, cart and orator stopped short.
Les Miserables	it was a beauce mare, old and lean and worthy of the knacker, dragging a very heavy cart.
Les Miserables	on getting in front of bombarda's, the beast, exhausted and worn out, refused to go any further, and this incident produced a crowd.
Les Miserables	the carter, swearing and indignant, had scarce time to utter with the suitable energy the sacramental word, "rascal!"
Les Miserables	backed up by a pitiless lash, ere the poor beast fell, never to rise again.
Les Miserables	tholomyès' gay hearers turned their heads away on noticing the confusion, while he wound up his speech by the following sad strophe,--  "elle était de ce monde où coucous et carrosses, ont le même destin, et, rosse, elle a vécu ce que vivent les rosses, l'espace d'un: mâtin!"
Les Miserables	"poor horse!"
Les Miserables	fantine said with a sigh; and dahlia shouted,--  "why, here is fantine beginning to feel pity for horses: how can she be such a fool!"
Les Miserables	at this moment, favourite crossed her arms and threw her head back; she then looked boldly at tholomyès, and said,--  "well, how about the surprise?"
Les Miserables	"that is true, the hour has arrived," tholomyès answered.
Les Miserables	"gentlemen, it is time to surprise the ladies.
Les Miserables	pray wait for us a moment."
Les Miserables	"it begins with a kiss," said blacheve.
Les Miserables	"on the forehead," tholomyès added.
Les Miserables	each solemnly kissed the forehead of his mistress: then they proceeded to the door in indian file, with a finger on their lip.
Les Miserables	favourite clapped her hands as they went out.
Les Miserables	"it is amusing already," she said.
Les Miserables	"do not be long," fantine murmured, "we are waiting for you."
Les Miserables	chapter ix.
Les Miserables	the joyous end of joy.
Les Miserables	the girls, when left alone, leaned out of the windows, two by two, talking, looking out, and wondering.
Les Miserables	they watched the young men leave the bombarda cabaret arm in arm; they turned round, made laughing signs, and disappeared in that dusty sunday mob which once a week invaded the champs élysées.
Les Miserables	"do not be long," fantine cried.
Les Miserables	"what will they bring us?"
Les Miserables	said zéphine.
Les Miserables	"i am certain it will be pretty," said dahlia.
Les Miserables	"for my part," favourite added, "i hope it will be set in gold."
Les Miserables	they were soon distracted by the movement on the quay, which they could notice through the branches of the lofty trees, and which greatly amused them.
Les Miserables	it was the hour for the mail-carts and stages to start, and nearly all those bound for the south and west at that time passed through the champs élysées.
Les Miserables	most of them followed the quay and went out by the passy barrier.
Les Miserables	every moment some heavy vehicle, painted yellow and black, heavily loaded and rendered shapeless by trunks and valises, dashed through the crowd with the sparks of a forge, the dust representing the smoke.
Les Miserables	this confusion amused the girls.
Les Miserables	"what a racket!"
Les Miserables	exclaimed favourite; "one might say a pile of chairs was flying about."
Les Miserables	one of these vehicles, which could hardly be distinguished through the branches, stopped for a moment, and then started again at a gallop.
Les Miserables	this surprised fantine.
Les Miserables	"that is strange," she said; "i fancied that the diligence never stopped."
Les Miserables	favourite shrugged her shoulders.
Les Miserables	"this fantine is really amazing, and is surprised at the simplest things.
Les Miserables	let us suppose that i am a traveller and say to the guard of the stage-coach, "i will walk on and you can pick me up on the quay as you pass."
Les Miserables	the coach passes, sees me, stops and takes me in.
Les Miserables	that is done every day; you are ignorant of life, my dear."
Les Miserables	some time elapsed; all at once favourite started as if waking from sleep.
Les Miserables	"well," she said, "where is the surprise?"
Les Miserables	"oh yes," dahlia continued, "the famous surprise."
Les Miserables	"they are a long time," said fantine.
Les Miserables	just as fantine had ended this sigh, the waiter who had served the dinner came in; he held in his hand something that resembled a letter.
Les Miserables	"what is that?"
Les Miserables	favourite asked.
Les Miserables	the waiter answered,--  "it is a paper which the gentlemen left for you, ladies."
Les Miserables	"why did you not bring it to us at once?"
Les Miserables	"because the gentlemen," the waiter went on, "ordered that it should not be delivered to you for an hour."
Les Miserables	favourite snatched the paper from the waiter's hands; it was really a letter.
Les Miserables	"stay," she said; "there is no address, but the following words are written on it: this is the surprise."
Les Miserables	she quickly opened the letter and read (she could read):--  "well-beloved,--know that we have relatives: perhaps you are not perfectly cognizant what they are; it means fathers and mothers in the civil, puerile, and honest code.
Les Miserables	well, these relatives are groaning; these old people claim us as their own; these worthy men and women call us prodigal sons.
Les Miserables	they desire our return home, and offer to kill the fatted calf.
Les Miserables	we obey them, as we are virtuous; at the hour when you read this, five impetuous steeds will be conveying us back to our papas and mammas.
Les Miserables	'we decamp,' as bossuet said; "we are going, gone."
Les Miserables	we are flying away in the arms of laffitte and on the wings of gaillard.
Les Miserables	the toulouse coach is dragging us away from the abyss, and that abyss is yourselves, pretty dears.
Les Miserables	we are re-entering society, duty, and order, at a sharp trot, and at the rate of nine miles an hour.
Les Miserables	it is important for our country that we should become, like everybody else, prefects, fathers of a family, game-keepers, and councillors of state.
Les Miserables	revere us, for we are sacrificing ourselves.
Les Miserables	dry up your tears for us rapidly, and get a substitute speedily.
Les Miserables	if this letter lacerates your hearts, treat it in the same fashion.
Les Miserables	good-by.
Les Miserables	for nearly two years we rendered you happy, so do not owe us any grudge.
Les Miserables	(signed) blachevelle.
Les Miserables	fameuil.
Les Miserables	listolier.
Les Miserables	felix tholomyès.
Les Miserables	"p.s.
Les Miserables	the dinner is paid for."
Les Miserables	the four girls looked at each other, and favourite was the first to break the silence.
Les Miserables	"i don't care," she said, "it is a capital joke."
Les Miserables	"it is very funny," zéphine remarked.
Les Miserables	"it must have been blachevelle who had that idea," favourite continued; "it makes me in love with him.
Les Miserables	so soon as he has left me i am beginning to grow fond of him; the old story."
Les Miserables	"no," said dahlia, "that is an idea of tholomyès.
Les Miserables	that can be easily seen."
Les Miserables	"in that case," favourite retorted, "down with blachevelle and long live tholomyès!"
Les Miserables	and they burst into a laugh, in which fantine joined.
Les Miserables	an hour later though, when she returned to her bed-room, she wept: this was, as we have said, her first love; she had yielded to tholomyès as to a husband, and the poor girl had a child.
Les Miserables	book iv.
Les Miserables	to confide is sometimes to abandon.
Les Miserables	chapter i.   two mothers meet.
Les Miserables	there was in the first quarter of this century a sort of pot-house at montfermeil, near paris, which no longer exists.
Les Miserables	it was kept by a couple of the name of thénardier, and was situated in the rue du boulanger.
Les Miserables	over the door a board was nailed to the wall, and on this board was painted something resembling a man carrying on his back another man, who wore large gilt general's epaulettes with silver stars; red dabs represented blood, and the rest of the painting was smoke, probably representing a battle.
Les Miserables	at the bottom could be read the inscription: the sergeant of waterloo.
Les Miserables	though nothing is more common than a cart at a pot-house door, the vehicle, or rather fragment of a vehicle, which blocked up the street in front of the sergeant of waterloo, one spring evening in 1818, would have certainly attracted the attention of any painter who had passed that way.
Les Miserables	it was the forepart of one of those wains used in wood countries for dragging planks and trunks of trees; it was composed of a massive iron axle-tree, in which a heavy pole was imbedded and supported by two enormous wheels.
Les Miserables	the whole thing was sturdy, crushing, and ugly, and it might have passed for the carriage of a monster gun.
Les Miserables	the ruts had given the wheels, felloes, spokes, axle-tree, and pole a coating of mud, a hideous yellow plaster, much like that with which cathedrals are so often adorned.
Les Miserables	the wood-work was hidden by mud and the iron by rust.
Les Miserables	under the axle-tree was festooned a heavy chain suited for a convict goliath.
Les Miserables	this chain made you think, not of the wood it was intended to secure, but of the mastodons and mammoths for which it would have served as harness; it had the air of a cyclopean and superhuman bagne, and seemed removed from some monster.
Les Miserables	homer would have bound polyphemus with it, and shakespeare, caliban.
Les Miserables	why was this thing at this place in the street?
Les Miserables	first, to block it up; secondly, to finish the rusting process.
Les Miserables	there is in the old social order a multitude of institutions which may be found in the same way in the open air, and which have no other reasons for being there.
Les Miserables	the centre of the chain hung rather close to the ground, and on the curve, as on the rope of a swing, two little girls were seated on this evening, in an exquisite embrace, one about two years and a half, the other eighteen months; the younger being in the arms of the elder.
Les Miserables	an artfully-tied handkerchief prevented them from falling, for a mother had seen this frightful chain, and said, "what a famous plaything for my children!"
Les Miserables	the two children, who were prettily dressed and with some taste, were radiant; they looked like two roses among old iron; their eyes were a triumph, their healthy cheeks laughed; one had auburn hair, the other was a brunette; their innocent faces had a look of surprise; a flowering shrub a little distance off sent to passers-by a perfume which seemed to come from them; and the younger displayed her nudity with the chaste indecency of childhood.
Les Miserables	above and around their two delicate heads, moulded in happiness and bathed in light, the gigantic wheels, black with rust, almost terrible, and bristling with curves and savage angles, formed the porch of a cavern, as it were.
Les Miserables	a few yards off, and seated in the inn door, the mother, a woman of no very pleasing appearance, but touching at this moment, was swinging the children by the help of a long cord, and devouring them with her eyes, for fear of an accident, with that animal and heavenly expression peculiar to maternity.
Les Miserables	at each oscillation the hideous links produced a sharp sound, resembling a cry of anger.
Les Miserables	the little girls were delighted; the setting sun mingled with the joy, and nothing could be so charming as this caprice of accident which had made of a titanic chain a cherub's swing.
Les Miserables	while playing with her little ones, the mother sang, terribly out of tune, a romance, very celebrated at that day,--  "il le faut, disait un guerrier."
Les Miserables	her song and contemplation of her daughters prevented her hearing and seeing what took place in the street.
Les Miserables	some one, however, had approached her, as she began the first couplets of the romance, and suddenly she heard a voice saying close to her ear,--  "you have two pretty children, madame."
Les Miserables	"--a la belle et tendre imogène,"  the mother answered, continuing her song, and then turned her head.
Les Miserables	a woman was standing a few paces from her, who also had a child, which she was carrying in her arms.
Les Miserables	she also carried a heavy bag.
Les Miserables	this woman's child was one of the most divine creatures possible to behold; she was a girl between two and three years of age, and could have vied with the two other little ones in the coquettishness of her dress.
Les Miserables	she had on a hood of fine linen, ribbons at her shoulders, and valenciennes lace in her cap.
Les Miserables	her raised petticoats displayed her white, dimpled, fine thigh; it was admirably pink and healthy, and her cheeks made one long to bite them.
Les Miserables	nothing could be said of her eyes, except that they were very large, and that she had magnificent lashes, for she was asleep.
Les Miserables	she was sleeping with the absolute confidence peculiar to her age; a mother's arms are made of tenderness, and children sleep soundly in them.
Les Miserables	as for the mother, she looked grave and sorrowful, and was dressed like a work-girl who was trying to become a country-woman again.
Les Miserables	she was young; was she pretty?
Les Miserables	perhaps so; but in this dress she did not appear so.
Les Miserables	her hair, a light lock of which peeped out, seemed very thick, but was completely hidden beneath a nun's hood; ugly, tight, and fastened under her chin.
Les Miserables	laughter displays fine teeth, when a person happens to possess them; but she did not laugh.
Les Miserables	her eyes looked as if they had not been dry for a long time; she had a fatigued and rather sickly air, and she looked at the child sleeping in her arms in the manner peculiar to a mother who has suckled her babe.
Les Miserables	a large blue handkerchief, like those served out to the invalids, folded like a shawl, clumsily hid her shape.
Les Miserables	her hands were rough and covered with red spots, and her forefinger was hardened and torn by the needle.
Les Miserables	she had on a brown cloth cloak, a cotton gown, and heavy shoes.
Les Miserables	it was fantine.
Les Miserables	it was difficult to recognize her, but, after an attentive examination, she still possessed her beauty.
Les Miserables	as for her toilette,--that aerian toilette of muslin and ribbons which seemed made of gayety, folly, and music, to be full of bells, and perfumed with lilacs,--it had faded away like the dazzling hoar-frost which looks like diamonds in the sun; it melts, and leaves the branch quite black.
Les Miserables	ten months had elapsed bince the "good joke."
Les Miserables	what had taken place during these ten months?
Les Miserables	we can guess.
Les Miserables	after desertion, want.
Les Miserables	fantine at once lost sight of favourite, zéphine, and dahlia, for this tie broken on the side of the men separated the women.
Les Miserables	they would have been greatly surprised a fortnight after had they been told that they were friends, for there was no reason for it.
Les Miserables	fantine remained alone when the father of her child had gone away--alas!
Les Miserables	such ruptures are irrevocable.
Les Miserables	she found herself absolutely isolated; she had lost her habit of working, and had gained a taste for pleasure.
Les Miserables	led away by her _liaison_ with tholomyès to despise the little trade she knew, she had neglected her connection, and it was lost.
Les Miserables	she had no resource.
Les Miserables	fantine could hardly read, and could not write; she had been merely taught in childhood to sign her name, and she had sent a letter to tholomyès, then a second, then a third, through a public writer, but tholomyès did not answer one of them.
Les Miserables	one day fantine heard the gossips say, while looking at her daughter, "children like that are not regarded seriously, people shrug their shoulders at them."
Les Miserables	then she thought of tholomyès who shrugged his shoulders at her child, and did not regard the innocent creature seriously, and her heart turned away from this man.
Les Miserables	what was she to do now?
Les Miserables	she knew not where to turn.
Les Miserables	she had committed a fault, but the foundation of her nature, we must remember, was modesty and virtue.
Les Miserables	she felt vaguely that she was on the eve of falling into distress, and gliding into worse.
Les Miserables	she needed courage, and she had it.
Les Miserables	the idea occurred to her of returning to her native town m. sur m. there some one might know her, and give her work; but she must hide her fault.
Les Miserables	and she vaguely glimpsed at the possible necessity of a separation more painful still than the first; her heart was contracted, but she formed her resolution.
Les Miserables	fantine, as we shall see, possessed the stern bravery of life.
Les Miserables	she had already valiantly given up dress; she dressed in calico, and had put all her silk ribbons and laces upon her daughter, the only vanity left her, and it was a holy one.
Les Miserables	she sold all she possessed, which brought her in 200 francs; and when she had paid her little debts, she had only about 80 francs left.
Les Miserables	at the age of two-and-twenty, on a fine spring morning, she left paris, carrying her child on her back.
Les Miserables	any one who had seen them pass would have felt pity for them; the woman had nothing in the world but her child, and the child nothing but her mother in her world.
Les Miserables	fantine had suckled her child; this had strained her chest, and she was coughing a little.
Les Miserables	we shall have no further occasion to speak of m. félix tholomyès.
Les Miserables	we will merely say that twenty years later, in the reign of louis philippe, he was a stout country lawyer, influential and rich, a sensible elector, and a very strict juror, but always a man of pleasure.
Les Miserables	about mid-day, after resting herself now and then by travelling from time to time, at the rate of three or four leagues an hour, in what were then called the "little vehicles of the suburbs of paris," fantine found herself at montfermeil, in the ruelle boulanger.
Les Miserables	as she passed the sergeant of waterloo, the two little girls in their monster swing had dazzled her, and she stopped before this vision of joy.
Les Miserables	there are charms in life, and these two little girls were one for this mother.
Les Miserables	she looked at them with great emotion, for the presence of angels is an announcement of paradise.
Les Miserables	she thought she saw over this inn the mysterious here of providence.
Les Miserables	these two little creatures were evidently happy!
Les Miserables	she looked then, and admired them with such tenderness that at the moment when the mother was drawing breath between two verses of her song, she could not refrain from saying to her what we have already recorded.
Les Miserables	"you have two pretty children, madame."
Les Miserables	the most ferocious creatures are disarmed by a caress given to their little ones.
Les Miserables	the mother raised her head, thanked her, and bade her sit down on the door bench.
Les Miserables	the two women began talking.
Les Miserables	"my name is madame thénardier," the mother of the little ones said; "we keep this inn."
Les Miserables	then returning to her romance, she went on humming,--  "il le faut, je suis chevalier, et je pars pour la palestine."
Les Miserables	this madame thénardier was a red-headed, thin, angular woman, the soldier's wife in all its ugliness, and, strange to say, with a languishing air which she owed to reading romances.
Les Miserables	she was a sort of lackadaisical male-woman.
Les Miserables	old romances, working on the imaginations of landladies, produce that effect.
Les Miserables	she was still young, scarce thirty.
Les Miserables	if this woman, now sitting, had been standing up, perhaps her height and colossal proportions, fitting for a show, would have at once startled the traveller, destroyed her confidence, and prevented what we have to record.
Les Miserables	a person sitting instead of standing up--destinies hang on this.
Les Miserables	the woman told her story with some modification.
Les Miserables	she was a work-girl, her husband was dead; she could get no work in paris, and was going to seek it elsewhere, in her native town.
Les Miserables	she had left paris that very morning on foot; as she felt tired from carrying her child, she had travelled by the stage-coach to villemomble, from that place she walked to montfermeil.
Les Miserables	the little one had walked a little, but not much, for she was so young, and so she had been obliged to cany her, and the darling had gone to sleep,--and as she said this she gave her daughter a passionate kiss, which awoke her.
Les Miserables	the babe opened her eyes, large blue eyes like her mother's, and gazed at what?
Les Miserables	nothing, everything, with that serious and at times stern air of infants, which is a mystery of their luminous innocence in the presence of our twilight virtues.
Les Miserables	we might say that they feel themselves to be angels, and know us to be men.
Les Miserables	then the child began laughing, and, though its mother had to check it, slipped down to the ground with the undauntable energy of a little creature wishing to run.
Les Miserables	all at once, she noticed the other two children in their swing, stopped short, and put out her tongue as a sign of admiration.
Les Miserables	mother thénardier unfastened her children, took them out of the swing, and said,--  "play about, all three."
Les Miserables	children soon get familiar, and in a minute the little thénardiers were playing with the new-comer at making holes in the ground, which was an immense pleasure.
Les Miserables	the stranger child was very merry; the goodness of the mother is written in the gayety of the baby.
Les Miserables	she had picked up a piece of wood which she used as a spade, and was energetically digging a grave large enough for a fly.
Les Miserables	the two went on talking.
Les Miserables	"what 's the name of your bantling?"
Les Miserables	"cosette."
Les Miserables	for cosette read euphrasie, for that was the child's real name; but the mother had converted euphrasie into cosette, through that gentle, graceful instinct peculiar to mothers and the people, which changes josefa into pépita, and françoise into sellette.
Les Miserables	it is a species of derivation which deranges and disconcerts the entire science of etymologists.
Les Miserables	we know a grandmother who contrived to make out of theodore, gnon.
Les Miserables	"what is her age?"
Les Miserables	"going on to three."
Les Miserables	"just the same age as my eldest."
Les Miserables	in the mean time the children were grouped in a posture of profound anxiety and blessedness; an event had occurred.
Les Miserables	a large worm crept out of the ground, and they were frightened, and were in ecstasy; their radiant brows touched each other; and they looked like three heads in a halo.
Les Miserables	"how soon children get to know one another," mother thénardier exclaimed; "why, they might be taken for three sisters."
Les Miserables	the word was probably the spark which the other mother had been waiting for; she seized the speaker's hand, looked at her fixedly, and said,--  "will you take charge of my child for me?"
Les Miserables	the woman gave one of those starts of surprise which are neither assent nor refusal.
Les Miserables	fantine continued,--  "look you, i cannot take the child with me to my town, for when a woman has a baby, it is a hard matter for her to get a situation.
Les Miserables	people are so foolish in our part.
Les Miserables	it was heaven that made me pass in front of your inn; when i saw your little ones so pretty, so clean, so happy, it gave me a turn.
Les Miserables	i said to myself, "she is a kind mother."
Les Miserables	it is so; they will be three sisters.
Les Miserables	then i shall not be long before i come back.
Les Miserables	will you take care of my child?"
Les Miserables	"we will see, said mother thénardier.
Les Miserables	"i would pay six francs a month."
Les Miserables	here a man's voice cried from the back of the tap-room,--  "can't be done under seven, and six months paid in advance."
Les Miserables	"six times seven are forty-two," said the landlady.
Les Miserables	"i will pay it," said the mother.
Les Miserables	"and seventeen francs in addition for extra expenses," the man's voice added.
Les Miserables	"total fifty-seven francs," said madame thénardier; and through these figures she sang vaguely,--  "il le faut, disait un guerrier."
Les Miserables	"i will pay it," the mother said; "i have eighty francs, and shall have enough left to get home on foot.
Les Miserables	i shall earn money there, and so soon as i have a little i will come and fetch my darling."
Les Miserables	the man's voice continued,--  "has the little one a stock of clothing?"
Les Miserables	"it is my husband," said mother thénardier.
Les Miserables	"of course she has clothes, poor little treasure.
Les Miserables	i saw it was your husband; and a fine stock of clothes too, a wonderful stock, a dozen of everything, and silk frocks like a lady.
Les Miserables	the things are in my bag."
Les Miserables	"they must be handed over," the man's voice remarked.
Les Miserables	"of course they must," said the mother; "it would be funny if i left my child naked."
Les Miserables	the master's face appeared.
Les Miserables	"all right," he said.
Les Miserables	the bargain was concluded, the mother spent the night at the inn, paid her money and left her child, fastened up her bag, which was now light, and started the next morning with the intention of returning soon.
Les Miserables	such departures are arranged calmly, but they entail despair.
Les Miserables	a neighbor's wife saw the mother going away, and went home saying,--  "i have just seen a woman crying in the street as if her heart was broken."
Les Miserables	when cosette's mother had gone, the man said to his wife,--  "that money will meet my bill for one hundred and ten francs, which falls due to-morrow, and i was fifty francs short.
Les Miserables	it would have been protested, and i should have had a bailiff put in.
Les Miserables	you set a famous mouse-trap with your young ones."
Les Miserables	"without suspecting it," said the woman.
Les Miserables	chapter ii   a sketch of two ugly faces.
Les Miserables	the captured mouse was very small, but the cat is pleased even with a thin mouse.
Les Miserables	who were the thénardiers?
Les Miserables	we will say one word about them for the present, and complete the sketch hereafter.
Les Miserables	these beings belonged to the bastard class, composed of coarse parvenus, and of degraded people of intellect, which stands between the classes called the middle and the lower, and combines some of the faults of the second with nearly all the vices of the first, though without possessing the generous impulse of the workingman or the honest regularity of the tradesman.
Les Miserables	theirs were those dwarf natures which easily become monstrous when any gloomy fire accidentally warms them.
Les Miserables	there was in the woman the basis of a witch, in the man the stuff for a beggar.
Les Miserables	both were in the highest degree susceptible of that sort of hideous progress which is made in the direction of evil.
Les Miserables	there are crab-like souls which constantly recoil toward darkness, retrograde in life rather than advance, employ experience to augment their deformity, incessantly grow worse, and grow more and more covered with an increasing blackness.
Les Miserables	this man and this woman had souls of this sort.
Les Miserables	thénardier was peculiarly troublesome to the physiognomist: there are some men whom you need only look at to distrust them, for they are restless behind and threatening in front.
Les Miserables	there is something of the unknown in them.
Les Miserables	we can no more answer for what they have done than for what they will do.
Les Miserables	the shadow they have in their glance denounces them.
Les Miserables	merely by hearing them say a word or seeing them make a gesture, we get a glimpse of dark secrets in their past, dark mysteries in their future.
Les Miserables	this thénardier, could he be believed, had been a soldier--sergeant, he said; he had probably gone through the campaign of 1815, and had even behaved rather bravely, as it seems.
Les Miserables	we shall see presently how the matter really stood.
Les Miserables	the sign of his inn was an allusion to one of his exploits, and he had painted it himself, for he could do a little of everything--badly.
Les Miserables	it was the epoch when the old classical romance--which after being _clélie_, had now become _lodoiska_, and though still noble, was daily growing more vulgar, and had fallen from mademoiselle de scudéri to madame bournon malarme, and from madame de lafayette to madame barthélémy hadot--was inflaming the loving soul of the porters' wives in paris, and even extended its ravages into the suburbs.
Les Miserables	madame thénardier was just intelligent enough to read books of this nature, and lived on them.
Les Miserables	she thus drowned any brains she possessed, and, so long as she remained young and a little beyond, it gave her a sort of pensive attitude by the side of her husband, who was a scamp of some depth, an almost grammatical ruffian, coarse and delicate at the same time, but who, in matters of sentimentalism, read pigault lebrun, and, in "all that concerned the sex," as he said in his jargon, was a correct and unadulterated booby.
Les Miserables	his wife was some twelve or fifteen years younger than he, and when her romantically flowing locks began to grow gray, when the megæra was disengaged from the pamela, she was only a stout wicked woman, who had been pampered with foolish romances.
Les Miserables	as such absurdities cannot be read with impunity, the result was that her eldest daughter was christened éponine; as for the younger, the poor girl was all but named gulnare, and owed it to a fortunate diversion made by a romance of ducray duminil's, that she was only christened azelma.
Les Miserables	by the way, all is not ridiculous and superficial in the curious epoch to which we are alluding, and which might be called the anarchy of baptismal names.
Les Miserables	by the side of the romantic element, which we have just pointed out, there was the social symptom.
Les Miserables	it is not rare at the present day for a drover's son to be called arthur, alfred, or alphonse, and for the viscount--if there are any viscounts left--to be called thomas, pierre, or jacques.
Les Miserables	this displacement which gives the "elegant" name to the plebeian, and the rustic name to the aristocrat, is nothing else than an eddy of equality.
Les Miserables	the irresistible penetration of the new breeze is visible in this as in everything else.
Les Miserables	beneath this apparent discord there is a grand and deep thing, the french revolution.
Les Miserables	chapter iii.
Les Miserables	the lark.
Les Miserables	it is not enough to be bad in order to prosper: and the pot-house was a failure.
Les Miserables	thanks to the fifty-seven francs, thénardier had been able to avoid a protest, and honor his signature; but the next month they wanted money again, and his wife took cosette's outfit to paris and pledged it for sixty francs.
Les Miserables	so soon as this sum was spent, the thénardiers grew accustomed to see in the little girl a child they had taken in through charity, and treated her accordingly.
Les Miserables	as she had no clothes, she was dressed in the left-off chemises and petticoats of the little thénardiers, that is to say, in rags.
Les Miserables	she was fed on the leavings of everybody, a little better than the dog, and a little worse than the cat.
Les Miserables	dog and cat were her usual company at dinner: for cosette ate with them under the table off a wooden trencher like theirs.
Les Miserables	the mother, who had settled, as we shall see hereafter, at m. sur m., wrote, or, to speak more correctly, had letters written every month to inquire after her child.
Les Miserables	the thénardiers invariably replied that cosette was getting on famously.
Les Miserables	when the first six months had passed, the mother sent seven francs for the seventh month, and continued to send the money punctually month by month.
Les Miserables	the year had not ended before thénardier said, "a fine thing that!
Les Miserables	what does she expect us to do with seven francs!"
Les Miserables	and he wrote to demand twelve.
Les Miserables	the mother, whom they persuaded that her child was happy and healthy, submitted, and sent the twelve francs.
Les Miserables	some natures cannot love on one side without hating on the other.
Les Miserables	mother thénardier passionately loved her own two daughters, which made her detest the stranger.
Les Miserables	it is sad to think that a mother's love can look so ugly.
Les Miserables	though cosette occupied so little room, it seemed to her as if her children were robbed of it, and that the little one diminished the air her daughters breathed.
Les Miserables	this woman, like many women of her class, had a certain amount of caresses and another of blows and insults to expend daily.
Les Miserables	if she had not had cosette, it is certain that her daughters, though they were idolized, would have received the entire amount; but the strange child did the service of diverting the blows on herself, while the daughters received only the caresses.
Les Miserables	cosette did not make a movement that did not bring down on her head a hailstorm of violent and unmerited chastisement.
Les Miserables	the poor weak child, unnecessarily punished, scolded, cuffed, and beaten, saw by her side two little creatures like herself who lived in radiant happiness.
Les Miserables	as madame thénardier was unkind to cosette, éponine and azelma were the same; for children, at that age, are copies of their mother; the form is smaller, that is all.
Les Miserables	a year passed, then another, and people said in the village,--  "those thénardiers are worthy people.
Les Miserables	they are not well off, and yet they bring up a poor child left on their hands."
Les Miserables	cosette was supposed to be deserted by her mother; thénardier, however, having learned in some obscure way that the child was probably illegitimate, and that the mother could not confess it, insisted on fifteen francs a month, saying that the creature was growing and eating, and threatening to send her back.
Les Miserables	"she must not play the fool with me," he shouted, "or i'll let her brat fall like a bomb-shell into her hiding-place.
Les Miserables	i must have an increase."
Les Miserables	the mother paid the fifteen francs.
Les Miserables	year by year the child grew, and so did her wretchedness: so long as cosette was little, she was the scape-goat of the two other children; so soon as she began to be developed a little, that is to say, even before she was five years old, she became the servant of the house.
Les Miserables	at five years, the reader will say, that is improbable; but, alas!
Les Miserables	it is true.
Les Miserables	social suffering begins at any age.
Les Miserables	have we not recently seen the trial of a certain dumollard, an orphan, who turned bandit, and who from the age of five, as the official documents tell us, was alone in the world and "worked for a living and stole"?
Les Miserables	cosette was made to go on messages, sweep the rooms, the yard, the street, wash the dishes, and even carry heavy bundles.
Les Miserables	the thénardiers considered themselves the more justified in acting thus, because the mother, who was still at m. sur m., was beginning to pay badly, and was several months in arrear.
Les Miserables	if the mother had returned to montfermeil at the end of three years, she would not have recognized her child.
Les Miserables	cosette, so pretty and ruddy on her arrival in this house, was now thin and sickly.
Les Miserables	she had a timid look about her; "it's cunning!"
Les Miserables	said the thénardiers.
Les Miserables	injustice had made her sulky and wretchedness had made her ugly.
Les Miserables	nothing was left her but her fine eyes, which were painful to look at, because, as they were so large, it seemed as if a greater amount of sadness was visible in them.
Les Miserables	it was a heart-rending sight to see this poor child, scarce six years of age, shivering in winter under her calico rags, and sweeping the street before day-break, with an enormous broom in her small red hands and a tear in her large eyes.
Les Miserables	the country people called her "the lark;" the lower classes, who are fond of metaphors, had given the name to the poor little creature, who was no larger than a bird, trembling, frightened, and starting, who was always the first awake in the house and the village, and ever in the street or the fields by day-break.
Les Miserables	there was this difference, however,--this poor lark never sung.
Les Miserables	book v.  the descent.
Les Miserables	chapter i.   progress in black-bead making.
Les Miserables	what had become of the mother, who, according to the people of montfermeil, appeared to have deserted her child?
Les Miserables	where was she; what was she doing?
Les Miserables	after leaving her little cosette with the thénardiers, she had continued her journey and arrived at m. sur m. fantine had been away from her province for ten years, and while she had been slowly descending from misery to misery, her native town had prospered.
Les Miserables	about two years before, one of those industrial facts which are the events of small towns had taken place.
Les Miserables	the details are important, and we think it useful to develop them; we might almost say, to understand them.
Les Miserables	from time immemorial m. sur m. had as a special trade the imitation of english jet and german black beads.
Les Miserables	this trade had hitherto only vegetated, owing to the dearness of the material, which reacted on the artisan.
Les Miserables	at the moment when fantine returned to m. sur m. an extraordinary transformation had taken place in the production of "black articles."
Les Miserables	toward the close of 1815, a man, a stranger, had settled in the town, and had the idea of substituting in this trade gum lac for rosin, and in bracelets particularly, scraps of bent plate for welded plate.
Les Miserables	this slight change was a revolution: it prodigiously reduced the cost of the material, which, in the first place, allowed the wages to be raised, a benefit for the town; secondly, improved the manufacture, an advantage for the consumer; and, thirdly, allowed the goods to be sold cheap, while tripling them the profit, an advantage for the manufacturer.
Les Miserables	in less than three years the inventor of the process had become rich, which is a good thing, and had made all rich about him, which is better.
Les Miserables	he was a stranger in the department; no one knew anything about his origin, and but little about his start.
Les Miserables	it was said that he had entered the town with but very little money, a few hundred francs at the most; but with this small capital, placed at the service of an ingenious idea, and fertilized by regularity and thought, he made his own fortune and that of the town.
Les Miserables	on his arrival at m. sur m. he had the dress, manners, and language of a workingman.
Les Miserables	it appears that on the very december night when he obscurely entered m. sur m. with his knapsack on his back, and a knotted stick in his hand, a great fire broke out in the town hall.
Les Miserables	this man rushed into the midst of the flames, and at the risk of his life saved two children who happened to belong to the captain of gendarmes; hence no one dreamed of asking for his passport.
Les Miserables	on this occasion his name was learned; he called himself father madeleine.
Les Miserables	chapter ii.
Les Miserables	madeleine.
Les Miserables	he was a man of about fifty, with a preoccupied air, and he was good-hearted.
Les Miserables	that was all that could be said of him.
Les Miserables	thanks to the rapid progress of this trade which he had so admirably remodelled, m. sur m. had become a place of considerable trade.
Les Miserables	spain, which consumes an immense amount of jet, gave large orders for it annually, and in this trade m. sur m. almost rivalled london and berlin.
Les Miserables	father madeleine's profits were so great, that after the second year he was able to build a large factory, in which were two spacious workshops, one for men, the other for women.
Les Miserables	any one who was hungry need only to come, and was sure to find there employment and bread.
Les Miserables	father madeleine expected from the men good-will, from the women purity, and from all probity.
Les Miserables	he had divided the workshops in order to separate the sexes, and enable the women and girls to remain virtuous.
Les Miserables	on this point he was inflexible, and it was the only one in which he was at all intolerant.
Les Miserables	this sternness was the more justifiable because m. sur m. was a garrison town, and opportunities for corruption abounded.
Les Miserables	altogether his arrival had been a benefit, and his presence was a providence.
Les Miserables	before father madeleine came everything was languishing, and now all led the healthy life of work.
Les Miserables	a powerful circulation warmed and penetrated everything; stagnation and wretchedness were unknown.
Les Miserables	there was not a pocket, however obscure, in which there was not a little money, nor a lodging so poor in which there was not a little joy.
Les Miserables	father madeleine employed every one.
Les Miserables	he only insisted on one thing,--be an honest man, a good girl!
Les Miserables	as we have said, in the midst of this activity, of which he was the cause and the pivot, father madeleine made his fortune, but, singularly enough in a plain man of business, this did not appear to be his chief care; he seemed to think a great deal of others and but little of himself.
Les Miserables	in 1820, he was known to have a sum of 630,000 francs in lafitte's bank; but before he put that amount on one side he had spent more than a million for the town and the poor.
Les Miserables	the hospital was badly endowed, and he added ten beds.
Les Miserables	m. sur m. is divided into an upper and a lower town; the latter, in which he lived, had only one school, a poor tenement falling in ruins, and he built two, one for boys and one for girls.
Les Miserables	he paid the two teachers double the amount of their poor official salary, and to some one who expressed surprise, he said, "the first two functionaries of the state are the nurse and the schoolmaster."
Les Miserables	he had established at his own charges an infant-school, a thing at that time almost unknown in france, and a charitable fund for old and infirm workmen.
Les Miserables	as his factory was a centre, a new district, in which there was a large number of indigent families, rapidly sprang up around it, and he opened there a free dispensary.
Les Miserables	at the beginning, kind souls said, "he is a man who wants to grow rich:" when it was seen that he enriched the town before enriching himself, the same charitable souls said, "he is ambitious."
Les Miserables	this seemed the more likely because he was religious, and even practised to a certain extent a course which was admired in those days.
Les Miserables	he went regularly to hear low mass on sundays, and the local deputy, who scented rivalry everywhere, soon became alarmed about this religion.
Les Miserables	this deputy, who had been a member of the legislative council of the empire, shared the religious ideas of a father of the oratory, known by the name of fouché, duc d'otranto, whose creature and friend he had been.
Les Miserables	but when he saw the rich manufacturer madeleine go to seven o'clock low mass, he scented a possible candidate, and resolved to go beyond him; he chose a jesuit confessor, and went to high mass and vespers.
Les Miserables	ambition at that time was, in the true sense of the term, a steeple-chase.
Les Miserables	the poor profited by the alarm, for the honorable deputy founded two beds at the hospital, which made twelve.
Les Miserables	in 1819, the report spread one morning through the town that, on the recommendation of the prefect, and in consideration of services rendered the town, father madeleine was about to be nominated by the king, mayor of m----.
Les Miserables	those who had declared the new-comer an ambitious man, eagerly seized this opportunity to exclaim: "did we not say so?"
Les Miserables	all m---- was in an uproar; for the rumor was well founded.
Les Miserables	a few days after, the appointment appeared in the _moniteur_, and the next day father madeleine declined the honor.
Les Miserables	in the same year, the new processes worked by him were shown at the industrial exhibition; and on the report of the jury, the king made the inventor a chevalier of the legion of honor.
Les Miserables	there was a fresh commotion in the little town; "well, it was the cross he wanted," but father madeleine declined the cross.
Les Miserables	decidedly the man was an enigma, but charitable souls got out of the difficulty by saying, "after all, he is a sort of adventurer."
Les Miserables	as we have seen, the country owed him much, and the poor owed him everything; he was so useful that he could not help being honored, and so gentle that people could not help loving him; his work-people especially adored him, and he bore this adoration with a sort of melancholy gravity.
Les Miserables	when he was known to be rich, "people in society" bowed to him, and he was called in the town monsieur madeleine; but his workmen and the children continued to call him father madeleine, and this caused him his happiest smile.
Les Miserables	in proportion as he ascended, invitations showered upon him; and society claimed him as its own.
Les Miserables	the little formal drawing-rooms, which had of course been at first closed to the artisan, opened their doors wide to the millionnaire.
Les Miserables	a thousand advances were made to him, but he refused them.
Les Miserables	this time again charitable souls were not thrown out: "he is an ignorant man of poor education.
Les Miserables	no one knows where he comes from.
Les Miserables	he could not pass muster in society, and it is doubtful whether he can read."
