Les Miserables	when he was seen to be earning money, they said, "he is a tradesman;" when he scattered his money, they said, "he is ambitious;" when he rejected honor, they said, "he is an adventurer;" and when he repulsed society, they said, "he is a brute."
Les Miserables	in 1820, five years after his arrival at m., the services he had rendered the town were so brilliant, the will of the whole country was so unanimous, that the king again nominated him mayor of the town.
Les Miserables	he refused again, but the prefect would not accept his refusal; all the notables came to beg, the people supplicated him in the open streets, and the pressure was so great, that he eventually assented.
Les Miserables	it was noticed that what appeared specially to determine him was the almost angry remark of an old woman, who cried to him from her door: "a good mayor is useful; a man should not recoil before the good he may be able to do."
Les Miserables	this was the third phase of his ascent; father madeleine had become monsieur madeleine, and monsieur madeleine became monsieur le maire.
Les Miserables	chapter iii.
Les Miserables	sums lodged at lafitte's.
Les Miserables	father madeleine remained as simple as he had been on the first day: he had gray hair, a serious eye, the bronzed face of a workingman, and the thoughtful face of a philosopher.
Les Miserables	he habitually wore a broad-brimmed hat, and a long coat of coarse cloth buttoned up to the chin.
Les Miserables	he performed his duties as mayor, but beyond that lived solitary; he spoke to few persons, shunned compliments, smiled to save himself from talking, and gave to save himself from smiling.
Les Miserables	the women said of him, "what a good bear!"
Les Miserables	and his great pleasure was to walk about the fields.
Les Miserables	he always took his meals with an open book before him, and he had a well-selected library.
Les Miserables	he was fond of books, for they are calm and sure friends.
Les Miserables	in proportion as leisure came with fortune, he seemed to employ it in cultivating his mind: it was noticed that with each year he spent in m---- his language became more polite, chosen, and gentle.
Les Miserables	he was fond of taking a gun with him on his walks, but rarely fired; when he did so by accident, he had an infallible aim, which was almost terrific.
Les Miserables	he never killed an inoffensive animal or a small bird.
Les Miserables	though he was no longer young, he was said to possess prodigious strength: he lent a hand to any one who needed it, raised a fallen horse, put his shoulder to a wheel stuck in the mud, or stopped a runaway bull by the horns.
Les Miserables	his pockets were always full of half-pence when he went out, and empty when he came home; whenever he passed through a village, the ragged children ran merrily after him, and surrounded him like a swarm of gnats.
Les Miserables	it was supposed that he must have formerly lived a rustic life, for he had all sorts of useful secrets which he taught the peasants.
Les Miserables	he showed them how to destroy blight in wheat by sprinkling the granary and pouring into the cracks of the boards a solution of common salt, and to get rid of weevils by hanging up everywhere, on the walls and roots, flowering orviot.
Les Miserables	he had recipes to extirpate from arable land tares and other parasitic plants which injure wheat, and would defend a rabbit hutch from rats by the mere smell of a little guinea pig, which he placed in it.
Les Miserables	one day he saw some countrymen very busy in tearing up nettles; he looked at the pile of uprooted and already withered plants and said: "they are dead, and yet they are good if you know how to use them.
Les Miserables	when nettles are young, the tops are an excellent vegetable.
Les Miserables	when they are old, they have threads and fibre like hemp and flax.
Les Miserables	when chopped up, nettles are good for fowls; when pounded, excellent for horned cattle.
Les Miserables	nettle-seed mixed with the food renders the coats of cattle shining, and the root mixed with salt produces a fine yellow color.
Les Miserables	the nettle is also excellent hay, which can be mown twice; and what does it require?
Les Miserables	a little earth, no care, and no cultivation.
Les Miserables	the only thing is that the seed falls as it ripens, and is difficult to garner.
Les Miserables	if a little care were taken, the nettle would be useful; but, being neglected, it becomes injurious, and is then killed.
Les Miserables	how men resemble nettles!"
Les Miserables	he added after a moment's silence: "my friends, remember this,--there are no bad herbs or bad men; there are only bad cultivators."
Les Miserables	the children also loved him, because he could make them pretty little toys of straw and cocoa-nut shells.
Les Miserables	when he saw a church door hung with black, he went in; he went after a funeral as other persons do after a christening.
Les Miserables	the misfortunes of others attracted him, owing to his great gentleness; he mingled with friends in mourning, and with the priests round a coffin.
Les Miserables	he seemed to be fond of hearing those mournful psalms which are full of the vision of another world.
Les Miserables	with his eye fixed on heaven, he listened, with a species of aspiration toward all the mysteries of infinitude, to the sad voice singing on the brink of the obscure abyss of death.
Les Miserables	he did a number of good actions, while as careful to hide them as if they were bad.
Les Miserables	he would quietly at night enter houses, and furtively ascend the stairs.
Les Miserables	a poor fellow, on returning to his garret, would find that his door had been opened, at times forced, during his absence; the man would cry that a robber had been there, but when he entered, the first thing he saw was a gold coin left on the table.
Les Miserables	the robber who had been there was father madeleine.
Les Miserables	he was affable and sad: people said, "there is a rich man who does not look proud: a lucky man who does not look happy."
Les Miserables	some persons asserted that he was a mysterious character, and declared that no one ever entered his bed-room, which was a real anchorite's cell, furnished with winged hour-glasses and embellished with cross-bones and death's-heads.
Les Miserables	this was so often repeated that some elegant and spiteful ladies of m---- came to him one day, and said, "monsieur le maire, _do_ show us your bed-room, for people say that it is a grotto."
Les Miserables	he smiled and led them straightway to the "grotto;" they were terribly punished for their curiosity, as it was a bed-room merely containing mahogany furniture as ugly as all furniture of that sort, and hung with a paper at twelve sous a roll.
Les Miserables	they could not notice anything but two double-branched candlesticks of an antiquated pattern, standing on the mantel-piece, and seeming to be silver, "because they were hall-marked,"--a remark full of the wit of small towns.
Les Miserables	people did not the less continue to repeat, however, that no one ever entered this bed-room, and that it was a hermitage, a hole, a tomb.
Les Miserables	they also whispered that he had immense sums lodged with lafitte, and with this peculiarity that things were always at his immediate disposal, "so that," they added, "m. madeleine could go any morning to lafitte's, sign a receipt, and carry off his two or three millions of francs in ten minutes."
Les Miserables	in reality, these "two or three millions" were reduced, as we have said, to six hundred and thirty or forty thousand francs.
Les Miserables	chapter iv.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine goes into mourning.
Les Miserables	at the beginning of 1821, the papers announced the decease of m. myriel, bishop of d----, "surnamed monseigneur welcome," who had died in the odor of sanctity at the age of eighty-two.
Les Miserables	the bishop of d----, to add here a detail omitted by the papers, had been blind for several years, and was satisfied to be blind as his sister was by his side.
Les Miserables	let us say parenthetically that to be blind and to be loved is one of the most strangely exquisite forms of happiness upon this earth, where nothing is perfect.
Les Miserables	to have continually at your side a wife, a sister, a daughter, a charming being, who is there because you have need of her, and because she cannot do without you; to know yourself indispensable to a woman who is necessary to you; to be able constantly to gauge her affection by the amount of her presence which she gives you, and to say to yourself: "she devotes all her time to me because i possess her entire heart;" to see her thoughts in default of her face; to prove the fidelity of a being in the eclipse of the world; to catch the rustling of a dress like the sound of wings; to hear her come and go, leave the room, return, talk, sing, and then to dream that you are the centre of those steps, those words, those songs; to manifest at every moment your own attraction, and feel yourself powerful in proportion to your weakness; to become in darkness and through darkness the planet round which this angel gravitates,--but few felicities equal this.
Les Miserables	the supreme happiness of life is the conviction of being loved for yourself, or, more correctly speaking, loved in spite of yourself; and this conviction the blind man has.
Les Miserables	in this distress to be served is to be caressed.
Les Miserables	does he want for anything?
Les Miserables	no.
Les Miserables	when you possess love, you have not lost the light.
Les Miserables	and what a love!
Les Miserables	a love entirely made of virtues.
Les Miserables	there is no blindness where there is certainty: the groping soul seeks a soul and finds it, and this found and tried soul is a woman.
Les Miserables	a hand supports you, it is hers; a mouth touches your forehead, it is hers; you hear a breathing close to you, it is she.
Les Miserables	to have everything she has, from her worship to her pity, to be never left, to have this gentle weakness to succor you, to lean on this unbending reed, to touch providence with her hands, and be able to take her in your arms: oh!
Les Miserables	what heavenly rapture is this!
Les Miserables	the heart, that obscure celestial flower, begins to expand mysteriously, and you would not exchange this shadow for all the light!
Les Miserables	the angel soul is thus necessarily there; if she go away, it is to return; she disappears like a dream, and reappears like reality.
Les Miserables	you feel heat approaching you, it is she.
Les Miserables	you overflow with serenity, ecstasy, and gayety; you are a sunbeam in the night.
Les Miserables	and then the thousand little attentions, the nothings which are so enormous in this vacuum!
Les Miserables	the most ineffable accents of the human voice employed to lull you, and taking the place of the vanished universe.
Les Miserables	you are caressed with the soul: you see nothing, but you feel yourself adored; it is a paradise of darkness.
Les Miserables	it was from this paradise that monseigneur welcome had passed to the other.
Les Miserables	the announcement of his death was copied by the local paper of m----, and on the next day monsieur madeleine appeared dressed in black, with crape on his hat.
Les Miserables	the mourning was noticed in the town, and people gossiped about it, for it seemed to throw a gleam, over m. madeleine's origin.
Les Miserables	it was concluded that he was somehow connected with the bishop.
Les Miserables	"he is in mourning for the bishop," was said in drawing-rooms; this added inches to m. madeleine's stature, and suddenly gave him a certain consideration in the noble world of m----.
Les Miserables	the microscopic faubourg st. germain of the town thought about putting an end to the coventry of m. madeleine, the probable relation of a bishop, and m. madeleine remarked the promotion he had obtained in the increased love of the old ladies, and the greater amount of smiles from the young.
Les Miserables	one evening a lady belonging to this little great world, curious by right of seniority, ventured to say, "m. le maire is doubtless a cousin of the late bishop of d----?"
Les Miserables	he answered, "no, madame."
Les Miserables	"but," the dowager went on, "you wear mourning for him."
Les Miserables	"in my youth i was a footman in his family," was the answer.
Les Miserables	another thing noticed was, that when a young savoyard passed through the town, looking for chimneys to sweep, the mayor sent for him, asked his name, and gave him money.
Les Miserables	the savoyard boys told each other of this, and a great many passed through m----.
Les Miserables	chapter v.   vague flashes on the horizon.
Les Miserables	by degrees and with time all the opposition died out; at first there had been calumnies against m. madeleine,--a species of law which all rising men undergo; then it was only backbiting; then it was only malice; and eventually all this faded away.
Les Miserables	the respect felt for him was complete, unanimous, and cordial, and the moment arrived in 1821 when the name of the mayor was uttered at m---- with nearly the same accent as "monseigneur the bishop" had been said at d---- in 1815. people came for ten leagues round to consult m. madeleine; he settled disputes, prevented lawsuits, and reconciled enemies.
Les Miserables	everybody was willing to accept him as arbiter, and it seemed as if he had the book of natural law for his soul.
Les Miserables	it was a sort of contagious veneration, which in six or seven years spread all over the country-side.
Les Miserables	only one man in the town and bailiwick resisted this contagion, and whatever m. madeleine might do, remained rebellious to it, as if a sort of incorruptible and imperturbable instinct kept him on his guard.
Les Miserables	it would appear, in fact, as if there is in certain men a veritable bestial instinct, though pure and honest as all instincts are, which creates sympathies and antipathies; which fatally separates one nature from another; which never hesitates; which is not troubled, is never silent, and never contradicts itself; which is clear in its obscurity, infallible, imperious; refractory to all the counsels of intelligence and all the solvents of the reason, and which, whatever the way in which destinies are made, surely warns the man-dog of the man-cat, and the man-fox of the presence of the man-lion.
Les Miserables	it often happened when m. madeleine passed along a street, calmly, kindly, and greeted by the blessings of all, that a tall man, dressed in an iron-gray great-coat, armed with a thick cane, and wearing a hat with turned-down brim, turned suddenly and looked after him till he disappeared; folding his arms, shaking his head, and raising his upper lip with the lower as high as his nose, a sort of significant grimace, which may be translated,--"who is that man?
Les Miserables	i am certain that i have seen him somewhere.
Les Miserables	at any rate, i am not his dupe."
Les Miserables	this person, who was grave, with an almost menacing gravity, was one of those men who, though only noticed for a moment, preoccupy the observer.
Les Miserables	his name was javert, and he belonged to the police, and performed at m---- the laborious but useful duties of an inspector.
Les Miserables	he had not seen madeleine's beginning, for he was indebted for the post he occupied to the secretary of count angle, at that time prefect of police at paris.
Les Miserables	when javert arrived at m----, the great manufacturer's fortune was made, and father madeleine had become monsieur madeleine.
Les Miserables	some police officers have a peculiar face, which is complicated by an air of baseness, blended with an air of authority.
Les Miserables	javert had this face, less the baseness.
Les Miserables	in our conviction, if souls were visible, we should distinctly see the strange fact that every individual of the human species corresponds to some one of the species of animal creation; and we might occurred to the thinker, that, from the oyster to the eagle, from the hog to the tiger, all animals are in man, and that each of them is in a man; at times several of them at once.
Les Miserables	animals are nothing else than the figures of our virtues and our vices, wandering before our eyes, the visible phantoms of our souls.
Les Miserables	god shows these to us in order to make us reflect; but, as animals are only shadows, god has not made them capable of education in the complete sense of the term, for of what use would it be?
Les Miserables	on the other hand, our souls being realities and having an end of their own, god has endowed them with intelligence; that is to say, possible education.
Les Miserables	social education, properly carried out, can always draw out of a soul, no matter its nature, the utility which it contains.
Les Miserables	now, if the reader will admit with me for a moment that in every man there is one of the animal species of creation, it will be easy for us to say what javert the policeman was.
Les Miserables	the asturian peasants are convinced that in every litter of wolves there is a dog which is killed by the mother, for, otherwise, when it grew it would devour the other whelps.
Les Miserables	give a human face to this dog-son of a she-wolf, and we shall have javert.
Les Miserables	he was born in prison; his mother was a fortune-teller, whose husband was at the galleys.
Les Miserables	when he grew up he thought that he was beyond the pale of society, and despaired of ever entering it.
Les Miserables	he noticed that society inexorably keeps at bay two classes of men,--those who attack it, and those who guard it; he had only a choice between these two classes, and at the same time felt within him a rigidness, regularity, and probity, combined with an inexpressible hatred of the race of bohemians to which he belonged.
Les Miserables	he entered the police, got on, and at the age of forty was an inspector.
Les Miserables	in his youth he was engaged in the southern bagnes.
Les Miserables	before going further, let us explain the words "human face" which we applied just now to javert.
Les Miserables	his human face consisted of a stub-nose, with two enormous nostrils, toward which enormous whiskers mounted on his cheeks.
Les Miserables	you felt uncomfortable the first time that you saw these two forests and these two caverns.
Les Miserables	when javert laughed, which was rare and terrible, his thin lips parted, and displayed, not only his teeth, but his gums, and a savage flat curl formed round his nose, such as is seen on the muzzle of a wild beast.
Les Miserables	javert when serious was a bull-dog; when he laughed he was a tiger.
Les Miserables	to sum up, he had but little skull and plenty of jaw; his hair hid his forehead and fell over his brows; he had between his eyes a central and permanent frown, like a star of anger, an obscure glance, a pinched-up and formidable mouth, and an air of ferocious command.
Les Miserables	this man was made up of two very simple and relatively excellent feelings, but which he almost rendered bad by exaggerating them,--respect for authority and hatred of rebellion; and in his eyes, robbery, murder, and every crime were only forms of rebellion.
Les Miserables	he enveloped in a species of blind faith everybody in the service of the state, from the prime minister down to the game-keeper.
Les Miserables	he covered with contempt, aversion, and disgust, every one who had once crossed the legal threshold of evil.
Les Miserables	he was absolute, and admitted of no exceptions; on one side he said: "a functionary cannot be mistaken, a magistrate can do no wrong;" on the other he said: "they are irremediably lost: no good can come of them."
Les Miserables	he fully shared the opinion of those extreme minds that attribute to the human law some power of making or verifying demons, and that place a styx at the bottom of society.
Les Miserables	he was stoical, stern, and austere; a sad dreamer, and humble yet haughty, like all fanatics.
Les Miserables	his glance was a gimlet, for it was cold and piercing.
Les Miserables	his whole life was composed in the two words, watching and overlooking.
Les Miserables	he had introduced the straight line into what is the most tortuous thing in the world; he was conscious of his usefulness, had religious respect for his duties, and was a spy as well as another is a priest.
Les Miserables	woe to the wretch who came into his clutches!
Les Miserables	he would have arrested his father if escaping from prison, and denounced his mother had she broken her ban.
Les Miserables	and he would have done it with that sort of inner satisfaction which virtue produces.
Les Miserables	with all this he spent a life of privation, isolation, self-denial, chastity.
Les Miserables	he was the implacable duty, the police comprehended as the spartans comprehended sparta, a pitiless watchman, a savage integrity, a marble-hearted spy, a brutus contained in a vidocq.
Les Miserables	javert's entire person expressed the man who spies and hides himself.
Les Miserables	the mystic school of joseph de maîstre, which at this epoch was seasoning with high cosmogony what were called the ultra journals, would not have failed to say that javert was a symbol.
Les Miserables	his forehead could not be seen, for it was hidden by his hat; his eyes could not be seen, because they were lost under his eye-brows; his chin was plunged into his cravat, his hands were covered by his cuffs, and his cane was carried under his coat.
Les Miserables	but when the opportunity arrived, there could be seen suddenly emerging from all this shadow, as from an ambush, an angular, narrow forehead, a fatal glance, a menacing chin, enormous hands, and a monstrous rattan.
Les Miserables	in his leisure moments, which were few, he read, though he hated books, and this caused him not to be utterly ignorant, as could be noticed through a certain emphasis in his language.
Les Miserables	as we have said, he had no vice; when satisfied with himself, he indulged in a pinch of snuff, and that was his connecting link with humanity.
Les Miserables	our readers will readily understand that javert was the terror of all that class whom the yearly statistics of the minister of justice designate under the rubric--vagabonds.
Les Miserables	the name of javert, if uttered, set them to flight; the face of javert, if seen, petrified them.
Les Miserables	such was this formidable man.
Les Miserables	javert was like an eye ever fixed on m. madeleine, an eye fall of suspicion and conjectures.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine noticed it in the end; but he considered it a matter of insignificance.
Les Miserables	he did not even ask javert his motive, he neither sought nor shunned him, and endured his annoying glance without appearing to notice it.
Les Miserables	he treated javert like every one else, easily and kindly.
Les Miserables	from some remarks that dropped from javert, it was supposed that he had secretly sought, with that curiosity belonging to the breed, and in which there is as much instinct as will, all the previous traces which father madeleine might have left.
Les Miserables	he appeared to know, and sometimes said covertly, that some one had obtained certain information in a certain district about a certain family which had disappeared.
Les Miserables	once he happened to say, talking to himself, "i believe that i have got him;" then he remained thoughtful for three days without saying a word.
Les Miserables	it seems that the thread which he fancied he held was broken.
Les Miserables	however, there cannot be any theory really infallible in a human creature, and it is the peculiarity of instinct that it can be troubled, thrown out, and routed.
Les Miserables	if not, it would be superior to intelligence, and the brute would have a better light than man.
Les Miserables	javert was evidently somewhat disconcerted by m. madeleine's complete naturalness and calmness.
Les Miserables	one day, however, his strange manner seemed to produce an impression on m. madeleine.
Les Miserables	the occasion was as follows.
Les Miserables	chapter vi.
Les Miserables	father fauchelevent.
Les Miserables	when m. madeleine was passing one morning through an unpaved lane in the town, he heard a noise and saw a group at some distance, to which he walked up.
Les Miserables	an old man, known as father fauchelevent, had fallen under his cart, and his horse was lying on the ground.
Les Miserables	this fauchelevent was one of the few enemies m. madeleine still had at this time.
Les Miserables	when madeleine came to these parts, fauchelevent, a tolerably well-educated peasant, was doing badly in business; and he saw the simple workman grow rich, while he, a master, was being ruined.
Les Miserables	this filled him with jealousy, and he had done all in his power, on every possible occasion, to injure madeleine.
Les Miserables	then bankruptcy came, and in his old days, having only a horse and cart left, and no family, he turned carter to earn a living.
Les Miserables	the horse had both legs broken and could not get up, while the old man was entangled between the wheels.
Les Miserables	the fall had been so unfortunate, that the whole weight of the cart was pressing on his chest, and it was heavily loaded.
Les Miserables	fauchelevent uttered lamentable groans, and attempts had been made, though in vain, to draw him out; any irregular effort, any clumsy help or shock, might kill him.
Les Miserables	it was impossible to extricate him except by raising the cart from below, and javert, who came up at the moment of the accident, had sent to fetch a jack.
Les Miserables	when m. madeleine approached, the mob made way respectfully.
Les Miserables	"help!"
Les Miserables	old fauchelevent cried; "is there no good soul who will save an old man?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine turned to the spectators.
Les Miserables	"have you a jack?"
Les Miserables	"they have gone to fetch one," a peasant answered.
Les Miserables	"how soon will it be here?"
Les Miserables	"well, the nearest is at flachot the blacksmith's, but it cannot be brought here under a good quarter of an hour."
Les Miserables	"a quarter of an hour!"
Les Miserables	madeleine exclaimed.
Les Miserables	it had rained on the previous night, the ground was soft, the cart sunk deeper into it every moment, and more and more pressed the old man's chest.
Les Miserables	it was evident that his ribs would be broken within five minutes.
Les Miserables	"it is impossible to wait a quarter of an hour," said m. madeleine to the peasants who were looking on.
Les Miserables	"we must."
Les Miserables	"but do you not see that the cart is sinking into the ground?"
Les Miserables	"hang it!
Les Miserables	so it is."
Les Miserables	"listen to me," madeleine continued; "there is still room enough for a man to slip under the cart and raise it with his back.
Les Miserables	it will only take half a minute, and the poor man can be drawn out.
Les Miserables	is there any one here who has strong loins?
Les Miserables	there are five louis to be earned."
Les Miserables	no one stirred.
Les Miserables	"ten louis," madeleine said.
Les Miserables	his hearers looked down, and one of them muttered, "a man would have to be deucedly strong, and, besides, he would run a risk of being smashed."
Les Miserables	"come," madeleine began again, "twenty louis."
Les Miserables	the same silence.
Les Miserables	"it is not the good-will they are deficient in," a voice cried.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine turned and recognized javert: he had noticed him when he came up.
Les Miserables	javert continued,--  "it is the strength.
Les Miserables	a man would have to be tremendously strong to lift a cart like that with his back."
Les Miserables	then, looking fixedly at m. madeleine, he continued, laying a marked stress on every word he uttered,--  "monsieur madeleine, i never knew but _one_ man capable of doing what you ask."
Les Miserables	madeleine started, but javert continued carelessly, though without taking his eyes off madeleine,--  "he was a galley-slave."
Les Miserables	"indeed!"
Les Miserables	said madeleine.
Les Miserables	"at the toulon bagne."
Les Miserables	madeleine turned pale; all this while the cart was slowly settling down, and father fauchelevent was screaming,--  "i am choking: it is breaking my ribs: a jack!
Les Miserables	something--oh!"
Les Miserables	madeleine looked around him.
Les Miserables	"is there no one here willing to earn twenty louis and save this poor old man's life?"
Les Miserables	no one stirred, and javert repeated,--  "i never knew but one man capable of acting as a jack, and it was that convict."
Les Miserables	"oh, it is crushing me!"
Les Miserables	the old man yelled.
Les Miserables	madeleine raised his head, met javert's falcon eye still fixed on him, gazed at the peasants, and sighed sorrowfully.
Les Miserables	then, without saying a word, he fell on his knees, and, ere the crowd had time to utter a cry, was under the cart.
Les Miserables	there was a frightful moment of expectation and silence.
Les Miserables	madeleine almost lying flat under the tremendous weight, twice tried in vain to bring his elbows up to his knees.
Les Miserables	the peasants shouted: "father madeleine, come out!"
Les Miserables	and old fauchelevent himself said: "monsieur madeleine, go away!
Les Miserables	i must die, so leave me; you will be killed too."
Les Miserables	madeleine made no answer; the spectators gasped; the wheels had sunk deeper, and it was now almost impossible for him to get out from under the cart.
Les Miserables	all at once the enormous mass shook, the cart slowly rose, and the wheels half emerged from the rut.
Les Miserables	a stifled voice could be heard crying, "make haste, help!"
Les Miserables	it was madeleine, who had made a last effort.
Les Miserables	they rushed forward, for the devotion of one man had restored strength and courage to all.
Les Miserables	the cart was lifted by twenty arms, and old fauchelevent was saved.
Les Miserables	madeleine rose; he was livid, although dripping with perspiration: his clothes were torn and covered with mud.
Les Miserables	the old man kissed his knees, and called him his savior, while madeleine had on his face a strange expression of happy and celestial suffering, and turned his placid eye on javert, who was still looking at him.
Les Miserables	[illustration: father fauchelevent.]
Les Miserables	chapter vii   fauchelevent becomes a gardener at paris.
Les Miserables	fauchelevent had put out his knee-cap in his fall, and father madeleine had him carried to an infirmary he had established for his workmen in his factory, and which was managed by two sisters of charity.
Les Miserables	the next morning the old man found a thousand-franc note by his bed-side, with a line in m. madeleine's handwriting, "payment for your cart and horse, which i have bought:" the cart was smashed and the horse dead.
Les Miserables	fauchelevent recovered, but his leg remained stiff, and, hence m. madeleine, by the recommendation of the sisters and his curé, procured him a situation as gardener at a convent in the st. antoine quarter of paris.
Les Miserables	some time after, m. madeleine was appointed mayor; the first time javert saw him wearing the scarf which gave him all authority in the town, he felt that sort of excitement a dog would feel that scented a wolf in its master's clothes.
Les Miserables	from this moment he avoided him as much as he could, and when duty imperiously compelled him, and he could not do otherwise than appear before the mayor, he addressed him with profound respect.
Les Miserables	the prosperity created in m---- by father madeleine had, in addition to the visible signs we have indicated, another symptom, which, though not visible, was not the less significant, for it is one that never deceives: when the population is suffering, when work is scarce and trade bad, tax-payers exhaust and exceed the time granted them, and the state spends a good deal of money in enforcing payment.
Les Miserables	when work abounds, when the country is happy and rich, the taxes are paid cheerfully, and cost the state little.
Les Miserables	we may say that wretchedness and the public exchequer have an infallible thermometer in the cost of collecting the taxes.
Les Miserables	in seven years these costs had been reduced three-fourths in the arrondissement of m----, which caused it to be frequently quoted by m. de villele, at that time minister of finances.
Les Miserables	such was the state of the town when fantine returned to it.
Les Miserables	no one remembered her, but luckily the door of m. madeleine's factory was like a friendly face; she presented herself at it, and was admitted to the female shop.
Les Miserables	as the trade was quite new to fantine, she was awkward at it and earned but small wages; but that was enough, for she had solved the problem,--she was earning her livelihood.
Les Miserables	chapter viii.
Les Miserables	madame victurnien spends thirty francs on morality.
Les Miserables	when fantine saw that she could earn her own living, she had a moment of joy.
Les Miserables	to live honestly by her own toil, what a favor of heaven!
Les Miserables	a taste for work really came back to her: she bought a looking-glass, delighted in seeing in it her youth, her fine hair and fine teeth; forgot many things, only thought of cosette, and her possible future, and was almost happy.
Les Miserables	she hired a small room and furnished it, on credit, to be paid for out of her future earnings,--this was a relic of her irregular habits.
Les Miserables	not being able to say that she was married, she was very careful not to drop a word about her child.
Les Miserables	at the outset, as we have seen, she punctually paid the thénardiers; and as she could only sign her name, she was compelled to write to them through the agency of a public writer.
Les Miserables	it was noticed that she wrote frequently.
Les Miserables	it was beginning to be whispered in the shop that fantine "wrote letters," and was "carrying on."
Les Miserables	no one spies the actions of persons so much as those whom they do not concern.
Les Miserables	why does this gentleman never come till nightfall?
Les Miserables	why does so-and-so never hang up his key on thursdays?
Les Miserables	why does he always take back streets?
Les Miserables	why does madame always get out of her hackney coach before reaching her house?
Les Miserables	why does she send out to buy a quire of note-paper, when she has a desk full?
Les Miserables	and so on.
Les Miserables	there are people who, in order to solve these inquiries, which are matters of utter indifference to them, spend more money, lavish more time, and take more trouble, than would be required for ten good deeds: and they do it gratuitously for the pleasure, and they are only paid for their curiosity with curiosity.
Les Miserables	they will follow a gentleman or a lady for whole days, will stand sentry at the corner of a street or in a gateway at night in the cold and rain; corrupt messengers, intoxicate hackney coachmen and footmen, buy a lady's-maid, and make a purchase of a porter,--why?
Les Miserables	for nothing; for a pure desire to see, know, and find out--it is a simple itch for talking.
Les Miserables	and frequently these secrets, when made known, these mysteries published, these enigmas brought to daylight, entail catastrophes, duels, bankruptcies, ruin of families, to the great delight of those who found it all out, without any personal motives, through pure instinct.
Les Miserables	it is a sad thing.
Les Miserables	some persons are wicked solely through a desire to talk, and this conversation, which is gossip in the drawing-room, scandal in the ante-room, is like those chimneys which consume wood rapidly; they require a great deal of combustible, and this combustible is their neighbor.
Les Miserables	fantine was observed then, and besides, more than one girl was jealous of her light hair and white teeth.
Les Miserables	it was noticed that she often wiped away a tear in the shop; it was when she was thinking of her child, perhaps of the man she had loved.
Les Miserables	it is a painful labor to break off all the gloomy connecting links with the past.
Les Miserables	it was a fact that she wrote, at least twice a month, and always to the same address, and paid the postage.
Les Miserables	they managed to obtain the address: "monsieur thénardier, publican, montfermeil."
Les Miserables	the public writer, who could not fill his stomach with wine without emptying his pocket of secrets, was made to talk at the wine-shop; and, in short, it was known that fantine had a child.
Les Miserables	a gossip undertook a journey to montfermeil, spoke to the thénardiers, and on her return said, "i do not begrudge my thirty francs, for i have seen the child."
Les Miserables	the gossip who did this was a gorgon of the name of madame victurnien, guardian and portress of everybody's virtue.
Les Miserables	she was fifty-six years of age, and covered the mask of ugliness with the mask of old age.
Les Miserables	astounding to say, this old woman had once been young; in her youth, in '93, she had married a monk, who escaped from the cloisters in a red cap, and passed over from the bernardines to the jacobins.
Les Miserables	she was dry, crabbed, sharp, thorny, and almost venomous, while remembering the monk whose widow she was and who had considerably tamed her.
Les Miserables	at the restoration she had turned bigot, and so energetically, that the priests forgave her her monk.
Les Miserables	she had a small estate which she left with considerable pallor to a religious community, and she was very welcome at the episcopal palace of arras.
Les Miserables	this madame victurnien, then, went to montfermeil, and when she returned, said, "i have seen the child."
Les Miserables	all this took time, and fantine had been more than a year at the factory, when one morning the forewoman handed her 50 francs in the mayor's name, and told her that she was no longer engaged, and had better leave the town, so the mayor said.
Les Miserables	it was in this very month that the thénardiers, after asking for 12 francs instead of 7, raised a claim for 15 instead of 12. fantine was startled; she could not leave the town, for she owed her rent and for her furniture, and 50 francs would not pay those debts.
Les Miserables	she stammered a few words of entreaty, but the forewoman intimated to her that she must leave the shop at once; moreover, fantine was but an indifferent workwoman.
Les Miserables	crushed by shame more than disgrace, she left the factory, and returned to her room: her fault then was now known to all!
Les Miserables	she did not feel the strength in her to say a word; she was advised to see the mayor, but did not dare do so.
Les Miserables	the mayor gave her 50 francs because he was kind, and discharged her because he was just; and she bowed her head to the sentence.
Les Miserables	chapter ix.
Les Miserables	success of madame victurnien.
Les Miserables	the monk's widow, then, was good for something.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine, however, knew nothing of all this; and they were combinations of events of which the world is fall.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine made it a rule hardly, ever to enter the female work-room; he had placed at its head an old maid, whom the curé had given him, and he had entire confidence in her.
Les Miserables	she was really a respectable, firm, equitable, and just person, fall of that charity which consists in giving, but not possessing to the same extent the charity which comprehends and pardons.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine trusted to her in everything, for the best men are often forced to delegate their authority, and it was with this fall power, and in the conviction she was acting rightly, that the forewoman tried, condemned, and executed fantine.
Les Miserables	as for the 50 francs, she had given them out of a sum m. madeleine had given her for alms and helping the workwomen, and which she did not account for.
Les Miserables	fantine tried to get a servant's place in the town, and went from house to house, but no one would have anything to do with her.
Les Miserables	she could not leave the town, for the broker to whom she was in debt for her furniture--what furniture!--said to her, "if you go away, i will have you arrested as a thief."
Les Miserables	the landlord to whom she owed her rent, said to her, "you are young and pretty, you can pay."
Les Miserables	she divided the 50 francs between the landlord and the broker, gave back to the latter three-fourths of the goods, only retaining what was absolutely necessary, and found herself without work, without a trade, with only a bed, and still owing about 100 francs.
Les Miserables	she set to work making coarse shirts for the troops, and earned at this sixpence a day, her daughter costing her fourpence.
Les Miserables	it was at this moment she began to fall in arrears with the thénardiers.
Les Miserables	an old woman, however, who lit her candle for her when she came in at nights, taught her the way to live in wretchedness.
Les Miserables	behind living on little, there is living on nothing: there are two chambers,--the first is obscure, the second quite dark.
Les Miserables	fantine learned how she could do entirely without fire in winter, how she must get rid of a bird that cost her a halfpenny every two days, how she could make a petticoat of her blanket and a blanket of her petticoat, and how candle can be saved by taking your meals by the light of the window opposite.
Les Miserables	we do not know all that certain weak beings, who have grown old in want and honesty, can get out of a halfpenny, and in the end it becomes a talent.
Les Miserables	fantine acquired this sublime talent, and regained a little courage.
Les Miserables	at this period she said to a neighbor, "nonsense, i say to myself; by only sleeping for five hours and working all the others at my needle, i shall always manage to earn bread, at any rate.
Les Miserables	and then, when you are sad, you eat less.
Les Miserables	well!
Les Miserables	suffering, anxiety, a little bread on one side and sorrow on the other, all will support me."
Les Miserables	in this distress, it would have been a strange happiness to have had her daughter with her, and she thought of sending for her.
Les Miserables	but, what!
Les Miserables	make her share her poverty?
Les Miserables	and then she owed money to the thénardiers!
Les Miserables	how was she to pay it and the travelling expenses?
Les Miserables	the old woman who had given her lessons in what may be called indigent life, was a pious creature, poor, and charitable to the poor and even to the rich, who could just write her name, "marguerite," and believed in god, which is knowledge.
Les Miserables	there are many such virtues down here, and one day they will be up above, for this life has a morrow.
Les Miserables	at the beginning fantine had been so ashamed that she did not dare go out.
Les Miserables	when she was in the streets, she perceived that people turned round to look at her and pointed to her.
Les Miserables	every one stared at her, and no one bowed to her; the cold bitter contempt of the passers-by passed through her flesh and her mind like an east wind.
Les Miserables	in small towns an unhappy girl seems to be naked beneath the sarcasm and curiosity of all.
Les Miserables	in paris, at least no one knows you, and that obscurity is a garment.
Les Miserables	oh!
Les Miserables	how glad she would have been to be back in paris.
Les Miserables	she must grow accustomed to disrespect, as she had done to poverty.
Les Miserables	gradually she made up her mind, and after two or three months shook off her shame, and went as if nothing had occurred.
Les Miserables	"it is no matter to me," she said.
Les Miserables	she came and went, with head erect and with a bitter smile, and felt that she was growing impudent.
Les Miserables	madame victurnien sometimes saw her pass from her window; she noticed the distress of "the creature whom she had made know her place," and congratulated herself.
Les Miserables	the wicked have a black happiness.
Les Miserables	excessive labor fatigued fantine, and the little dry cough she had grew worse.

Les Miserables	still, in the morning, when she passed an old broken comb through her glorious hair, which shone like floss silk, she had a minute of happy coquettishness.
Les Miserables	chapter x.   result of her success.
Les Miserables	she had been discharged toward the end of winter; the next summer passed away, and winter returned.
Les Miserables	short days and less work; in winter there is no warmth, no light, no mid-day, for the evening is joined to the morning; there is fog, twilight, the window is gray, and you cannot see clearly.
Les Miserables	the sky is like a dark vault, and the sun has the look of a poor man.
Les Miserables	it is a frightful season; winter changes into stone the water of heaven and the heart of man.
Les Miserables	her creditors pressed her, for fantine was earning too little, and her debts had increased.
Les Miserables	the thénardiers, being irregularly paid, constantly wrote her letters, whose contents afflicted her, and postage ruined her.
Les Miserables	one day they wrote her that little cosette was quite naked, that she wanted a flannel skirt, and that the mother must send at least ten francs for the purpose.
Les Miserables	she crumpled the letter in her hands all day, and at nightfall went to a barber's at the corner of the street, and removed her comb.

Les Miserables	she had long shared the universal veneration for father madeleine: but, through the constant iteration that he had discharged her and was the cause of her misfortune, she grew to hate him too, and worse than the rest.
Les Miserables	when she passed the factory she pretended to laugh and sing.
Les Miserables	an old workwoman who once saw her doing so, said, "that's a girl who will come to a bad end."

Les Miserables	she did not get rid of her cough, and she felt a cold perspiration in her back.
Les Miserables	one day she received from the thénardiers a letter to the following effect: "cosette is ill with a complaint which is very prevalent in the country.
Les Miserables	it is called miliary fever.
Les Miserables	she must have expensive drugs, and that ruins us, and we cannot pay for them any longer.

Les Miserables	and she answered, "it is a piece of stupidity some country folk have written; they want forty francs of me--the asses."
Les Miserables	as she passed across the market-place she saw a crowd surrounding a vehicle of a strange shape, on the box of which a man dressed in red was haranguing.

Les Miserables	it had only been a trick of the thénardiers to get money, for cosette was not ill.  fantine threw her looking-glass out of the window; she had long before left her cell on the second floor for a garret under the roof,--one of those tenements in which the ceiling forms an angle with the floor, and you knock your head at every step.
Les Miserables	the poor man can only go to the end of his room, as to the end of his destiny, by stooping more and more.

Les Miserables	seventeen hours' work for nine sous!

Les Miserables	about the same time thénardier wrote to her, that he had decidedly waited too patiently, and that unless he received one hundred francs at once, he would turn poor cosette, who had scarce recovered, out of doors into the cold, and she must do what she could or die.

Les Miserables	chapter xl   christus nos liberavit.
Les Miserables	what is this story of fantine?
Les Miserables	it is society buying a slave.
Les Miserables	of whom?
Les Miserables	of misery, of hunger, of cold, of loneliness, of desertion, of destitution.
Les Miserables	cursed bargain!
Les Miserables	a soul for a morsel of bread.
Les Miserables	misery offers its wares, and society accepts.
Les Miserables	the holy law of jesus christ governs our civilization, but it does not yet pervade it.
Les Miserables	they say that slavery has disappeared from european civilization.

Les Miserables	it weighs on woman; that is, on grace, on helplessness, on beauty, on motherhood.
Les Miserables	this is not one of the least reproaches upon man.
Les Miserables	at the point which we have reached in this painful drama, there is nothing left in fantine of her former self.
Les Miserables	she became marble when she became mud.
Les Miserables	whoever touches her is chilled.
Les Miserables	she is handed along, she submits to you, but she forgets your presence.
Les Miserables	she is the type of dishonor and rigidity.
Les Miserables	life and social order have said to her their last word.
Les Miserables	everything that can happen to her, has already happened.
Les Miserables	she has felt all, borne all, endured all, suffered all, lost all, wept for all.

Les Miserables	she shuns nothing now.

Les Miserables	let the whole sky fall on her, let the whole ocean pass over her!

Les Miserables	she is a sponge soaked full.

Les Miserables	he who knows this sees the whole shadow.
Les Miserables	he is one alone.
Les Miserables	his name is god.
Les Miserables	chapter xii.
Les Miserables	m. bamatabois' idleness.
Les Miserables	there is in all small towns, and there was at m---- in particular, a class of young men, who squander fifteen hundred francs a year in the provinces with the same air as those of the same set in paris devour two hundred thousand.
Les Miserables	they are beings of the great neutral species; geldings, parasites, nobodies, who possess a little land, a little folly, and a little wit, who would be rustics in a drawing-room, and believe themselves gentlemen in a pot-house.
Les Miserables	they talk about my fields, my woods, my peasants, horses, the actresses, to prove themselves men of taste; quarrel with the officers, to prove themselves men of war; shoot, smoke, yawn, drink, smell of tobacco, play at billiards, watch the travellers get out of the stage-coach, live at the café, dine at the inn, have a dog that gnaws bones under the table, and a mistress who places the dishes upon it; haggle over a son, exaggerate the fashions, admire tragedy, despise women, wear out their old boots, copy london through paris, and paris through pont-à-mousson; grow stupidly old, do not work, are of no use, and do no great harm.
Les Miserables	had m. félix tholomyès remained in his province and not seen paris, he would have been one of them.
Les Miserables	if they were richer, people would say they are dandies; if poorer, they are loafers; but they are simply men without occupation.
Les Miserables	among them there are bores and bored, dreamers, and a few scamps.
Les Miserables	at that day, a dandy was composed of a tall collar, a large cravat, a watch and seals, three waist-coats over one another, blue and red inside, a short-waisted olive-colored coat, with a swallow tail, and a double row of silver buttons, sewn on close together, and ascending to the shoulders, and trousers of a lighter olive, adorned on the seams with an undetermined but always uneven number of ribs, varying from one to eleven, a limit which was never exceeded.
Les Miserables	add to this, slipper-boots with iron-capped heels, a tall, narrow-brimmed hat, hair in a tuft, an enormous cane, and a conversation improved by potier's puns; over and above all these were spurs and moustachios, for at that period moustachios indicated the civilian, and spurs the pedestrian.
Les Miserables	the provincial dandy wore longer spurs and more ferocious moustachios.
Les Miserables	it was the period of the struggle of the south american republics against the king of spain, of bolivar against morillo.
Les Miserables	narrow-brimmed hats were royalist, and called morillos, while the liberals wore broad brims, which were called bolivars.
Les Miserables	eight or ten months after the events we have described in the previous chapter, toward the beginning of january, and on a night when snow had fallen, one of these dandies--a man of "right sentiments," for he wore a morillo, and was also warmly wrapped up in one of the large spanish cloaks which at that time completed the fashionable costume in cold weather--was amusing himself by annoying a creature who was prowling about in a low-neck balldress, and with flowers in her hair, before the window of the officers' café.
Les Miserables	this dandy was smoking, as that was a decided mark of fashion.
Les Miserables	each time this woman passed him, he made some remark to her, which he fancied witty and amusing, as: "how ugly you are!--why don't you go to kennel?--you have no teeth," etc., etc.
Les Miserables	this gentleman's name was monsieur bamatabois.
Les Miserables	the woman, a sad-dressed phantom walking backwards and forwards in the snow, made him no answer, did not even look at him, but still continued silently and with a gloomy regularity her walk, which, every few minutes, brought her under his sarcasms, like the condemned soldier running the gauntlet.
Les Miserables	the slight effect produced doubtless annoyed the idler, for taking advantage of her back being turned, he crept up behind her, stooped to pick up a handful of snow, and suddenly plunged it between her bare shoulders.
Les Miserables	the girl uttered a yell, turned, leaped like a panther on the man, and dug her nails into his face with the most frightful language that could fall from a guard-room into the gutter.
Les Miserables	these insults, vomited by a voice rendered hoarse by brandy, hideously issued from a mouth in which the two front teeth were really missing.
Les Miserables	it was fantine.
Les Miserables	at the noise, the officers left the café in a throng, the passers-by stopped, and a laughing, yelling, applauding circle was made round these two beings, in whom it was difficult to recognize a man and a woman,--the man struggling, his hat on the ground, the woman striking with feet and fists, bareheaded, yelling, without teeth or hair, livid with passion, and horrible.
Les Miserables	all at once a tall man quickly broke through the crowd, seized the woman's satin dress, which was covered with mud, and said: "follow me."
Les Miserables	the woman raised her hand, and her passionate voice suddenly died out.
Les Miserables	her eyes were glassy, she grew pale instead of being livid, and trembled with fear.
Les Miserables	she had recognized javert.
Les Miserables	the dandy profited by this incident to make his escape.
Les Miserables	chapter xiii.
Les Miserables	the police office.
Les Miserables	javert broke through the circle and began walking with long strides toward the police office, which is at the other end of the market-place, dragging the wretched girl after him.
Les Miserables	she allowed him to do so mechanically, and neither he nor she said a word.
Les Miserables	the crowd of spectators, in a paroxysm of delight, followed them with coarse jokes, for supreme misery is an occasion for obscenities.
Les Miserables	on reaching the police office, which was a low room, heated by a stove, and guarded by a sentry, and having a barred glass door opening on the street, javert walked in with fantine, and shut the door after him, to the great disappointment of the curious, who stood on tip-toe, and stretched out their necks in front of the dirty window trying to see.
Les Miserables	curiosity is gluttony, and seeing is devouring.
Les Miserables	on entering, fantine crouched down motionless in a corner like a frightened dog.
Les Miserables	the sergeant on duty brought in a candle.
Les Miserables	javert sat down at a table, took a sheet of stamped paper from his pocket, and began writing.
Les Miserables	women of this class are by the french laws left entirely at the discretion of the police: they do what they like with them, punish them as they think proper, and confiscate the two sad things which they call their trade and their liberty.
Les Miserables	javert was stoical: his grave face displayed no emotion, and yet he was seriously and deeply preoccupied.
Les Miserables	it was one of those moments in which he exercised without control, but with all the scruples of a strict conscience, his formidable discretionary power.
Les Miserables	at this instant he felt that his high stool was a tribunal, and himself the judge.
Les Miserables	he tried and he condemned: he summoned all the ideas he had in his mind round the great thing he was doing.
Les Miserables	the more he examined the girl's deed, the more outraged he felt: for it was evident that he had just seen a crime committed.

Les Miserables	a prostitute had assaulted a citizen, and he, javert, had witnessed it.
Les Miserables	he wrote on silently.

Les Miserables	oh, monsieur javert, you said that i was to be set at liberty, did you not?
Les Miserables	make inquiries, speak to my landlord; i pay my rent now, and you will hear that i am honest.
Les Miserables	oh, good gracious!
Les Miserables	i ask your pardon, but i have touched the damper of the stove without noticing it, and made a smoke."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine listened to her with deep attention: while she was talking, he took out his purse, but as he found it empty on opening it, he returned it to his pocket.
Les Miserables	he now said to fantine,--  "how much did you say that you owed?"
Les Miserables	fantine, who was looking at javert, turned round to him,--  "am i speaking to you?"
Les Miserables	then she said to the soldiers,--  "tell me, men, did you see how i spat in his face?
Les Miserables	ah, you old villain of a mayor!
Les Miserables	you have come here to frighten me, but i am not afraid of you; i am only afraid of m. javert, my kind monsieur javert."
Les Miserables	while saying this, she turned again to the inspector,--  "after all, people should be just.
Les Miserables	i can understand that you are a just man, m. javert; in fact, it is quite simple; a man who played at putting snow down a woman's back, made the officers laugh; they must have some amusement, and we girls are sent into the world for them to make fun of.
Les Miserables	and then you came up: you are compelled to restore order; you remove the woman who was in the wrong, but, on reflection, as you are kind-hearted, you order me to be set at liberty, for the sake of my little girl, for six months' imprisonment would prevent my supporting her.
Les Miserables	only don't come here again, fagot!
Les Miserables	oh, i will not come here again, m. javert; they can do what they like to me in future, and i will not stir.
Les Miserables	still i cried out to-night because it hurt me; i did not at all expect that gentleman's snow; and then besides, as i told you; i am not very well,--i cough, i have something like a ball in my stomach which burns, and the doctor says: "take care of yourself."
Les Miserables	here, feel, give me your hand; do not be frightened."
Les Miserables	she no longer cried, her voice was caressing; she laid javert's large coarse hand on her white, delicate throat, and looked up at him smilingly.
Les Miserables	all at once she hurriedly repaired the disorder in her clothes, let the folds of her dress fall, which had been almost dragged up to her knee, and walked toward the door, saying to the soldiers with a friendly nod,--  "my lads, m. javert says i may go, so i will be off."
Les Miserables	she laid her hand on the hasp; one step further, and she would be in the street.
Les Miserables	up to this moment javert had stood motionless, with his eyes fixed on the ground, appearing in the centre of this scene like a statue waiting to be put up in its proper place.
Les Miserables	the sound of the hasp aroused him: he raised his head with an expression of sovereign authority,--an expression the more frightful, the lower the man in power stands; it is ferocity in the wild beast, atrocity in the nobody.
Les Miserables	"sergeant," he shouted, "do you not see that the wench is bolting?
Les Miserables	who told you to let her go?"
Les Miserables	"i did," said madeleine.
Les Miserables	fantine, at the sound of javert's voice, trembled, and let go the hasp, as a detected thief lets fall the stolen article.
Les Miserables	at madeleine's voice she turned, and from this moment, without uttering a word, without even daring to breathe freely, her eye wandered from madeleine to javert, and from javert to madeleine, according as each spoke.
Les Miserables	it was evident that javert must have been "lifted off the hinges," as people say, when he ventured to address the sergeant as he had done, after the mayor's request that fantine should be set at liberty.
Les Miserables	had he gone so far as to forget the mayor's presence?
Les Miserables	did he eventually declare to himself that it was impossible for "an authority" to have given such an order, and that the mayor must certainly have said one thing for another without meaning it?
Les Miserables	or was it that, in the presence of all the enormities he had witnessed during the last two hours, he said to himself that he must have recourse to a supreme resolution, that the little must become great, the detective be transformed into the magistrate, and that, in this prodigious extremity, order, law, morality, government, and society were personified in him, javert?
Les Miserables	however this may be, when m. madeleine said "i did," the inspector of police could be seen to turn to the mayor, pale, cold, with blue lips, with a desperate glance, and an imperceptible tremor all over him, and--extraordinary circumstance!--to say to him, with downcast eye, but in a fierce voice,--  "monsieur le maire, that cannot be."
Les Miserables	"why so?"
Les Miserables	"this creature has insulted a gentleman."
Les Miserables	"inspector javert," m. madeleine replied with a conciliating and calm accent, "listen to me.
Les Miserables	you are an honest man, and i shall have no difficulty in coming to an explanation with you.
Les Miserables	the truth is as follows: i was crossing the market-place at the time you were leading this girl away; a crowd was still assembled; i inquired, and know all.
Les Miserables	the man was in the wrong, and, in common justice, ought to have been arrested instead of her."
Les Miserables	javert objected,--  "the wretched creature has just insulted m. le maire."
Les Miserables	"that concerns myself," m. madeleine said; "my insult is, perhaps, my own, and i can do what i like with it."
Les Miserables	"i ask your pardon, sir; the insult does not belong to you, but to the judicial court."
Les Miserables	"inspector javert," madeleine replied, "conscience is the highest of all courts.
Les Miserables	i have heard the woman, and know what i am doing."
Les Miserables	"and i, monsieur le maire, do not know what i am seeing."
Les Miserables	"in that case, be content with obeying."
Les Miserables	"i obey my duty; my duty orders that this woman should go to prison for six months."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine answered gently,--  "listen to this carefully; she will not go for a single day."
Les Miserables	on hearing these decided words, javert ventured to look fixedly at the mayor, and said to him, though still with a respectful accent,--  "i bitterly regret being compelled to resist you.
Les Miserables	monsieur le maire, it is the first time in my life, but you will deign to let me observe that i am within the limits of my authority.
Les Miserables	as you wish it, sir, i will confine myself to the affair with the gentleman.
Les Miserables	i was present; this girl attacked m. bamatabois, who is an elector and owner of that fine three-storied house, built of hewn stone, which forms the corner of the esplanade.
Les Miserables	well, there are things in this world!
Les Miserables	however this may be, m. le maire, this is a matter of the street police which concerns me, and i intend to punish the woman fantine."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine upon this folded his arms, and said in a stern voice, which no one in the town had ever heard before,--  "the affair to which you allude belongs to the borough police; and by the terms of articles nine, eleven, fifteen, and sixty-six of the criminal code, i try it.
Les Miserables	i order that this woman be set at liberty."
Les Miserables	javert tried a final effort.
Les Miserables	"but, monsieur le maire--"  "i call your attention to article eighty-one of the law of dec. 13th, 1799, upon arbitrary detention."
Les Miserables	"permit me, sir--"  "not a word!"
Les Miserables	"still--"  "leave the room!"
Les Miserables	said m. madeleine.
Les Miserables	javert received the blow right in his chest like a russian soldier; he bowed down to the ground to the mayor, and went out.
Les Miserables	fantine stood up against the door, and watched him pass by her in stupor.
Les Miserables	she too was suffering from a strange perturbation: for she had seen herself, so to speak, contended for by two opposite powers.
Les Miserables	she had seen two men struggling in her presence, who held in their hands her liberty, her life, her soul, her child.
Les Miserables	one of these men dragged her towards the gloom, the other restored her to the light.
Les Miserables	in this struggle, which she gazed at through the exaggeration of terror, the two men seemed to her giants,--one spoke like a demon, the other like her good angel.
Les Miserables	the angel had vanquished the demon, and the thing which made her shudder from head to foot was that this angel, this liberator, was the very man whom she abhorred, the mayor whom she had so long regarded as the cause of all her woes; and at the very moment when she had insulted him in such a hideous way, he saved her.
Les Miserables	could she be mistaken?
Les Miserables	must she change her whole soul?
Les Miserables	she did not know, but she trembled; she listened wildly, she looked on with terror, and at every word that m. madeleine said, she felt the darkness of hatred fade away in her heart, and something glowing and ineffable spring up in its place, which was composed of joy, confidence, and love.
Les Miserables	when javert had left the room, m. madeleine turned to her, and said in a slow voice, like a serious man who is making an effort to restrain his tears,--  "i have heard your story.
Les Miserables	i knew nothing about what you have said, but i believe, i feel, that it is true.
Les Miserables	i was even ignorant that you had left the factory, but why did you not apply to me?
Les Miserables	this is what i will do for you; i will pay your debts and send for your child, or you can go to it.
Les Miserables	you can live here, in paris, or wherever you please, and i will provide for your child and yourself.
Les Miserables	i will give you all the money you require, and you will become respectable again in becoming happy; and i will say more than that: if all be as you say, and i do not doubt it, you have never ceased to be virtuous and holy in the sight of god!
Les Miserables	poor woman!"
Les Miserables	this was more than poor fantine could endure.
Les Miserables	to have her cosette!
Les Miserables	to leave this infamous life!
Les Miserables	to live free, rich, happy, and respectable with cosette!
Les Miserables	to see all these realities of paradise suddenly burst into flower, in the midst of her wretchedness!
Les Miserables	she looked as if stunned at the person who was speaking, and could only sob two or three times: "oh, oh, oh!"
Les Miserables	her legs gave way, she fell on her knees before m. madeleine, and before he could prevent it, he felt her seize his hand and press her lips to it.
Les Miserables	then she fainted.
Les Miserables	book vi.
Les Miserables	javert.
Les Miserables	chapter i.   the commencement of repose.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine had fantine conveyed to the infirmary he had established in his own house, and intrusted her to the sisters, who put her to bed.
Les Miserables	a violent fever had broken out; she spent a part of the night in raving and talking aloud, but at length fell asleep.
Les Miserables	on the morrow, at about mid-day, fantine woke, and hearing a breathing close to her bed, she drew the curtain aside, and noticed m. madeleine gazing at something above her head.
Les Miserables	his glance was full of pity and agony, and supplicated: she followed its direction, and saw that it was fixed on a crucifix nailed to the wall.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine was now transfigured in fantine's eyes, and seemed to her surrounded by light.
Les Miserables	he was absorbed in a species of prayer, and she looked at him for some time without daring to interrupt him, but at length said, timidly,--  "what are you doing there?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine had been standing at this spot for an hour, waiting till fantine should wake.
Les Miserables	he took her hand, felt her pulse, and answered,--  "how are you?"
Les Miserables	"very comfortable; i have slept, and fancy i am better.
Les Miserables	it will be nothing."
Les Miserables	he continued answering the question she had asked him first, and as if he had only just heard it,--  "i was praying to the martyr up there;" and he mentally added, "for the martyr down here."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine had spent the night and morning in making inquiries, and had learned everything; he knew all the poignant details of fantine's history.
Les Miserables	he continued,--  "you have suffered deeply, poor mother.
Les Miserables	oh!
Les Miserables	do not complain, for you have at present the dowry of the elect: it is in this way that human beings become angels.
Les Miserables	it is not their fault; they do not know what to do otherwise.
Les Miserables	the hell you have now left is the ante-room to heaven, and you were obliged to begin with that."
Les Miserables	he breathed a deep sigh, but she smiled upon him with the sublime smile in which two teeth were wanting.
Les Miserables	javert had written a letter during the past night, and posted it himself the next morning.
Les Miserables	it was for paris, and the address was: "monsieur chabouillet, secretary to the prefect of police."
Les Miserables	as a rumor had spread about the affair in the police office, the lady-manager of the post, and some other persons who saw the letter before it was sent off and recognized javert's handwriting, supposed that he was sending in his resignation.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine hastened to write to the thénardiers.
Les Miserables	fantine owed them over 120 francs, and he sent them 300, bidding them pay themselves out of the amount, and bring the child at once to m----, where a sick mother was awaiting it.
Les Miserables	this dazzled thénardier.
Les Miserables	"hang it all!"
Les Miserables	he said to his wife, "we must not let the brat go, for the lark will become a milch cow for us.
Les Miserables	i see it all; some fellow has fallen in love with the mother."
Les Miserables	he replied by sending a bill for 500 and odd francs very well drawn up.
Les Miserables	in this bill two undeniable amounts figure, one from a physician, the other from an apothecary, who had attended éponine and azelma in a long illness.
Les Miserables	cosette, as we said, had not been ill, and hence it was merely a little substitution of names.
Les Miserables	at the bottom of the bill thénardier gave credit for 300 francs received on account.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine at once sent 300 francs more, and wrote, "make haste and bring cosette."
Les Miserables	"christi!"
Les Miserables	said thénardier, "we must not let the child go."
Les Miserables	in the mean while fantine did not recover, and still remained in the infirmary.
Les Miserables	the sisters had at first received and nursed "this girl" with some repugnance; any one who has seen the bas-relief at rheims will remember the pouting lower lip of the wise virgins looking at the foolish virgins.
Les Miserables	this ancient contempt of vestals for ambubaïæ is one of the deepest instincts of the feminine dignity, and the sisters had experienced it, with the increased dislike which religion adds.
Les Miserables	but in a few days fantine disarmed them; she had all sorts of humble and gentle words, and the mother within her was touching.
Les Miserables	one day the sisters heard her say in the paroxysm of fever, "i have been a sinner, but when i have my child by my side, that will show that god has forgiven me.
Les Miserables	while i was living badly, i should not have liked to have cosette with me, for i could not have endured her sad and astonished eyes.
Les Miserables	and yet it was for her sake that i did wrong, and for that reason god pardons me.
Les Miserables	i shall feel the blessing of heaven when cosette is here; i shall look at her, and it will do me good to see the innocent creature.
Les Miserables	she knows nothing, as she is an angel.
Les Miserables	my sisters, at her age the wings have not yet dropped off."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine went to see her twice a day, and every time she asked him, "shall i see my cosette soon?"
Les Miserables	he would answer,--  "to-morrow, perhaps; she may arrive at any moment, for i am expecting her."
Les Miserables	and the mother's pale face would grow radiant.
Les Miserables	"oh!"
Les Miserables	she said, "how happy i shall be!"
Les Miserables	we have said that she did not improve; on the contrary, her condition seemed to grow worse week by week.
Les Miserables	the handful of snow placed between her naked shoulder-blades produced a sudden check of perspiration, which caused the illness that had smouldered in her for years suddenly to break out.
Les Miserables	larmier's fine method for studying and healing diseases of the lungs was just beginning to be employed; the physician placed the stethoscope to fantine's chest, and shook his head.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine said to him,--  "well?"
Les Miserables	"has she not a child that she wishes to see?"
Les Miserables	asked the doctor.
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"well, make haste to send for her."
Les Miserables	madeleine gave a start, and fantine asked him,--  "what did the doctor say to you?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine forced a smile.
Les Miserables	"he said that your child must come at once, for that would cure you."
Les Miserables	"oh," she replied, "he is right; but what do those thénardiers mean by keeping my cosette?
Les Miserables	oh, she will come, and then i shall see happiness close to me."
Les Miserables	thénardier, however, would not let the child go, and alleged a hundred poor excuses.
Les Miserables	cosette was ailing, and it would be dangerous for her to travel in winter; and then there were some small debts still to pay, which he was collecting, &c.  "i will send some one to fetch cosette," said father madeleine; "if necessary, i will go myself."
Les Miserables	he wrote to fantine's dictation the following letter, which she signed.
Les Miserables	"m. thénardier,--" you will hand over cosette to the bearer, who will pay up all little matters.
Les Miserables	"yours, fantine."
Les Miserables	about this time a great incident happened.
Les Miserables	however cleverly we may have carved the mysterious block of which our life is made, the black vein of destiny ever reappears in it.
Les Miserables	chapter ii.
Les Miserables	how "jean" may become "champ."
Les Miserables	one morning m. madeleine was in his study, engaged in settling some pressing mayoralty matters, in case he decided on the journey to montfermeil, when he was told that inspector javert wished to speak with him.
Les Miserables	on hearing this name pronounced, m. madeleine could not refrain from a disagreeable impression.
Les Miserables	since the guard-room adventure javert had avoided him more than ever, and m. madeleine had not seen him again.
Les Miserables	"show him in," he said.
Les Miserables	javert entered.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine remained at his table near the fire-place with a pen in his hand and his eyes fixed on a bundle of papers, which he ran through and annotated.
Les Miserables	he did not put himself out of the way for javert, for he could not refrain from thinking of poor fantine.
Les Miserables	javert bowed respectfully to the mayor, who had his back turned to him; the mayor did not look at him, but continued to make his notes.
Les Miserables	javert walked a little way into the study, and then halted without a word.
Les Miserables	a physiognomist familiar with javert's nature, and who had studied for any length of time this savage in the service of civilization,--this strange composite of the roman, the spartan, the monk, and the corporal, this spy incapable of falsehood, this virgin detective,--a physiognomist aware of his secret and old aversion to m. madeleine, and his conflict with him about fantine, and who regarded javert at this moment, would have asked himself, what has happened?
Les Miserables	it was evident to any one who knew this upright, clear, sincere, honest, austere, and ferocious conscience, that javert had just emerged from some great internal struggle.
Les Miserables	javert had nothing in his mind which he did not also have in his face, and, like all violent men, he was subject to sudden changes.
Les Miserables	never had his face been stranger or more surprising.
Les Miserables	on entering, he bowed to m. madeleine with a look in which there was neither rancor, anger, nor suspicion; he had halted a few yards behind the mayor's chair, and was now standing there in an almost military attitude, with the simple cold rudeness of a man who has never been gentle and has ever been patient.
Les Miserables	he was waiting, without saying a word, without making a movement, in a true humility and tranquil resignation, till the mayor might think proper to turn round,--calm, serious, hat in hand, and with an expression which was half-way between the private before his officer and the culprit before the judge.
Les Miserables	all the feelings as well as all the resolutions he might be supposed to possess had disappeared: there was nothing but a gloomy sadness on this face, which was impenetrable and simple as granite.
Les Miserables	his whole person displayed humiliation and firmness, and a sort of courageous despondency.
Les Miserables	at length the mayor laid down his pen and half turned round.
Les Miserables	"well, what is the matter, javert?"
Les Miserables	javert remained silent for a moment, as if reflecting, and then raised his voice with a sad solemnity, which, however, did not exclude simplicity.
Les Miserables	"a culpable deed has been committed, sir."
Les Miserables	"what deed?"
Les Miserables	"an inferior agent of authority has failed in his respect to a magistrate in the gravest matter.
Les Miserables	i have come, as is my duty, to bring the fact to your knowledge."
Les Miserables	"who is this agent?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine asked  "myself."
Les Miserables	"and who is the magistrate who has cause to complain of the agent?"
Les Miserables	"you, monsieur le maire."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine sat up, and javert continued with a stern air and still looking down,--  "monsieur le maire, i have come to request that you will procure my dismissal from the service."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine in his stupefaction opened his mouth, but javert interrupted him,--  "you will say that i could have sent in my resignation, but that is not enough.
Les Miserables	such a course is honorable, but i have done wrong, and deserve punishment.
Les Miserables	i must be dismissed."
Les Miserables	and after a pause he added,--  "monsieur le maire, you were severe to me the other day unjustly, be so to-day justly."
Les Miserables	"what is the meaning of all this nonsense?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine exclaimed.
Les Miserables	"what is the culpable act you have committed?
Les Miserables	what have you done to me?
