Les Miserables	you accuse yourself, you wish to be removed--"  "dismissed," said javert.
Les Miserables	"very good, dismissed.
Les Miserables	i do not understand it."
Les Miserables	"you shall do so, sir."
Les Miserables	javert heaved a deep sigh, and continued still coldly and sadly,--  "six weeks ago, m. le maire, after the scene about that girl, i was furious, and denounced you."
Les Miserables	"denounced me?"
Les Miserables	"to the prefect of police at paris."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine, who did not laugh much oftener than javert, burst into a laugh.
Les Miserables	"as a mayor who had encroached on the police?"
Les Miserables	"as an ex-galley slave."
Les Miserables	the mayor turned livid, but javert, who had not raised his eyes, continued,--  "i thought you were so, and have had these notions for a long time.
Les Miserables	a resemblance, information you sought at faverolles, the strength of your loins, the adventures with old fauchelevent, your skill in firing, your leg which halts a little--and so on.
Les Miserables	it was very absurd, but i took you for a man of the name of jean valjean."
Les Miserables	"what name did you say?"
Les Miserables	"jean valjean; he is a convict i saw twenty years ago when i was assistant keeper at the toulon bagne.
Les Miserables	on leaving the galley, this valjean, as it appears, robbed a bishop, and then committed a highway robbery on a little savoyard.
Les Miserables	for eight years he has been out of the way and could not be found, and i imagined--in a word, i did as i said.
Les Miserables	passion decided me, and i denounced you to the prefect."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine, who had taken up the charge-book again, said with a careless accent,--  "and what was the answer you received?"
Les Miserables	"that i was mad!"
Les Miserables	"well?"
Les Miserables	"they were right."
Les Miserables	"it is fortunate that you allow it."
Les Miserables	"i must do so, for the real jean valjean has been found."
Les Miserables	the book m. madeleine was holding fell from his grasp, he raised his head, looked searchingly at javert, said with an indescribable accent,--  "ah!"
Les Miserables	javert continued,--  "the facts are these, m. le maire.
Les Miserables	it seems that there was over at ailly le haut clocher, an old fellow who was called father champmathieu.
Les Miserables	he was very wretched, and no attention was paid to him, for no one knows how such people live.
Les Miserables	this autumn father champmathieu was arrested for stealing cider apples: there was a robbery, a wall climbed over, and branches broken.
Les Miserables	this champmathieu was arrested with the branch still in his hand, and was locked up.
Les Miserables	up to this point it is only a matter for a police court, but here providence interposes.
Les Miserables	as the lock-up was under repair, the magistrates ordered that champmathieu should be taken to the departmental prison at arras.
Les Miserables	in this prison there is an ex-convict of the name of brevet, under imprisonment for some offence, and he has been made room-turnkey for his good behavior.
Les Miserables	champmathieu no sooner arrived than brevet cries out, "why, i know this man: he is an ex-convict.
Les Miserables	look at me, old fellow: you are jean valjean."
Les Miserables	"what do you mean?"
Les Miserables	says champmathieu, affecting surprise.
Les Miserables	"don't play the humbug with me," says brevet; "you are jean valjean.
Les Miserables	you were at the toulon bagne twenty years ago, and i was there too."
Les Miserables	champmathieu denied identity, and, as you may suppose, the affair was thoroughly investigated, with the following result.
Les Miserables	this champmathieu about thirty years ago was a journeyman wood-cutter at several places, especially at faverolles, where his trail is lost.
Les Miserables	a long time after he is found again in auvergne, and then in paris, where he says he was a blacksmith, and had a daughter a washer-woman,--though there is no evidence of this,--and lastly, he turned up in these parts.
Les Miserables	now, before being sent to the galleys, what was jean valjean?
Les Miserables	a wood-cutter.
Les Miserables	where?
Les Miserables	at faverolles.
Les Miserables	and here is another fact: this valjean's christian name was jean, and his mother's family name mathieu.
Les Miserables	what is more natural to suppose than that on leaving the bagne he assumed his mother's name as a disguise, and called himself jean mathieu?
Les Miserables	he went to auvergne, where jean is pronounced chan, and thus he was transformed into champmathieu.
Les Miserables	you are following me, i suppose?
Les Miserables	inquiries have been made at faverolles, but jean valjean's family is no longer there, and no one knows where it has gone.
Les Miserables	as you are aware, in those places families frequently disappear in such a way; these people, if they are not mud, are dust.
Les Miserables	and then, again, as the beginning of this story dates back thirty years, there is no one in faverolles who knew jean valjean: and beside brevet, there are only two convicts who remember him.
Les Miserables	these two were brought from the bagne and confronted with the pretended champmathieu, and they did not hesitate for a moment.
Les Miserables	the same age,-- fifty-four,--the same height, the same look, the same man, in short.
Les Miserables	it was at this very moment that i sent my denunciation to paris, and the answer i received was that i had lost my senses, for jean valjean was in the hands of justice at arras.
Les Miserables	you can conceive that this surprised me, as i fancied that i held my jean valjean here.
Les Miserables	i wrote to the magistrates, who sent for me, and champmathieu was brought in."
Les Miserables	"well?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine interrupted him.
Les Miserables	javert answered with his incorruptible and sad face,--  "monsieur le maire, truth is truth: i am sorry, but that man is jean valjean: i recognized him too."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine said in a very low voice,--  "are you sure?"
Les Miserables	javert burst into that sorrowful laugh which escapes from a profound conviction,--  "oh!
Les Miserables	certain."
Les Miserables	he stood for a moment pensive, mechanically taking pinches of saw-dust out of the sprinkler in the inkstand, and added,--  "and now that i have seen the real jean valjean, i cannot understand how i could have believed anything else.
Les Miserables	i ask your pardon, m. le maire."
Les Miserables	while addressing these supplicating words to the person who six weeks previously had humiliated him so deeply and bidden him leave the room, this haughty man was unconsciously full of dignity and simplicity.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine merely answered his entreaty with the hurried question,--  "and what does this man say?"
Les Miserables	"well, monsieur le maire, it is an ugly business, for if he is jean valjean, he is an escaped convict.
Les Miserables	scaling a wall, breaking a branch, and stealing apples is a peccadillo with a child, an offence in a man, but a crime in a convict.
Les Miserables	it is no longer a matter for the police courts, but for the assizes; it is no longer imprisonment for a few days, but the galleys for life.
Les Miserables	and there is the matter with the savoyard, which, i trust, will be brought up again.
Les Miserables	there is enough to settle a man, is there not?
Les Miserables	but jean valjean is artful, and in that i recognize him too.
Les Miserables	any other man would find it warm; he would struggle, cry out, refuse to be jean valjean, and so on.
Les Miserables	he pretends though not to understand, and says, "i am champmathieu, and i shall stick to it."
Les Miserables	he has a look of amazement, and plays the brute-beast, which is better.
Les Miserables	oh!
Les Miserables	he is a clever scoundrel!
Les Miserables	but no matter, the proofs are ready to hand; he has been recognized by four persons, and the old scoundrel will be found guilty.
Les Miserables	he is to be tried at arras assizes, and i have been summoned as a witness."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine had turned round to his desk again, taken up his papers, and was quietly turning over the leaves, and busily reading and writing in turn.
Les Miserables	he now said to the inspector,--  "enough, javert; after all, these details interest me but very slightly; we are losing our time, and have a deal of work before us.
Les Miserables	javert, you will go at one to mother busaupied, who sells vegetables at the corner of the rue st. saulve, and tell her to take out a summons against pierre the carter; he is a brutal fellow, who almost drove over this woman and her child, and he must be punished.
Les Miserables	you will then go to m. charcillay in the rue champigny; he complains that there is a gutter next door which leaks, and is shaking the foundation of his house.
Les Miserables	but i am giving you a deal to do, and i think you said you were going away.
Les Miserables	did you not state you were going to arras on this matter in a week or ten days?"
Les Miserables	"sooner than that, sir."
Les Miserables	"on what day, then?"
Les Miserables	"i fancied i told you that the trial comes off to-morrow, and that i should start by to-night's coach."
Les Miserables	"and how long will the trial last?"
Les Miserables	"a day at the most, and sentence will be passed to-morrow night at the latest.
Les Miserables	but i shall not wait for that, but return so soon as i have given my evidence."
Les Miserables	"very good," said m. madeleine; and he dismissed javert with a wave of his hand.
Les Miserables	but he did not go.
Les Miserables	"i beg your pardon, m. le maire," he said.
Les Miserables	"what's the matter now?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine asked.
Les Miserables	"i have one thing to remind you of, sir."
Les Miserables	"what is it?"
Les Miserables	"that i must be discharged."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine rose.
Les Miserables	"javert, you are a man of honor, and i esteem you; you exaggerate your fault, and besides, it is another insult which concerns me.
Les Miserables	javert, you are worthy of rising, not of sinking, and i insist on your keeping your situation."
Les Miserables	javert looked at m. madeleine with his bright eyes, in which it seemed as if his unenlightened but rigid and chaste conscience could be seen, and he said quietly,--  "m. le maire, i cannot allow it."
Les Miserables	"i repeat," m. madeleine replied, "that the affair concerns myself."
Les Miserables	but javert, only attending to his own thoughts, continued,--  "as for exaggerating, i am not doing so, for this is how i reason.
Les Miserables	i suspected you unjustly; that is nothing: it is the duty of men like myself to suspect, though there is an abuse in suspecting those above us.
Les Miserables	but, without proofs, in a moment of passion and for the purpose of revenge, i denounced you, a respectable man, a mayor and a magistrate; this is serious, very serious,--i, an agent of the authority, insulted that authority in your person.
Les Miserables	had any of my subordinates done what i have done, i should have declared him unworthy of the service and discharged him.
Les Miserables	stay, monsieur le maire, one word more.
Les Miserables	i have often been severe in my life to others, for it was just, and i was doing my duty, and if i were not severe to myself now, all the justice i have done would become injustice.
Les Miserables	ought i to spare myself more than others?
Les Miserables	no.
Les Miserables	what!
Les Miserables	i have been only good to punish others and not myself?
Les Miserables	why, i should be a scoundrel, and the people who call me that rogue of a javert, would be in the right!
Les Miserables	m. le maire, i do not wish you to treat me with kindness, for your kindness caused me sufficient ill-blood when dealt to others, and i want none for myself.
Les Miserables	the kindness that consists in defending the street-walker against the gentleman, the police agent against the mayor, the lower classes against the higher, is what i call bad kindness, and it is such kindness that disorganizes society.
Les Miserables	good lord!
Les Miserables	it is easy enough to be good, but the difficulty is to be just.
Les Miserables	come!
Les Miserables	if you had been what i believed you, i should not have been kind to you, as you would have seen.
Les Miserables	m. le maire, i am bound to treat myself as i would treat another man; when i repressed malefactors, when i was severe with scamps, i often said to myself, "if you ever catch yourself tripping, look out," i have tripped, i have committed a fault, and all the worse for me.
Les Miserables	i have strong arms and will turn laborer.
Les Miserables	m. le maire, the good of the service requires an example.
Les Miserables	i simply demand the discharge of inspector javert."
Les Miserables	all this was said with a humble, proud, despairing, and convinced accent, which gave a peculiar grandeur to this strangely honest man.
Les Miserables	"we will see," said m. madeleine, and he offered him his hand; but javert fell back, and said sternly,--  "pardon me, sir, but that must not be; a mayor ought not to give his hand to a spy."
Les Miserables	he added between his teeth,--  "yes, a spy; from the moment when i misused my authority, i have been only a spy."
Les Miserables	then he bowed deeply and walked to the door.
Les Miserables	when he reached it he turned round and said, with eyes still bent on the ground,--  "m. le maire, i will continue on duty till my place is filled up."
Les Miserables	he went out.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine thoughtfully listened to his firm, sure step as he walked along the paved passage.
Les Miserables	book vii   the champmathieu affair.
Les Miserables	chapter i.   sister simplice.
Les Miserables	the incidents we are about to record were only partially known at m----, but the few which were known left such a memory in that town, that it would be a serious gap in this book if we did not tell them in their smallest details.
Les Miserables	in these details the reader will notice two or three improbable circumstances, which we retain through respect for truth.
Les Miserables	in the afternoon that followed javert's visit, m. madeleine went to see fantine as usual; but before going to her, he asked for sister simplice.
Les Miserables	the two nuns who managed the infirmary, who were lazarets, like all sisters of charity, were known by the names of sisters perpetua and simplice.
Les Miserables	sister perpetua was an ordinary village girl, a clumsy sister of charity, who had entered the service of heaven just as she would have taken a cook's place.
Les Miserables	this type is not rare, for the monastic orders gladly accept this clumsy peasant clay, which can be easily fashioned into a capuchin friar or an ursuline nun; and these rusticities are employed in the heavy work of devotion.
Les Miserables	the transition from a drover to a carmelite is no hard task; the common substratum of village and cloister ignorance is a ready-made preparation, and at once places the countryman on a level with the monk.
Les Miserables	widen the blouse a little and you have a gown.
Les Miserables	sister perpetua was a strong nun belonging to marnies near pantoise, who talked with a country accent, sang psalms to match, sugared the _tisane_ according to the bigotry or hypocrisy of the patient, was rough with the sick, and harsh with the dying, almost throwing god in their faces, and storming their last moments with angry prayer.
Les Miserables	withal she was bold, honest, and red-faced.
Les Miserables	sister simplice was pale, and looked like a wax taper by the side of sister perpetua, who was a tallow candle in comparison.
Les Miserables	st. vincent de paul has divinely described the sister of charity in those admirable words in which so much liberty is blended with slavery: "they will have no other convent but the hospital, no other cell but a hired room, no chapel but the parish church, no cloister beyond the streets or the hospital wards, no walls but obedience, no grating but the fear of god, and no veil but modesty."
Les Miserables	sister simplice was the living ideal of this: no one could have told her age, for she had never been young, and seemed as if she would never grow old.
Les Miserables	she was a gentle, austere, well-nurtured, cold person--we dare not say a woman--who had never told a falsehood; she was so gentle that she appeared fragile, but she was more solid than granite.
Les Miserables	she touched the wretched with her delicate and pure fingers.
Les Miserables	there was, so to speak, silence in her language; she only said what was necessary, and possessed an intonation of voice which would at once have edified a confessional and delighted a drawing-room.
Les Miserables	this delicacy harmonized with the rough gown, for it formed in this rough contact a continual reminder of heaven.
Les Miserables	let us dwell on one detail; never to have told a falsehood, never to have said, for any advantage or even indifferently, a thing which was not the truth, the holy truth, was the characteristic feature of sister simplice.
Les Miserables	she was almost celebrated in the congregation for this imperturbable veracity, and the abbé suard alludes to sister simplice in a letter to the deaf, mute massieu.
Les Miserables	however sincere and pure we may be, we have all the brand of a little white lie on our candor, but she had not.
Les Miserables	can there be such a thing as a white lie, an innocent lie?
Les Miserables	lying is the absolute of evil.
Les Miserables	lying a little is not possible; the man who lies tells the whole lie; lying is the face of the fiend, and satan has two names,--he is called satan and lying.
Les Miserables	that is what she thought, and she practised as she thought.
Les Miserables	the result was the whiteness to which we have alluded, a whiteness which even covered with its radiance her lips and eyes, for her smile was white, her glance was white.
Les Miserables	there was not a spider's web nor a grain of dust on the window of this conscience; on entering the obedience of st. vincent de paul she took the name of simplice through special choice.
Les Miserables	simplice of sicily, our readers will remember, is the saint who sooner let her bosom be plucked out than say she was a native of segeste, as she was born at syracuse, though the falsehood would have saved her.
Les Miserables	such a patron saint suited this soul.
Les Miserables	simplice on entering the order had two faults, of which she had gradually corrected herself; she had a taste for dainties and was fond of receiving letters.
Les Miserables	now she never read anything but a prayer-book in large type and in latin; though she did not understand the language, she understood the book.
Les Miserables	this pious woman felt an affection for fantine, as she probably noticed the latent virtue in her, and nearly entirely devoted herself to nursing her.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine took sister simplice on one side and recommended fantine to her with a singular accent, which the sister remembered afterwards.
Les Miserables	on leaving the sister he went to fantine.
Les Miserables	the patient daily awaited the appearance of m. madeleine, as if he brought her warmth and light; she said to the sisters, "i only live when m. le maire is here."
Les Miserables	this day she was very feverish, and so soon as she saw m. madeleine she asked him,--  "where is cosette?"
Les Miserables	he replied with a smile, "she will be here soon."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine behaved to fantine as usual, except that he remained with her an hour instead of half an hour, to her great delight.
Les Miserables	he pressed everybody not to allow the patient to want for anything, and it was noticed at one moment that his face became very dark, but this was explained when it was learned that the physician had bent down to his ear and said, "she is rapidly sinking."
Les Miserables	then he returned to the mayoralty, and the office clerk saw him attentively examining a road-map of france which hung in his room, and write a few figures in pencil on a piece of paper.
Les Miserables	chapter ii   scaufflaire's perspicacity.
Les Miserables	from the mayoralty m. madeleine proceeded to the end of the town, to a fleming called master scaufflaer, gallicized into scaufflaire, who let out horses and gigs by the day.
Les Miserables	to reach his yard the nearest way was through an unfrequented street, in which stood the house of the parish priest.
Les Miserables	the curé was said to be a worthy and respectable man, who gave good advice.
Les Miserables	at the moment when m. madeleine came in front of his house there was only one person in the street, and he noticed the following circumstances: m. le maire, after passing the house, stopped for a moment, then turned back and walked up to the curé's door, which had an iron knocker.
Les Miserables	he quickly seized the knocker and lifted it; then he stopped again as if in deep thought, and, after a few seconds, instead of knocking, he softly let the knocker fall back in its place and continued his way with a haste which he had not displayed before.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine found master scaufflaire at home and engaged in mending a set of harness.
Les Miserables	"master scaufflaire", he inquired, "have you a good horse?"
Les Miserables	"m. le maire," the fleming replied, "all my horses are good.
Les Miserables	what do you mean by a good horse?"
Les Miserables	"i mean a horse that can cover twenty leagues of ground in a day."
Les Miserables	"harnessed in a gig?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"and how long will it rest after the journey?"
Les Miserables	"it must be in a condition to start again the next morning if necessary."
Les Miserables	"to return the same distance?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"hang it all!
Les Miserables	and it is twenty leagues?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine took from his pocket the paper on which he had pencilled the figures; they were "5, 6, 8 1/2."
Les Miserables	"you see," he said, "total, nineteen and a half, or call them twenty leagues."
Les Miserables	"m. le maire," the fleming continued, "i can suit you.
Les Miserables	my little white horse--you may have seen it pass sometimes--is an animal from the bas boulonnais, and full of fire.
Les Miserables	they tried at first to make a saddle-horse of it, but it reared and threw everybody that got on its back.
Les Miserables	it was supposed to be vicious, and they did not know what to do with it; i bought it and put it in a gig.
Les Miserables	that was just what it wanted; it is as gentle as a maid and goes like the wind.
Les Miserables	but you must not try to get on its back, for it has no notion of being a saddle-horse.
Les Miserables	everybody has his ambition, and it appears as if the horse had said to itself,--draw, yes; carry, no."
Les Miserables	"and it will go the distance?"
Les Miserables	"at a trot, and under eight hours, but on certain conditions."
Les Miserables	"what are they?"
Les Miserables	"in the first place, you will let it breathe for an hour half way; it will feed, and you must be present while it is doing so, to prevent the ostler stealing the oats, for i have noticed that at inns oats are more frequently drunk by the stable-boys than eaten by the horses."
Les Miserables	"i will be there."
Les Miserables	"in the next place, is the gig for yourself, sir?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"do you know how to drive?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"well, you must travel alone and without luggage, in order not to overweight the horse."
Les Miserables	"agreed."
Les Miserables	"i shall expect thirty francs a day, and the days of rest paid for as well,--not a farthing less; and you will pay for the horse's keep."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine took three napoleons from his purse and laid them on the table.
Les Miserables	"there are two days in advance."
Les Miserables	"in the fourth place, a cabriolet would be too heavy for such a journey, and tire the horse.
Les Miserables	you must oblige me by travelling in a little tilbury i have."
Les Miserables	"i consent."
Les Miserables	"it is light, but it is open."
Les Miserables	"i do not care."
Les Miserables	"have you thought, sir, that it is now winter?"
Les Miserables	m. madeleine made no answer, and the fleming continued,--  "that it is very cold?"
Les Miserables	monsieur madeleine was still silent.
Les Miserables	"that it may rain?"
Les Miserables	the mayor raised his head and said,--  "the tilbury and the horse will be before my door at half-past four to-morrow morning."
Les Miserables	"very good, sir," scaufflaire answered; then scratching with his thumb-nail a stain in the wood of his table, he continued, with that careless air with which the flemings so cleverly conceal their craft,--  "good gracious!
Les Miserables	i have not thought of asking where you are going?
Les Miserables	be kind enough to tell me, sir."
Les Miserables	he had thought of nothing else since the beginning of the conversation, but somehow he had not dared to ask the question.
Les Miserables	"has your horse good legs?"
Les Miserables	said m. madeleine.
Les Miserables	"yes, m. le maire; you will hold it up a little in going down-hill.
Les Miserables	are there many hills between here and the place you are going to?"
Les Miserables	"do not forget to be at my door at half-past four exactly," m. madeleine answered, and went away.
Les Miserables	the fleming stood "like a fool," as he said himself a little while after.
Les Miserables	m. le maire had been gone some two or three minutes when the door opened again; it was m. le maire.
Les Miserables	he still wore the same impassive and preoccupied air.
Les Miserables	"m. scaufflaire," he said, "at how much do you value the tilbury and horse you are going to let me, one with the other?"
Les Miserables	"do you wish to buy them of me, sir?"
Les Miserables	"no, but i should like to guarantee them against any accident, and when i come back you can return me the amount.
Les Miserables	what is the estimated value?"
Les Miserables	"five hundred francs, m. le maire."
Les Miserables	"here they are."
Les Miserables	m. madeleine laid a bank note on the table, then went out, and this time did not come back.
Les Miserables	master scaufflaire regretted frightfully that he had not said a thousand francs, though tilbury and horse, at a fair valuation, were worth just three hundred.
Les Miserables	the fleming called his wife and told her what had occurred.
Les Miserables	"where the deuce can the mayor be going?"
Les Miserables	they held a council.
Les Miserables	"he is going to paris," said the wife.
Les Miserables	"i don't believe it," said the husband.
Les Miserables	m. madeleine had left on the table the paper on which he had written the figures; the fleming took it up and examined it.
Les Miserables	"'5, 6, 8 1/2;' why, that must mean post stations."
Les Miserables	he turned to his wife: "i have found it out."
Les Miserables	"how?"
Les Miserables	"it is five leagues from here to hesdin, six from there to st. pol, and eight and a half from st. pol to arras.
Les Miserables	he is going to arras."
Les Miserables	in the mean while the mayor had returned home, and had taken the longest road, as if the gate of the priest's house were a temptation to him which he wished to avoid.
Les Miserables	he went up to his bed-room and locked himself in, which was not unusual, for he was fond of going to bed at an early hour.
Les Miserables	still the factory portress, who was at the same time m. madeleine's only servant, remarked that his candle was extinguished at a quarter-past eight, and mentioned the fact to the cashier when he came in, adding,--  "can master be ill?
Les Miserables	i thought he looked very strange to-day."
Les Miserables	the cashier occupied a room exactly under m. madeleine's; he paid no attention to the remarks of the portress, but went to bed and fell asleep.
Les Miserables	about midnight he woke with a start, for he heard in his sleep a noise above his head.
Les Miserables	he listened; it was a footfall coming and going, as if some one were walking about the room above him.
Les Miserables	he listened more attentively, and recognized m. madeleine's step; and this seemed to him strange, for usually no sound could be heard from the mayor's room till he rose.
Les Miserables	a moment later the cashier heard something like a wardrobe open and shut; a piece of furniture was moved, there was a silence, and the walking began again.
Les Miserables	the cashier sat up in bed, wide awake, looked out, and through his window noticed on a wall opposite, the red reflection of a lighted window; from the direction of the rays it could only be the window of m. madeleine's bed-room.
Les Miserables	the reflection flickered as if it came from a fire rather than a candle, while the shadow of the framework could not be traced, which proved that the window was wide open, and this was a curious fact, considering the cold.
Les Miserables	the cashier fell asleep and woke again some two hours after; the same slow and regular footfall was still audible above his head.
Les Miserables	the reflection was still cast on the wall, but was now pale and quiet, as if it came from a lamp or a candle.
Les Miserables	the window was still open.
Les Miserables	this is what was occurring in m. madeleine's bed-room.
Les Miserables	chapter iii.
Les Miserables	a tempest in a brain.
Les Miserables	the reader has, of course, guessed that m. madeleine is jean valjean.
Les Miserables	we have already looked into the depths of this conscience, and the moment has arrived to look into them again.
Les Miserables	we do not do this without emotion or tremor, for there is nothing more terrifying than this species of contemplation.
Les Miserables	the mental eye can nowhere find greater brilliancy or greater darkness than within man; it cannot dwell on anything which is more formidable, complicated, mysterious, or infinite.
Les Miserables	there is a spectacle grander than the sea, and that is the sky; there is a spectacle grander than the sky, and it is the interior of the soul.
Les Miserables	to write the poem of the human conscience, were the subject only one man, and he the lowest of men, would be to resolve all epic poems into one supreme and final epic.
Les Miserables	conscience is the chaos of chimeras, envies, and attempts, the furnace of dreams, the lurking-place of ideas we are ashamed of; it is the pandemonium of sophistry, the battlefield of the passions.
Les Miserables	at certain hours look through the livid face of a reflecting man, look into his soul, peer into the darkness.
Les Miserables	beneath the external silence, combats of giants are going on there, such as we read of in homer; _mêlées_ of dragons and hydras and clouds of phantoms, such as we find in milton; and visionary spirals, as in dante.
Les Miserables	a sombre thing is the infinitude which every man bears within him, and by which he desperately measures the volitions of his brain and the actions of his life.
Les Miserables	alighieri one day came to a gloomy gate, before which he hesitated; we have one before us, on the threshold of which we also hesitate, but we will enter.
Les Miserables	we have but little to add to what the reader already knows as having happened to jean valjean since his adventure with little gervais.
Les Miserables	from this moment, as we have seen, he became another man, and he made himself what the bishop wished to make him.
Les Miserables	it was more than a transformation, it was a transfiguration.
Les Miserables	he succeeded in disappearing, sold the bishop's plate, only keeping the candlesticks as a souvenir, passed through france, reached m----, had the idea we have described, accomplished what we have narrated, managed to make himself unseizable and inaccessible, and henceforth settled at m----, happy at feeling his conscience saddened by the past, and the first half of his existence contradicted by the last half; he lived peacefully, reassured and trusting, and having but two thoughts,--to hide his name and sanctify his life; escape from men and return to god.
Les Miserables	these two thoughts were so closely blended in his mind, that they only formed one; they were both equally absorbing and imperious, and governed his slightest actions.
Les Miserables	usually they agreed to regulate the conduct of his life; they turned him toward the shadow; they rendered him beneficent and simple, and they counselled him the same things.
Les Miserables	at times, however, there was a conflict between them, and in such cases the man whom the whole town of m---- called monsieur madeleine did not hesitate to sacrifice the first to the second,--his security to his virtue.
Les Miserables	hence, despite all his caution and prudence, he had kept the bishop's candlesticks, worn mourning for him, questioned all the little savoyards who passed through the town, inquired after the family at faverolles, and saved the life of old fauchelevent, in spite of the alarming insinuations of javert.
Les Miserables	it seemed, as we have already remarked, that he thought, after the example of all those who have been wise, holy, and just, that his first duty was not toward himself.
Les Miserables	still, we are bound to say, nothing like the present had before occurred; never had the two ideas which governed the unhappy man whose sufferings we are describing, entered upon so serious a struggle.
Les Miserables	he comprehended confusedly, but deeply, from the first words which javert uttered on entering his study.
Les Miserables	at the moment when the name which he had buried so deeply was so strangely pronounced, he was struck with stupor, and, as it were, intoxicated by the sinister peculiarity of his destiny.
Les Miserables	and through this stupor he felt that quivering which precedes great storms; he bowed like an oak at the approach of a storm, like a soldier before a coming assault.
Les Miserables	he felt the shadows full of thunder and lightning collecting over his head: while listening to javert he had a thought of running off, denouncing himself, taking champmathieu out of prison, and taking his place.
Les Miserables	this was painful, like an incision in the flesh; but it passed away, and he said to himself, "we will see!"
Les Miserables	he repressed this first generous movement, and recoiled before his heroism.
Les Miserables	it would doubtless be grand if, after the bishop's holy remarks, after so many years of repentance and self-denial, in the midst of a penitence so admirably commenced, this man, even in the presence of such a terrible conjuncture, had not failed for a moment, but continued to march at the same pace toward this open abyss, at the bottom of which heaven was: this would be grand, but it did not take place.
Les Miserables	we are bound to describe all the things that took place in this mind, and cannot say that this was one of them.
Les Miserables	what carried him away first was the instinct of self-preservation.
Les Miserables	he hastily collected his ideas, stifled his emotion, deferred any resolution with the firmness of terror, deadened himself against what he had to do, and resumed his calmness as a gladiator puts up his buckler.
Les Miserables	for the remainder of the day he was in the same state,--a hurricane within, a deep tranquillity outside,--and he only took what may be called "conservative measures."
Les Miserables	all was still confused and jumbled in his brain; the trouble in it was so great that he did not see distinctly the outline of any idea, and he could have said nothing about himself, save that he had received a heavy blow.
Les Miserables	he went as usual to fantine's bed of pain, and prolonged his visit, with a kindly instinct, saying to himself that he must act thus, and recommend her to the sisters in the event of his being obliged to go away.
Les Miserables	he felt vaguely that he must perhaps go to arras; and, though not the least in the world decided about the journey, he said to himself that, safe from suspicion as he was, there would be no harm in being witness of what might take place, and he hired scaufflaire's tilbury, in order to be ready for any event.
Les Miserables	he dined with considerable appetite, and, on returning to his bed-room, reflected.
Les Miserables	he examined his situation, and found it extraordinary,--so extraordinary that, in the midst of his reverie, through some almost inexplicable impulse of anxiety, he rose from his chair and bolted his door.
Les Miserables	he was afraid lest something might enter, and he barricaded himself against the possible.
Les Miserables	a moment after, he blew out his light, for it annoyed him, and he fancied that he might be overseen.
Les Miserables	by whom?
Les Miserables	alas!
Les Miserables	what he wanted to keep out had entered; what he wished to blind was looking at him.
Les Miserables	it was his conscience, that is to say, god.
Les Miserables	still, at the first moment, he deceived himself; he had a feeling of security and solitude.
Les Miserables	when he put in the bolt, he thought himself impregnable; when the candle was out, he felt himself invisible.
Les Miserables	he then regained his self-possession; and he put his elbows on the table, leaned his head on his hand, and began dreaming in the darkness.
Les Miserables	"where am i?
Les Miserables	am i not dreaming?
Les Miserables	what was i told?
Les Miserables	is it really true that i saw that javert, and that he spoke to me so?
Les Miserables	who can this champmathieu be?
Les Miserables	it seems he resembles me.
Les Miserables	is it possible?
Les Miserables	when i think that i was so tranquil yesterday, and so far from suspecting anything!
Les Miserables	what was i doing yesterday at this hour?
Les Miserables	what will be the result of this event?
Les Miserables	what am i to do?"
Les Miserables	such was the trouble he was in that his brain had not the strength to retain ideas.
Les Miserables	they passed like waves, and he clutched his forehead with both hands to stop them.
Les Miserables	from this tumult which overthrew his wits and reason, and from which he sought to draw an evidence and a resolution, nothing issued but agony.
Les Miserables	his head was burning; and he went by the window and threw it wide open.
Les Miserables	there were no stars in the heavens, and he went back to the table and sat down by it.
Les Miserables	the first hour passed away thus, but gradually vague features began to shape themselves, and become fixed in his thoughts, and he could observe with the precision of reality some details of the situation, if not its entirety.
Les Miserables	he began by noticing that however critical and extraordinary his situation might be, he was utterly the master of it, and his stupor was only augmented.
Les Miserables	independently of the stern and religious object he proposed to himself in his actions, all that he had done up to this day was only a hole he dug in which to bury his name.
Les Miserables	what he had always most feared, in his hours of reflection as in his sleepless nights, was ever to hear _that_ name pronounced.
Les Miserables	he said to himself that this would be to him the end of everything; that on the day when that name re-appeared, it would cause his new life to fade away, and possibly the new soul he had within him.
Les Miserables	he shuddered at the mere thought that this could happen.
Les Miserables	assuredly if any one had told him at such moments that the hour would arrive in which this name would echo in his ear, when the hideous name of jean valjean would suddenly emerge from the night and rise before him, when this formidable light which dissipated the mystery with which he surrounded himself would suddenly shine above his head, and that the name would no longer menace him; that the light would produce only a denser gloom; that this rent veil would increase the mystery; that the earthquake would consolidate his edifice; that this prodigious incident would have no other result, if he thought proper, but to render his existence clearer and yet more impenetrable, and that from his confrontation with the phantom of jean valjean, the good and worthy m. madeleine would come forth more honored, more peaceful, and more respected than ever,--if any one had told him this, he would have shaken his head, and considered such talk insane.
Les Miserables	and yet all this had really happened, and this heap of impossibilities was a fact, and heaven had permitted all these wild things to become real.
Les Miserables	his reverie continued to grow clearer, and each moment he comprehended his position better.
Les Miserables	it seemed to him that he had just awakened from a dream, and that he was descending an incline in the middle of the night, shuddering and recoiling in vain from the brink of an abyss.
Les Miserables	he distinctly saw in the shadows an unknown man, a stranger, whom destiny took for him, and thrust into the gulf in his place.
Les Miserables	in order that the gulf should close, either he or another must fall in.
Les Miserables	he had no necessity to do anything, the clearness became complete, and he confessed to himself--that his place was vacant at the galleys; that, whatever he might do, it constantly expected him, that the robbery of little gervais led him back to it, that this vacant place would wait for him and attract him until he filled it, and that this was inevitable and fatal.
Les Miserables	and then he said to himself that at this moment he had a substitute,--that it seemed a man of the name of champmathieu had this ill-luck; and that, in future, himself at the bagne in the person of this champmathieu, and present in society under the name of m. madeleine, would have nothing more to fear, provided that he did not prevent justice from laying over the head of this champmathieu the stone of infamy which, like the tombstone, falls once and is never raised again.
Les Miserables	all this was so violent and so strange, that he suddenly felt within him that species of indescribable movement which no man experiences more than twice or thrice in his life,--a sort of convulsion of the conscience, which disturbs everything doubtful in the heart, which is composed of irony, joy, and despair, and what might be called an internal burst of laughter.
Les Miserables	he suddenly relit his candle.
Les Miserables	"well, what am i afraid of?"
Les Miserables	he said to himself; "what reason have i to have such thoughts?
Les Miserables	i am saved, and all is settled.
Les Miserables	there was only one open door through which my past could burst in upon my life: and that door is now walled up forever.
Les Miserables	that javert, who has so long annoyed me, the formidable instinct which seemed to have scented me, and by heavens!
Les Miserables	had scented me, the frightful dog ever making a point at me, is routed, engaged elsewhere, and absolutely thrown out!
Les Miserables	he is henceforth satisfied, he will leave me at peace, for he has got his jean valjean!
Les Miserables	it is possible that he may wish to leave the town too.
Les Miserables	and all this has taken place without my interference, and so, what is there so unlucky in it all?
Les Miserables	on my word, any people who saw me would believe that a catastrophe had befallen me.
Les Miserables	after all, if some people are rendered unhappy, it is no fault of mine.
Les Miserables	providence has done it all, and apparently decrees it.
Les Miserables	have i the right to derange what he arranges?
Les Miserables	what is it that i am going to interfere in?
Les Miserables	it does not concern me.
Les Miserables	what!
Les Miserables	i am not satisfied?
Les Miserables	why!
Les Miserables	what else can i want?
Les Miserables	i have attained the object to which i have been aspiring for so many years, the dream of my nights, the matter of my prayers,--security.
Les Miserables	it is heaven that wills it, and i have done nothing contrary to god's desire.
Les Miserables	and why has heaven decreed it?
Les Miserables	that i may continue what i have begun; that i may do good; that i may one day be a grand and encouraging example; that it may be said that there is after all a little happiness attaching to the penance i have undergone.
Les Miserables	i really cannot understand why i was so afraid just now about visiting that worthy curé, telling all to him as to a confessor, and asking his advice, for this is certainly what he would have advised me.
Les Miserables	it is settled; i will let matters take their course, and leave the decision to heaven."
Les Miserables	he spoke this in the depths of his conscience, while leaning over what might be called his own abyss.
Les Miserables	he got up from his chair and walked about the room.
Les Miserables	"come," he said, "i will think no more of it; i have made up my mind;" but he felt no joy.
Les Miserables	it is no more possible to prevent thought from reverting to an idea than the sea from returning to the shore.
Les Miserables	with the sailor this is called the tide, with the culprit it is called remorse; god heaves the soul like the ocean.
Les Miserables	after a few moments, whatever he might do, he resumed the gloomy dialogue in which it was he who spoke and he who listened, saying what he wished to be silent about, listening to what he did not desire to hear, and yielding to that mysterious power which said to him "think," as it did, two thousand years ago, to another condemned man, "go on."
Les Miserables	before going further, and in order to be fully understood, let us dwell on a necessary observation.
Les Miserables	it is certain that men talk to themselves; and there is not a thinking being who has not realized the fact.
Les Miserables	it is only in this sense that the words frequently employed in this chapter, _he said, he exclaimed,_ must be understood; men talk to themselves, speak to themselves, cry out within themselves, but the external silence is not interrupted.
Les Miserables	there is a grand tumult; everything speaks to us, excepting the mouth.
Les Miserables	the realities of the soul, for all that they are not visible and palpable, are not the less realities.
Les Miserables	he asked himself then, what he had arrived at, and cross-questioned himself about the resolution he had formed.
Les Miserables	he confessed to himself that all he had arranged in his mind was monstrous, and that leaving "god to act" was simply horrible.
Les Miserables	to allow this mistake of destiny and of men to be accomplished, not to prevent it, to lend himself to it, do nothing, in short, was to do everything; it was the last stage of hypocritical indignity!
Les Miserables	it was a low, cowardly, cunning, abject, hideous crime.
Les Miserables	for the first time during eight years this hapless man had the taste of a bad thought and a bad action, and he spat it out in disgust.
Les Miserables	he continued to cross-question himself.
Les Miserables	he asked himself what he had meant by the words, "my object is attained"?
Les Miserables	he allowed that his life had an object, but what was its nature?--conceal his name!
Les Miserables	deceive the police.
Les Miserables	was it for so paltry a thing that he had done all that he had effected?
Les Miserables	had he not another object which was the great and true one,--to save not his person, but his soul; to become once again honest and good?
Les Miserables	to be a just man!
Les Miserables	was it not that he craved solely, and that the bishop had ordered him?
Les Miserables	close the door on his past?
Les Miserables	but, great heaven, he opened it again by committing an infamous action.
Les Miserables	he was becoming a robber once more, and the most odious of robbers!
Les Miserables	he was robbing another man of his existence, his livelihood, his peace, and his place in the sunshine.
Les Miserables	he was becoming an assassin, he was killing, morally killing, a wretched man; he was inflicting on him the frightful living death, the open-air death, which is called the galleys.
Les Miserables	on the other hand, if he gave himself up, freed this man who was suffering from so grievous an error, resumed his name, became through duty the convict jean valjean, that would be really completing his resurrection, and eternally closing the hell from which he was emerging!
Les Miserables	falling back into it apparently would be leaving it in reality!
Les Miserables	he must do this: he would have done nothing unless he did this; all his life would be useless, all his penitence wasted.
Les Miserables	he felt that the bishop was here, that he was the more present because he was dead, that the bishop was steadfastly looking at him, and that henceforth madeleine the mayor would be an abomination to him, and jean valjean the convict admirable and pure in his sight.
Les Miserables	men saw his mask, but the bishop saw his face; men saw his life, but the bishop saw his conscience.
Les Miserables	he must consequently go to arras, deliver the false jean valjean, and denounce the true one.
Les Miserables	alas!
Les Miserables	this was the greatest of sacrifices, the most poignant of victories, the last step to take; but he must take it.
Les Miserables	frightful destiny his!
Les Miserables	he could not obtain sanctity in the sight of heaven unless he returned to infamy in the sight of man.
Les Miserables	"well," he said, "i will make up my mind to this.
Les Miserables	i will do my duty and save this man."
Les Miserables	he uttered those words aloud without noticing he had raised his voice.
Les Miserables	he fetched his books, verified and put them in order.
Les Miserables	he threw into the fire a number of claims he had upon embarrassed tradesmen, and wrote a letter, which he addressed "to m. lafitte, banker, rue d'artois, paris."
Les Miserables	he then took from his desk a pocket-book, which contained a few bank-notes and the passport he had employed just previously to go to the elections.
Les Miserables	any one who had seen him while he was accomplishing these various acts, with which such grave meditation was mingled, would not have suspected what was taking place in him.
Les Miserables	at moments his lips moved, at others he raised his head and looked at a part of the wall, as if there were something there which he desired to clear up or question.
Les Miserables	when the letter to m. lafitte was finished, he put it into his portfolio, and began his walk once more.
Les Miserables	his reverie had not deviated; he continued to see his duty clearly written in luminous letters which flashed before his eyes, and moved about with his glance, name yourself, denounce yourself!
Les Miserables	he could also see the two ideas which had hitherto been the double rule of his life--to hide his name and sanctify his life--moving before him as it were in a tangible shape.
Les Miserables	for the first time they seemed to him absolutely distinct, and he saw the difference that separated them.
Les Miserables	he recognized that one of these ideas was necessarily good, while the other might become bad; that the former was self-sacrifice, the latter selfishness; that one said, "my neighbor," the other "myself;" that one came from the light and the other from darkness.
Les Miserables	they strove with each other, and he could see them doing so.
Les Miserables	while he was thinking, they had grown before his mental eye, and they had now colossal forms, and he fancied he could see a god and a giant wrestling within him, in the infinitude to which we just now alluded, and in the midst of obscurity and flashes of light.
Les Miserables	it was a horrible sight, but it seemed to him as if the good thought gained the victory.
Les Miserables	he felt that he was approaching the second decisive moment of his life; that the bishop marked the first phase of his new life, and that this champmathieu marked the second; after the great crisis came the great trial.
Les Miserables	the fever, appeased for a moment, gradually returned, however.
Les Miserables	a thousand thoughts crossed his mind, but they continued to strengthen him in his resolution.
Les Miserables	at one moment he said to himself that he perhaps regarded the matter too seriously; that, after all, this champmathieu did not concern him, and in any case was a thief.
Les Miserables	he answered himself: if this man has really stolen apples, he will have a month's imprisonment, but that is a long way from the galleys.
Les Miserables	and then, again, is it proved that he has committed a robbery?
Les Miserables	the name of jean valjean is crushing him, and seems to dispense with proofs.
Les Miserables	do not public prosecutors habitually act in this way?
Les Miserables	a man is believed to be a thief because he is known to be a convict.
Les Miserables	at another moment the idea occurred to him that, when he had denounced himself, the heroism of his deed might perhaps be taken into consideration, as well as his life of honesty during the last seven years, and the good he had done the town, and that he would be pardoned.
Les Miserables	but this supposition soon vanished, and he smiled bitterly at the thought that the robbery of the 40 sous from gervais rendered him a relapsed convict; that this affair would certainly be brought forward, and, by the precise terms of the law, sentence him to the galleys for life.
Les Miserables	he turned away from all illusions, detached himself more and more from earth, and sought consolation and strength elsewhere.
Les Miserables	he said to himself that he must do his duty: that, perhaps, he would not be more wretched after doing it than he would have been had he eluded it: that, if he let matters take their course and remained at m----, his good name, good deeds, charity, wealth, popularity, and virtue would be tainted by a crime; and what flavor would all these sacred things have, when attached to this hideous thought; while, if he accomplished his sacrifice, he would mingle a heavenly idea with the galleys, the chain, the green cap, the unrelaxing toil, and the pitiless shame.
Les Miserables	at last he said to himself that it was a necessity, that his destiny was thus shaped, that he had no power to derange the arrangements of heaven, and that in any case he must choose either external virtue and internal abomination, or holiness within and infamy outside him.
Les Miserables	his courage did not fail him in revolving so many mournful ideas, but his brain grew weary.
Les Miserables	he began thinking involuntarily of other and indifferent matters.
Les Miserables	his arteries beat violently in his temples, and he was still walking up and down; midnight struck, first from the parish church, and then from the town hall: he counted the twelve strokes of the two clocks, and compared the sound of the two bells.
Les Miserables	they reminded him that a few days before he had seen an old bell at a marine store, on which was engraved the name antoine albier, romainville.
Les Miserables	as he felt cold, he lit a fire, but did not dream of closing the window.
Les Miserables	then he fell back into his stupor, obliged to make a mighty effort to remember what he had been thinking of before midnight struck.
Les Miserables	at last he succeeded.
Les Miserables	"ah, yes," he said to himself, "i had formed the resolution to denounce myself."
Les Miserables	and then he suddenly began thinking of fantine.
Les Miserables	"stay," he said; "and that poor woman!"
Les Miserables	here a fresh crisis broke out: fantine, suddenly appearing in the midst of his reverie, was like a ray of unexpected light.
Les Miserables	he fancied that all changed around him, and exclaimed,--  "wait a minute!
Les Miserables	hitherto, i have thought of myself and consulted my own convenience.
Les Miserables	whether it suits me to be silent or denounce myself--hide my person or save my soul--be a contemptible and respected magistrate, or an infamous and venerable convict--it is always self, nought but self.
Les Miserables	good heavens!
Les Miserables	all this is egotism; under different shapes, 't is true, but still egotism.
Les Miserables	suppose i were to think a little about others!
Les Miserables	it is the first duty of a christian to think of his neighbor.
Les Miserables	well, let me examine: when i am effaced and forgotten, what will become of all this?
Les Miserables	if i denounce myself, that champmathieu will be set at liberty.
Les Miserables	i shall be sent back to the galleys, and what then?
Les Miserables	what will occur here?
Les Miserables	here are a town, factories, a trade, work-people, men, women, old grandfathers, children, and poor people: i have created all this.
Les Miserables	i keep it all alive: wherever there is a chimney smoking, i placed the brand in the fire and the meat in the pot: i have produced easy circumstances, circulation, and credit.
Les Miserables	before i came there was nothing of all this; i revived, animated, fertilized, stimulated, and enriched the whole district.
Les Miserables	when i am gone the soul will be gone; if i withdraw all will die; and then, this woman, who has suffered so greatly, who has so much merit in her fall, and whose misfortune i unwittingly caused, and the child which i intended to go and fetch, and restore to the mother--do not i also owe something to this woman in reparation of the wrong which i have done her?
Les Miserables	if i disappear, what will happen?
Les Miserables	the mother dies, and the child will become what it can.
Les Miserables	this will happen if i denounce myself.
Les Miserables	if i do not denounce myself?
Les Miserables	come, let me see."
Les Miserables	after asking himself this question, he hesitated, and trembled slightly; but this emotion lasted but a short time, and he answered himself calmly:--  "well, this man will go to the galleys, it is true, but, hang it all!
Les Miserables	he has stolen.
Les Miserables	although i may say to myself that he has not stolen, he has done so!
Les Miserables	i remain here and continue my operations: in ten years i shall have gained ten millions.
Les Miserables	i spread them over the country.
Les Miserables	i keep nothing for myself; but what do i care?
Les Miserables	i am not doing this for myself.
Les Miserables	the prosperity of all is increased; trades are revived, factories and forges are multiplied, and thousands of families are happy; the district is populated; villages spring up where there are only farms, and farms where there is nothing; wretchedness disappears, and with it debauchery, prostitution, robbery, murder, all the vices, all the crimes--and this poor mother brings up her child.
Les Miserables	why, i was mad, absurd, when i talked about denouncing myself, and i must guard against precipitation.
Les Miserables	what!
Les Miserables	because it pleases me to play the grand and the generous--it is pure melodrama after all--because i only thought of myself, and in order to save from a perhaps exaggerated though substantially just punishment a stranger, a thief, and an apparent scoundrel--a whole department must perish, a poor woman die in the hospital, and a poor child starve in the streets, like dogs!
Les Miserables	why, it is abominable!
Les Miserables	without the mother seeing her child again, or the child knowing her mother!
Les Miserables	and all this on behalf of an old scamp of an apple-stealer, who has assuredly deserved the galleys for something else, if not for that.
Les Miserables	these are fine scruples that save a culprit and sacrifice the innocent; that save an old vagabond who has not many years to live, and will not be more unhappy at the galleys than in his hovel, and destroy an entire population,--mothers, wives, and children.
Les Miserables	that poor little cosette, who has only me in the world, and is doubtless at this moment shivering with cold in the den of those thénardiers.
Les Miserables	there is another pair of wretches.
Les Miserables	and i would fail in my duties to all these poor creatures, and commit such a folly as to denounce myself!
Les Miserables	let us put things at the worst: suppose that i am committing a bad action in this, and that my conscience reproaches me with it some day; there will be devotion and virtue in accepting, for the good of my neighbor, these reproaches, which only weigh on me, and this bad action, which only compromises my own soul."
Les Miserables	he got up and began walking up and down again: this time he seemed to be satisfied with himself.
Les Miserables	diamonds are only found in the darkness of the earth; truths are only found in the depths of thought.
Les Miserables	it seemed to him that after descending into these depths, after groping for some time in the densest of this darkness, he had found one of these diamonds, one of these truths, which he held in his hand and which dazzled his eyes when he looked at it.
Les Miserables	"yes," he thought, "i am on the right track and hold the solution of the problem.
Les Miserables	a man must in the end hold on to something, and my mind is made up.
Les Miserables	i will let matters take their course, so no more vacillation or backsliding.
Les Miserables	it is for the interest of all, not of myself.
Les Miserables	i am madeleine, and remain madeleine, and woe to the man who is jean valjean.
Les Miserables	i am no longer he.
Les Miserables	i do not know that man, and if any one happen to be jean valjean at this moment, he must look out for himself, for it does not concern me.
Les Miserables	it is a fatal name that floats in the night, and if it stop and settle on a head, all the worse for that head."
Les Miserables	he looked into the small looking-glass over the mantel-piece, and said to himself,--  "how greatly has forming a resolution relieved me!
Les Miserables	i am quite a different man at present."
Les Miserables	he walked a little way and then stopped short.
Les Miserables	"come," he said, "i must not hesitate before any of the consequences of the resolution i have formed.
Les Miserables	there are threads which still attach me to jean valjean which must be broken.
Les Miserables	there are in this very room objects which would accuse me,--dumb things which would serve as witnesses, and they must all disappear."
Les Miserables	he took his purse from his pocket, and drew a small key out of it.
Les Miserables	he put this key in a lock, the hole of which could scarcely be seen, for it was hidden in the darkest part of the design on the paper that covered the walls.
Les Miserables	a sort of false cupboard made between the corner of the wall and the mantel-piece was visible.
Les Miserables	in this hiding-place there were only a few rags,--a blue blouse, worn trousers, an old knapsack, and a large thorn-stick shod with iron at both ends.
Les Miserables	any one who saw jean valjean pass through d---- in october, 1815, would easily have recognized all these wretched articles.
Les Miserables	he had preserved them, as he had done the candlesticks, that they might constantly remind him of his starting-point; still he hid what came from the galleys, and displayed the candlesticks which came from the bishop.
Les Miserables	he took a furtive glance at the door, as if afraid that it might open in spite of the bolt; and then with a rapid movement he made but one armful of the things he had so religiously and perilously kept for so many years, and threw them all--rags, stick, and knapsack--into the fire.
Les Miserables	he closed the cupboard, and, redoubling his precautions, which were now useless since it was empty, dragged a heavy piece of furniture in front of it.
Les Miserables	in a few seconds, the room and opposite wall were lit up with a large red and flickering glow; all was burning, and the thorn-stick crackled and threw out sparks into the middle of the room.
Les Miserables	from the knapsack, as it burned with all the rags it contained, fell something that glistened in the ashes.
Les Miserables	on stooping it could be easily recognized as a coin; it was doubtless the little savoyard's two-franc piece.
Les Miserables	he did not look at the fire, and continued his walk backwards and forwards.
Les Miserables	all at once his eye fell on the two candlesticks which the fire-light caused to shine vaguely on the mantel-piece.
Les Miserables	"stay," he thought, "all jean valjean is in them, and they must be destroyed too."
Les Miserables	he seized the candlesticks--there was a fire large enough to destroy their shape, and convert them into unrecognizable ingots.
Les Miserables	he leaned over the hearth and wanned his hands for a moment; it was a great comfort to him.
Les Miserables	he stirred up the ashes with one of the candlesticks, and in a moment they were both in the fire.
Les Miserables	all at once he fancied he heard a voice cry within him, "jean valjean!
Les Miserables	jean valjean!"
Les Miserables	his hair stood erect, and he became like a man who is listening to a terrible thing.
Les Miserables	"yes, that is right; finish!"
Les Miserables	the voice said: "complete what you are about; destroy those candlesticks, annihilate that reminiscence!
Les Miserables	forget the bishop!
Les Miserables	forget everything!
Les Miserables	rain that champmathieu; that is right.
Les Miserables	applaud yourself; come, all is settled and resolved on.
Les Miserables	this old man, who does not know what they want with him, who is perhaps innocent, whose whole misfortune your name causes, on whom your name weighs like a crime, is going to be taken for you, sentenced, and will end his days in abjectness and horror.
Les Miserables	that is excellent!
Les Miserables	be an honest man yourself; remain mayor, honorable and honored, enrich the town, assist the indigent, bring up orphans, live happy, virtuous, and applauded; and during this time, while you are here in joy and light, there will be somebody who wears your red jacket, bears your name in ignominy, and drags along your chain at the galleys.
Les Miserables	yes, that is excellently arranged.
Les Miserables	oh, you scoundrel!"
Les Miserables	the perspiration beaded on his forehead, and he fixed his haggard eye upon the candlesticks.
Les Miserables	the voice within him, however, had not ended yet.
Les Miserables	"jean valjean!
Les Miserables	there will be around you many voices making a great noise, speaking very loud and blessing you, and one which no one will hear, and which will curse you in the darkness.
Les Miserables	well, listen, infamous man!
Les Miserables	all these blessings will fall back on the ground before reaching heaven, and the curse alone will ascend to god!"
Les Miserables	this voice, at first very faint, and which spoke from the obscurest nook of his conscience, had gradually become sonorous and formidable, and he now heard it in his ear.
Les Miserables	he fancied that it was not his own voice, and he seemed to hear the last words so distinctly that he looked round the room with a species of terror.
Les Miserables	"is there any one here?"
Les Miserables	he asked, in a loud voice and wildly.
Les Miserables	then he continued with a laugh, which seemed almost idiotic,--  "what a fool i am!
Les Miserables	there can be nobody."
Les Miserables	there was somebody, but not of those whom the human eye can see.
Les Miserables	he placed the candlesticks on the mantel-piece, and then resumed that melancholy, mournful walk, which aroused the sleeper underneath him.
Les Miserables	this walking relieved him, and at the same time intoxicated him.
Les Miserables	it appears sometimes as if on supreme occasions people move about to ask advice of everything they pass.
Les Miserables	at the end of a few moments he no longer knew what result to arrive at.
Les Miserables	he now recoiled with equal horror from the two resolutions he had formed in turn; the two ideas that counselled him seemed each as desperate as the other.
Les Miserables	what a fatality that this champmathieu should be taken for him!
Les Miserables	he was hurled down precisely by the means which providence at first seemed to have employed to strengthen his position.
Les Miserables	there was a moment during which he regarded his future.
Les Miserables	denounce himself!
Les Miserables	great heavens!
Les Miserables	give himself up!
Les Miserables	he thought with immense despair of all that he must give up, of all that he must resume.
Les Miserables	he would be forced to bid adieu to this good, pure, radiant life,--to the respect of all classes,--to honor, to liberty!
Les Miserables	he would no longer walk about the fields, he would no longer hear the birds sing in may, or give alms to the little children!
Les Miserables	he would no longer feel the sweetness of glances of gratitude and love fixed upon him!
Les Miserables	he would leave this little house, which he had built, and his little bed-room.
Les Miserables	all appeared charming to him at this moment.
Les Miserables	he would no longer read those books or write at the little deal table; his old servant would no longer bring up his coffee in the morning.
Les Miserables	great god!
Les Miserables	instead of all this, there would be the gang, the red jacket, the chain on his foot, fatigue, the dungeon, the camp-bed, and all the horrors he knew!
Les Miserables	at his age, after all he had borne!
Les Miserables	it would be different were he still young.
Les Miserables	but to be old, coarsely addressed by anybody, searched by the jailer, and receive blows from the keeper's stick!
Les Miserables	to thrust his naked feet into iron-shod shoes!
Les Miserables	to offer his leg morning and night to the man who examines the fetters!
Les Miserables	to endure the curiosity of strangers who would be told, "that is the famous jean valjean, who was mayor of m----!"
Les Miserables	at night, when pouring with perspiration, and crushed by fatigue, with a green cap on his head, to go up two by two, under the sergeants whip, the side ladder of the hulks!
Les Miserables	oh, what misery!
Les Miserables	destiny, then, can be as wicked as an intelligent being and prove as monstrous as the human heart!
Les Miserables	and whatever he might do, he ever fell back into this crushing dilemma, which was the basis of his reverie,--remain in paradise, and become a demon there; or re-enter hell, and become an angel?
Les Miserables	what should he do?
Les Miserables	great god!
Les Miserables	what should he do?
Les Miserables	the trouble, from which he had escaped with such difficulty, was again let loose on him, and his thoughts became composed once more.
Les Miserables	they assumed something stupefied and mechanical, which is peculiar to despair.
Les Miserables	the name of romainville incessantly returned to his mind, with two lines of a song which he had formerly heard.
Les Miserables	he remembered that romainville is a little wood, near paris, where lovers go to pick lilac in april.
Les Miserables	he tottered both externally and internally; he walked like a little child allowed to go alone.
Les Miserables	at certain moments, he struggled against his lassitude, and tried to recapture his intelligence; he tried to set himself, for the last time, the problem over which he had fallen in a state of exhaustion,--must he denounce himself, or must he be silent?
Les Miserables	he could not succeed in seeing anything distinct, the vague outlines of all the reasonings sketched in by his reverie were dissipated in turn like smoke.
Les Miserables	still, he felt that, however he resolved, and without any possibility of escape, something belonging to him was about to die; that he entered a sepulchre, whether on his right hand or his left, and that either his happiness or his virtue would be borne to the grave.
Les Miserables	alas!
Les Miserables	all his irresolution had seized him again, and he was no further advanced than at the beginning.
Les Miserables	thus the wretched soul writhed in agony!
Les Miserables	eighteen hundred years before this unhappy man, the mysterious being in whom are embodied all the sanctities and sufferings of humanity had also, while the olive-trees shuddered in the fierce wind of the infinite, long put away with his hand the awful cup which appeared to him, dripping with shadow and overflowing with darkness in the starry depths.
Les Miserables	chapter iv.
Les Miserables	sufferings in sleep.
Les Miserables	three a.m. had struck, and he had been walking about in this way for five hours without a break, when he fell into his chair.
Les Miserables	he fell asleep, and had a dream.
Les Miserables	this dream, like most dreams, was only connected with his situation by something poignant and mournful, but it made an impression on him.
Les Miserables	this nightmare struck him so much that he wrote it down at a later date, and we think we are bound to transcribe it verbatim; for whatever the history of this man may be, it would be incomplete if we omitted it.
Les Miserables	here it is then; on the envelope we notice the line,--_the dream i had on that night._  "i was upon a plain, a large mournful plain, on which no grass grew.
Les Miserables	it did not seem to me to be day, but it was not night.
Les Miserables	i was walking with my brother, the brother of my boyish years, of whom i am bound to say i never think, and whom i scarce remember.
Les Miserables	we were talking, and met travellers.
Les Miserables	we spoke about a woman, formerly a neighbor of ours, who had always worked with her window open, since she had occupied a front room.
Les Miserables	while talking, we felt cold on account of this open window.
Les Miserables	there were no trees on the plain.
Les Miserables	we saw a man pass close by us; he was a perfectly naked man, of the color of ashes, mounted on a horse of an earthen color.
Les Miserables	the man had no hair, and i could see his skull, and the veins on his skull.
Les Miserables	he held in his hand a wand, which was supple as a vine-twig and heavy as lead.
Les Miserables	this horseman passed and said nothing to us.
Les Miserables	"my brother said to me: 'let us turn into the hollow way.'
Les Miserables	"it was a hollow way in which not a bramble or even a patch of moss could be seen; all was earth-colored, even the sky.
Les Miserables	after going a few yards, i received no answer when i spoke, and i noticed that my brother was no longer with me.
Les Miserables	i entered a village that i saw, and i fancied that it must be romainville.
Les Miserables	the first street i entered was deserted; i entered a second street, and behind the angle formed by the two streets a man was standing against the wall.
Les Miserables	i asked this man, "what is this place?
Les Miserables	where am i?"
Les Miserables	but he gave me no answer.
Les Miserables	i saw the door of a house open, and walked in.
Les Miserables	"the first room was deserted, and i entered a second.
Les Miserables	behind the door of this room there was a man leaning against the wall.
Les Miserables	i asked him, "to whom does this house belong?
Les Miserables	where am i?"
Les Miserables	but the man gave me no answer.
Les Miserables	i went out into the garden of the house, and it was deserted.
Les Miserables	behind the first tree i found a man standing; i said to the man, "whose is this garden?
Les Miserables	where am i?"
Les Miserables	but he made me no answer.
Les Miserables	"i wandered about this village and fancied that it was a town.
Les Miserables	all the streets were deserted, all the doors open.
Les Miserables	not a living soul passed along the street, moved in the rooms, or walked in the gardens.
Les Miserables	but there was behind every corner, every door, and every tree, a man standing silently.
Les Miserables	i never saw more than one at a time, and these men looked at me as i passed.
Les Miserables	"i left the village and began walking about the fields.
Les Miserables	at the end of some time i turned back and saw a great crowd coming after me.
Les Miserables	i recognized all the men whom i had seen in the town, and they had strange heads.
Les Miserables	they did not appear to be in a hurry, and yet they walked faster than i, and made no noise in walking.
Les Miserables	in an instant this crowd joined me and surrounded me.
Les Miserables	the faces of these men were earth-colored.
Les Miserables	then the man i had seen first and questioned when i entered the town said to me, "where are you going?
Les Miserables	do you not know that you have been dead for a long time?"
Les Miserables	i opened my mouth to answer, and i perceived that there was no one near me."
Les Miserables	he woke up, chilled to the marrow, for a wind, cold as the morning breeze, was shaking the open window.
Les Miserables	the fire had died away, the candle was nearly burned out, and it was still black night.
Les Miserables	he rose and went to the window; there were still no stars in the sky.
Les Miserables	from his window he could see the yard and his street, and a dry sharp sound on the ground below him induced him to look out.
Les Miserables	he saw two red stars whose rays lengthened and shortened curiously in the gloom.
Les Miserables	as his mind was half submerged in the mist of dreams, he thought, "there are no stars in the sky: they are on the earth now."
Les Miserables	a second sound like the first completely woke him, and he perceived that those two stars were carriage lamps, and by the light which they projected he could distinguish the shape of the vehicle; it was a tilbury, in which a small white horse was harnessed.
Les Miserables	the sound he had heard was the pawing of the horse's hoof on the ground.
Les Miserables	"what's the meaning of this conveyance?"
Les Miserables	he said to himself.
Les Miserables	"who can have come at so early an hour?"
Les Miserables	at this moment there was a gentle tap at his bed-room door; he shuddered from head to foot, and shouted in a terrible voice, "who's there?"
Les Miserables	some one replied, "i, sir," and he recognized his old servant's voice.
Les Miserables	"well," he continued, "what is it?"
Les Miserables	"it is getting on for four o'clock, sir."
Les Miserables	"what has that to do with me?"
Les Miserables	"the tilbury has come, sir."
Les Miserables	"what tilbury?"
Les Miserables	"did you not order one?"
Les Miserables	"no," he said.
Les Miserables	"the ostler says that he has come to fetch m. le maire."
Les Miserables	"what ostler?"
Les Miserables	"m.
Les Miserables	scaufflaire's."
Les Miserables	this name made him start as if a flash of lightning had passed before his eyes.
Les Miserables	"ah, yes," he repeated, "m.
Les Miserables	scaufflaire."
Les Miserables	could the old woman have seen him at this moment, she would have been horrified.
Les Miserables	there was a lengthened silence, during which he stupidly examined the candle flame and rolled up some of the wax in his fingers.
Les Miserables	the old woman, who was waiting, at length mustered up courage to raise her voice again.
Les Miserables	"m. le maire, what answer am i to give?"
Les Miserables	"say it is quite right, and that i shall be down directly."
Les Miserables	chapter v.   obstacles.
Les Miserables	the letter-bags between arras and m---- were still carried in small mail-carts, dating from the empire.
Les Miserables	they were two-wheeled vehicles, lined with tawny leather, hung on springs, and having only two seats, one for the driver, and another for a passenger.
Les Miserables	the wheels were armed with those long offensive axle-trees, which kept other carriages at a distance, and may still be seen on german roads.
Les Miserables	the compartment for the bags was an immense oblong box at the back; it was painted black, and the front part was yellow.
