Les Miserables	these vehicles, like which we have nothing at the present day, had something ugly and humpbacked about them, and when you saw them pass at a distance or creeping up a hill on the horizon, they resembled those insects called, we think, termites, and which with a small body drag a heavy burden after them.
Les Miserables	they went very fast, however, and the mail which left arras at one in the morning, after the paris mail had arrived, reached m---- a little before five a.m.  on this morning, the mail-cart, just as it entered m----, and while turning a corner, ran into a tilbury drawn by a white horse, coming in the opposite direction, and in which there was only one sitter, a man wrapped in a cloak.
Les Miserables	the wheel of the tilbury received a rather heavy blow, and though the driver of the mail-cart shouted to the man to stop, he did not listen, but went on at a smart trot.
Les Miserables	"the man is in a deuce of a hurry," said the courier.
Les Miserables	the man in this hurry was he whom we have just seen struggling in convulsions, assuredly deserving of pity.
Les Miserables	where was he going?
Les Miserables	he could not have told.
Les Miserables	why was he hurrying?
Les Miserables	he did not know.
Les Miserables	he was going onwards unthinkingly.
Les Miserables	where to?
Les Miserables	doubtless to arras; but he might also be going elsewhere.
Les Miserables	he buried himself in the darkness as in a gulf.
Les Miserables	something urged him on; something attracted him.
Les Miserables	what was going on in him no one could tell, but all will understand it,--for what man has not entered, at least once in his life, this obscure cavern of the unknown?
Les Miserables	however, he had settled, decided, and done nothing; not one of the acts of his conscience had been definitive, and he was still as unsettled as at the beginning.
Les Miserables	why was he going to arras?
Les Miserables	he repeated what he had already said on hiring the gig of scaufflaire--that, whatever the result might be, there would be no harm in seeing with his own eyes, and judging matters for himself--that this was prudent; and he was bound to know what was going on--that he could not decide anything till he had observed and examined--that, at a distance, a man made mountains of molehills--that after all, when he had seen this champmathieu, his conscience would probably be quietly relieved, and he could let the scoundrel go to the galleys in his place: that javert would be there and the three convicts who had known him,--but, nonsense!
Les Miserables	they would not recognize him, for all conjectures and suppositions were fixed on this champmathieu, and there is nothing so obstinate as conjectures and suppositions,--and that hence he incurred no danger.
Les Miserables	it was doubtless a black moment, but he would emerge from it.
Les Miserables	after all, he held his destiny, however adverse it might try to be, in his own hands, and was master of it.
Les Miserables	he clung wildly to the latter thought.
Les Miserables	although, to tell the whole truth, he would have preferred not to go to arras, yet he went.
Les Miserables	while reflecting he lashed the horse, which was going at that regular and certain trot which covers two leagues and a half in an hour; and as the gig advanced, he felt something within him recoil.
Les Miserables	at day-break he was in the open country, and the town of m---- was far behind him.
Les Miserables	he watched the horizon grow white; he looked, without seeing them, at all the cold figures of a winter dawn.
Les Miserables	morning has its spectres like night.
Les Miserables	he did not see them, but unconsciously, and through a sort of almost physical penetration, these black outlines of trees and hills added something gloomy and sinister to the violent state of his soul.
Les Miserables	each time that he passed one of those isolated houses which skirt high roads, he said to himself: "and yet there are people asleep in them."
Les Miserables	the trot of the horse, the bells on the harness, the wheels on the stones, produced a gentle and monotonous sound, which is delightful when you are merry, and mournful when you are sad.
Les Miserables	it was broad daylight when he reached hesdin, and he stopped at the inn to let the horse breathe and give it a feed.
Les Miserables	this horse, as scaufflaire had said, belonged to that small boulonnais breed, which has too large a head, too much stomach, and not enough neck, but which also has a wide crupper, lean, slender legs, and a solid hoof: it is an ugly but strong and healthy breed.
Les Miserables	the capital little beast had done five leagues in two hours, and had not turned a hair.
Les Miserables	he did not get out of the tilbury; the ostler who brought the oats suddenly stooped down and examined the left wheel.
Les Miserables	"are you going far in this state?"
Les Miserables	the man said.
Les Miserables	he answered almost without emerging from his reverie,--  "why do you ask?"
Les Miserables	"have you come any distance?"
Les Miserables	the ostler continued.
Les Miserables	"five leagues."
Les Miserables	"ah!"
Les Miserables	"why do you say, 'ah'?"
Les Miserables	the ostler bent down again, remained silent for a moment, with his eye fixed on the wheel, and then said as he drew himself up,--  "because this wheel, which may have gone five leagues, cannot possibly go another mile."
Les Miserables	he jumped out of the tilbury.
Les Miserables	"what are you saying, my friend?"
Les Miserables	"i say that it is a miracle you and your horse did not roll into a ditch by the road-side.
Les Miserables	just look."
Les Miserables	the wheel was, in fact, seriously damaged.
Les Miserables	the blow dealt it by the mail-cart had broken two spokes, and almost carried away the axle-tree.
Les Miserables	"my good fellow," he said to the ostler, "is there a wheelwright here?"
Les Miserables	"of course, sir."
Les Miserables	"be good enough to go and fetch him."
Les Miserables	"he lives close by.
Les Miserables	hilloh, master bourgaillard."
Les Miserables	master bourgaillard was standing in his doorway: he examined the wheel, and made a face like a surgeon regarding a broken leg.
Les Miserables	"can you mend this wheel?"
Les Miserables	"yes, sir."
Les Miserables	"when can i start again?"
Les Miserables	"to-morrow: there is a good day's work.
Les Miserables	are you in a hurry, sir?"
Les Miserables	"in a great hurry: i must set out again in an hour at the latest."
Les Miserables	"it is impossible, sir."
Les Miserables	"i will pay anything you ask."
Les Miserables	"impossible."
Les Miserables	"well, in two hours?"
Les Miserables	"it is impossible for to-day; you will not be able to go on till to-morrow."
Les Miserables	"my business cannot wait till to-morrow.
Les Miserables	suppose, instead of mending this wheel, you were to put another on?"
Les Miserables	"how so?"
Les Miserables	"you are a wheelwright, and have probably a wheel you can sell me, and then i could set out again directly."
Les Miserables	"i have no ready-made wheel to suit your gig, for wheels are sold in pairs, and it is not easy to match one."
Les Miserables	"in that case, sell me a pair of wheels."
Les Miserables	"all wheels, sir, do not fit all axle-trees."
Les Miserables	"at any rate try."
Les Miserables	"it is useless, sir; i have only cart-wheels for sale, for ours is a small place."
Les Miserables	"have you a gig i can hire?"
Les Miserables	the wheelwright had noticed at a glance that the tilbury was a hired vehicle; he shrugged his shoulders.
Les Miserables	"you take such good care of gigs you hire, that if i had one i would not let it to you."
Les Miserables	"well, one to sell me?"
Les Miserables	"i have not one."
Les Miserables	"what, not a tax-cart?
Les Miserables	i am not particular, as you see."
Les Miserables	"this is a small place.
Les Miserables	i have certainly," the wheelwright added, "an old calèche in my stable, which belongs to a person in the town, and who uses it on the thirty-sixth of every month.
Les Miserables	i could certainly let it out to you, for it is no concern of mine, but the owner must not see it pass; and besides, it is a calèche, and will want two horses."
Les Miserables	"i will hire post-horses."
Les Miserables	"where are you going to, sir?"
Les Miserables	"to arras."
Les Miserables	"and you wish to arrive to-day?"
Les Miserables	"certainly."
Les Miserables	"by taking post-horses?"
Les Miserables	"why not?"
Les Miserables	"does it make any difference to you if you reach arras at four o'clock to-morrow morning?"
Les Miserables	"of course it does."
Les Miserables	"there is one thing to be said about hiring post-horses; have you your passport, sir?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"well, if you take post-horses, you will not reach arras before to-morrow.
Les Miserables	we are on a cross-country road.
Les Miserables	the relays are badly served, and the horses are out at work.
Les Miserables	this is the ploughing season, and as strong teams are required, horses are taken anywhere, from the post-houses like the rest.
Les Miserables	you will have to wait three or four hours, sir, at each station, and only go at a foot-pace, for there are many hills to ascend."
Les Miserables	"well, i will ride.
Les Miserables	take the horse out.
Les Miserables	i suppose i can purchase a saddle here?"
Les Miserables	"of course, but will this horse carry a saddle?"
Les Miserables	"no, i remember now that it will not."
Les Miserables	"in that case--"  "but surely i can hire a saddle-horse in the village?"
Les Miserables	"what!
Les Miserables	to go to arras without a break?"
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"you would want a horse such as is not to be found in these parts.
Les Miserables	in the first place, you would have to buy it, as you are a stranger, but you would not find one to buy or hire for five hundred francs,--not for a thousand."
Les Miserables	"what is to be done?"
Les Miserables	"the best thing is to let me mend the wheel and put off your journey till to-morrow."
Les Miserables	"to-morrow will be too late."
Les Miserables	"hang it!"
Les Miserables	"is there not the arras mail-cart?
Les Miserables	when does that pass?"
Les Miserables	"not till to-night."
Les Miserables	"what!
Les Miserables	you will take a whole day in mending that wheel?"
Les Miserables	"an honest day."
Les Miserables	"suppose you employed two workmen?"
Les Miserables	"ay, if i had ten."
Les Miserables	"suppose the spokes were tied with cords?"
Les Miserables	"what is to be done with the axle?
Les Miserables	besides, the felloe is in a bad state."
Les Miserables	"is there any one who lets out vehicles in the town?"
Les Miserables	"no."
Les Miserables	"is there another wheelwright?"
Les Miserables	the ostler and the wheelwright replied simultaneously--,  "no."
Les Miserables	he felt an immense joy, for it was evident that providence was interfering.
Les Miserables	providence had broken the tilbury wheel and stopped his journey.
Les Miserables	he had not yielded to this species of first summons; he had made every possible effort to continue his journey; he had loyally and scrupulously exhausted all resources; he had not recoiled before the season, fatigue, or expense; and he had nothing to reproach himself with.
Les Miserables	if he did not go farther, it did not concern him; it was not his fault, it was not the doing of his conscience, but of providence.
Les Miserables	he breathed freely and fully for the first time since javert's visit.
Les Miserables	he felt as if the iron hand which had been squeezing his heart for twenty hours had relaxed its grasp; god now appeared to be on his side, and declared himself openly.
Les Miserables	he said to himself that he had done all in his power, and at present need only return home quietly.
Les Miserables	had the conversation with the wheelwright taken place in an inn-room, it would probably have not been heard by any one,--matters would have remained in this state, and we should probably not have had to record any of the following events; but the conversation took place in the street.
Les Miserables	any colloquy in the street inevitably produces a crowd, for there are always people who only ask to be spectators.
Les Miserables	while he was questioning the wheelwright, some passers-by stopped around, and a lad to whom no one paid any attention, after listening for some moments, ran off.
Les Miserables	at the instant when the traveller made up his mind to turn back, this boy returned, accompanied by an old woman.
Les Miserables	"sir," the woman said, "my boy tells me that you wish to hire a conveyance?"
Les Miserables	this simple remark, made by an old woman led by a child, made the perspiration pour down his back.
Les Miserables	he fancied he saw the hand which had let him loose reappear in the shadow behind him, ready to clutch him again.
Les Miserables	he replied,--  "yes, my good woman, i want to hire a gig."
Les Miserables	and he hastily added, "but there is not one in the town."
Les Miserables	"yes there is," said the old woman.
Les Miserables	"where?"
Les Miserables	the wheelwright remarked.
Les Miserables	"at my house," the old crone answered.
Les Miserables	he gave a start, for the fatal hand had seized him again.
Les Miserables	the poor woman really had a sort of wicker-cart under a shed.
Les Miserables	the wheelwright and the ostler, sorry to see the traveller escape them, interfered:--  "it was a frightful rattle-trap, and had no springs,--it is a fact that the inside seats were hung with leathern straps--the rain got into it--the wheels were rusty, and ready to fall to pieces--it would not go much farther than the tilbury--the gentleman had better not get into it,"--and so on.
Les Miserables	all this was true; but the rattle-trap, whatever it might be, rolled on two wheels, and could go to arras.
Les Miserables	he paid what was asked, left the tilbury to be repaired against his return, had the horse put into the cart, got in, and went his way.
Les Miserables	at the moment when the cart moved ahead, he confessed to himself that an instant before he had felt a sort of joy at the thought that he could not continue his journey.
Les Miserables	he examined this joy with a sort of passion, and found it absurd.
Les Miserables	why did he feel joy at turning back?
Les Miserables	after all, he was making this journey of his free will, and no one forced him to do so.
Les Miserables	and assuredly nothing could happen, except what he liked.
Les Miserables	as he was leaving hesdin, he heard a voice shouting to him, "stop, stop!"
Les Miserables	he stopped the cart with a hurried movement in which there was something feverish and convulsive that resembled joy.
Les Miserables	it was the old woman's boy.
Les Miserables	"sir," he said, "it was i who got you the cart."
Les Miserables	"well?"
Les Miserables	"you have given me nothing."
Les Miserables	he who gave to all, and so easily, considered this demand exorbitant, and almost odious.
Les Miserables	"oh, it's you, scamp," he said; "well, you will not have anything."
Les Miserables	he flogged his horse, which started again at a smart trot.
Les Miserables	he had lost much time at hesdin, and would have liked to recover it.
Les Miserables	the little horse was courageous, and worked for two; but it was february, it had been raining, and the roads were bad.
Les Miserables	the cart too ran much more heavily than the tilbury, and there were numerous ascents.
Les Miserables	he took nearly four hours in going from hesdin to st. pol: four hours for five leagues!
Les Miserables	at st. pol he pulled up at the first inn he came to, and had the horse put in a stable.
Les Miserables	as he had promised scaufflaire, he stood near the crib while it was eating, and had troubled and confused thoughts.
Les Miserables	the landlady entered the stable.
Les Miserables	"do you not wish to breakfast, sir?"
Les Miserables	"it is true," said he, "i am very hungry."
Les Miserables	he followed the woman, who had a healthy, ruddy face; she led him to a ground-floor room, in which were tables covered with oil-cloth.
Les Miserables	"make haste," he remarked, "for i am in a great hurry."
Les Miserables	a plump flemish servant-girl hastened to lay the cloth, and he looked at her with a feeling of comfort.
Les Miserables	"that was the trouble," he thought; "i had not breakfasted."
Les Miserables	he pounced upon the bread, bit a mouthful, and then slowly laid it back on the table, and did not touch it again.
Les Miserables	a wagoner was sitting at another table, and he said to him,--  "why is their bread so bitter?"
Les Miserables	the wagoner was a german, and did not understand him; he returned to his horse.
Les Miserables	an hour later he had left st. pol, and was proceeding toward tinques, which is only five leagues from arras.
Les Miserables	what did he do during the drive?
Les Miserables	what was he thinking of?
Les Miserables	as in the morning, he looked at the trees, the roofs, the ploughed fields, and the diversities of a landscape which every turn in the road changes, as he passed them.
Les Miserables	to see a thousand different objects for the first and last time is most melancholy!
Les Miserables	travelling is birth and death at every moment.
Les Miserables	perhaps in the vaguest region of his mind he made a comparison between the changing horizon and human existence, for everything in this life is continually flying before us.
Les Miserables	shadow and light are blended; after a dazzling comes an eclipse; every event is a turn in the road, and all at once you are old.
Les Miserables	you feel something like a shock, all is black, you distinguish an obscure door, and the gloomy horse of life which dragged you, stops, and you see a veiled, unknown form unharnessing it.
Les Miserables	twilight was setting in at the moment when the school-boys, leaving school, saw this traveller enter tinques.
Les Miserables	he did not halt there, but as he left the village, a road-mender, who was laying stones, raised his head, and said to him,--  "your horse is very tired."
Les Miserables	the poor brute, in fact, could not get beyond a walk.
Les Miserables	"are you going to arras?"
Les Miserables	the road-mender continued.
Les Miserables	"yes."
Les Miserables	"if you go at that pace, you will not reach it very soon."
Les Miserables	he stopped his horse, and asked the road-mender--,  "how far is it from here to arras?"
Les Miserables	"nearly seven long leagues."
Les Miserables	"how so?
Les Miserables	the post-book says only five and a quarter leagues."
Les Miserables	"ah" the road-mender continued, "you do not know that the road is under repair; you will find it cut up about a mile farther on, and it is impossible to pass."
Les Miserables	"indeed!"

Les Miserables	"but i shall lose my way in the dark."
Les Miserables	"you do not belong to these parts?"
Les Miserables	"no."
Les Miserables	"and it is a cross-road; stay, sir," the road-mender continued; "will you let me give you a piece of advice?
Les Miserables	your horse is tired, so return to tinques, where there is a good inn; sleep there, and go to arras to-morrow."
Les Miserables	"i must be there to-night."
Les Miserables	"that is different.
Les Miserables	in that case go back to the inn all the same, and hire a second horse.
Les Miserables	the stable boy will act as your guide across the country."
Les Miserables	he took the road-mender's advice, turned back, and half an hour after passed the same spot at a sharp trot with a strong second horse.
Les Miserables	a stable lad, who called himself a postilion, was sitting on the shafts of the cart.
Les Miserables	still he felt that he had lost time, for it was now dark.
Les Miserables	they entered the cross-road, and it soon became frightful; the cart tumbled from one rut into another, but he said to the postilion,--  "keep on at a trot, and i will give you a double fee."
Les Miserables	in one of the jolts the whipple-tree broke.
Les Miserables	"the whipple-tree is broken, sir," said the postilion, "and i do not know how to fasten my horse, and the road is very bad by night.
Les Miserables	if you will go back and sleep at tinques, we can get to arras at an early hour to-morrow."
Les Miserables	he answered, "have you a piece of rope and a knife?"
Les Miserables	"yes, sir."
Les Miserables	he cut a branch and made a whipple-tree; it was a further loss of twenty minutes, but they started again at a gallop.
Les Miserables	the plain was dark, and a low, black fog was creeping over the hills.
Les Miserables	a heavy wind, which came from the sea, made in all the corners of the horizon a noise like that of furniture being moved.
Les Miserables	all that he could see had an attitude of terror, for how many things shudder beneath the mighty breath of night!
Les Miserables	the cold pierced him, for he had eaten nothing since the previous morning.
Les Miserables	he vaguely recalled his other night-excursion, on the great plain of d---- eight years before, and it seemed to him to be yesterday.
Les Miserables	a clock struck from a distant steeple, and he asked the lad,--  "what o'clock is that?"
Les Miserables	"seven, sir, and we shall be at arras by eight, for we have only three leagues to go."
Les Miserables	at this moment he made for the first time this reflection--and considered it strange that it had not occurred to him before--that all the trouble he was taking was perhaps thrown away; he did not even know the hour for the trial, and he might at least have asked about that; it was extravagant to go on thus, without knowing if it would be of any service.
Les Miserables	then he made some mental calculations: usually the sittings of assize courts began at nine o'clock; this matter would not occupy much time, the theft of the apples would be easily proved, and then there would be merely the identification, four or five witnesses to hear, and little for counsel to say.
Les Miserables	he would arrive when it was all over.
Les Miserables	the postilion flogged the horses; they had crossed the river and left mont st hoy behind them; the night was growing more and more dark.
Les Miserables	chapter vi.
Les Miserables	sister simplice is sorely tried.
Les Miserables	at this very moment fantine was joyful.
Les Miserables	she had passed a very bad night, she had coughed fearfully, and her fever had become worse.
Les Miserables	in the morning, when the physician paid his visit, she was raving; he felt alarmed, and begged to be sent for so soon as m. madeleine arrived.
Les Miserables	all the morning she was gloomy, said little, and made folds in sheets, while murmuring in a low voice, and calculating what seemed to be distances.
Les Miserables	her eyes were hollow and fixed, they seemed almost extinct, and then, at moments, they were relit, and flashed like stars.
Les Miserables	it seems as if, on the approach of a certain dark hour, the brightness of heaven fills those whom the brightness of earth is quitting.

Les Miserables	madeleine."
Les Miserables	a few months previously, at the time when fantine lost her last modesty, her last shame, and her last joy, she was the shadow of herself: now she was the ghost.
Les Miserables	physical suffering had completed the work of moral suffering.
Les Miserables	this creature of five-and-twenty years of age had a wrinkled forehead, sunken cheeks, a pinched nose, a leaden complexion, a bony neck, projecting shoulder-blades, thin limbs, an earthy skin, and white hairs were mingled with the auburn.
Les Miserables	alas!

Les Miserables	m. madeleine usually came at three o'clock, and as punctuality was kindness, he was punctual.
Les Miserables	at about half-past two fantine began to grow agitated, and in the next twenty minutes asked the nun more than ten times, "what o'clock is it?"

Les Miserables	then fantine turned and looked at the door: but no one entered, and the door was not opened.

Les Miserables	her thoughts could be clearly read, but she did not say a word, complain, or make any accusation: she merely coughed in a sad way.

Les Miserables	it was an old cradle-song with which she had in former times lulled her little cosette to sleep, and which had not once recurred to her during the five years she had been parted from her child.

Les Miserables	inside the court.
Les Miserables	without suspecting the fact, the mayor of m---- enjoyed a species of celebrity.
Les Miserables	during the seven years that his reputation for virtue had filled the whole of the bas boulonnais, it had gradually crossed the border line into two or three adjoining departments.

Les Miserables	he had ever assisted and promoted, when necessary, the trades of other departments: thus he had supported with his credit and funds, the tulle factory at boulogne, the flax-spinning machine at nivers, and the hydraulic manufacture of canvas at bourbus sur cauche.

Les Miserables	he crumpled the paper in his hands, as if the words had a strange and bitter after-taste for him.
Les Miserables	he followed the usher, and a few minutes later found himself alone in a room of severe appearance, lighted by two wax candles standing on a green-baize covered table.
Les Miserables	he still had in his ears the last words of the usher, who had just left him,-- "you are in the judges' chamber; you have only to turn the handle of that door, and you will find yourself in court behind the president's chair."
Les Miserables	these words were mingled in his thoughts with a confused recollection of narrow passages and dark staircases, which he had just passed through.
Les Miserables	the usher had left him alone; the supreme moment had arrived.
Les Miserables	he tried to collect himself, but could not succeed; for it is especially in the hours when men have the most need of thought that all the threads are broken in the brain.
Les Miserables	he was at the actual spot where the judges deliberate and pass sentence.
Les Miserables	he gazed with stupid tranquillity at this peaceful and yet formidable room, in which so many existences had been broken, where his name would be echoed ere long, and which his destiny was traversing at this moment.
Les Miserables	he looked at the walls and then at himself, astonished that it was this room and that it was he.
Les Miserables	he had not eaten for more than twenty-four hours, he was exhausted by the jolting of the cart, but he did not feel it; it seemed to him that he did not feel anything.
Les Miserables	he walked up to a black frame hanging on the wall, and which contained under glass an autograph letter of jean nicolas pache, mayor of paris, and minister, dated, doubtless in error, juin 9 an ii., and in which pache sent to the commune a list of the ministers and deputies under arrest at their own houses.
Les Miserables	any who saw him at this moment would doubtless have imagined that this letter appeared to him very curious, for he did not remove his eyes from it, and read it two or three times.
Les Miserables	but he read it without paying attention; and unconsciously he was thinking of fantine and cosette.
Les Miserables	while thinking, he turned, and his eyes met the brass handle of the door that separated him from the assize court.
Les Miserables	he had almost forgotten this door, but his eye, at first calm, rested on it, then became wild and fixed, and was gradually filled with terror.
Les Miserables	drops of perspiration started out from his hair and streamed down his temples.
Les Miserables	at one moment he made with a species of authority blended with rebellion that indescribable gesture which means and says so well,--"by heaven, who forces me?"
Les Miserables	then he turned hurriedly, saw before him the door by which he had entered, walked up, opened it, and went out.
Les Miserables	he was no longer in that room, but in a passage, a long narrow passage, cut up by steps and wickets, making all sorts of turns, lit up here and there by reflectors like the night-lamps for the sick,--the passage by which he had come.
Les Miserables	he breathed, he listened, not a sound behind him, not a sound before him, and he began to fly as if he were pursued.
Les Miserables	when he had passed several turnings, he listened again,--there was still the same silence and the same gloom around him.
Les Miserables	he panted, tottered, and leaned against the wall; the stone was cold, the perspiration was chilled on his forehead, and he drew himself up with a shudder.
Les Miserables	then standing there alone, trembling from cold, and perhaps from something else, he thought.
Les Miserables	he had thought all night, he had thought all day; but he only heard within him a voice that said, alas!
Les Miserables	a quarter of an hour passed thus; at length he inclined his head, sighed with agony, let his arms droop, and turned back.
Les Miserables	he walked slowly and as if stunned; it looked as if he had been caught up in his flight, and was being brought back.
Les Miserables	he entered the judges chamber, and the first thing he saw was the handle of the door.
Les Miserables	this handle, which was round and made of polished brass, shone for him like a terrific star; he looked at it as a sheep would look at the eye of a tiger.
Les Miserables	his eyes would not leave it, and from time to time he took a step which brought him nearer to the door.
Les Miserables	had he listened he would have heard, like a species of confused murmur, the noise in the adjoining court; but he did not listen and did not hear.
Les Miserables	all at once, and without knowing how, he found himself close to the door; he convulsively seized the handle, and the door opened.
Les Miserables	he was in the assize court.
Les Miserables	chapter ix.
Les Miserables	the trial.
Les Miserables	he advanced a step, closed the door mechanically after him, and gazed at the scene before him.
Les Miserables	it was a dimly-lighted large hall, at one moment full of sounds, and at another of silence, in which all the machinery of a criminal trial was displayed, with its paltry and lugubrious gravity, in the midst of a crowd.
Les Miserables	at one of the ends of the hall, the one where he was, judges with a vacant look, in shabby gowns, biting their nails or shutting their eye-lids; barristers in all sorts of attitudes; soldiers with honest harsh faces; old stained wainscoting, a dirty ceiling; tables covered with baize, which was rather yellow than green; doors blackened by hands; pot-house sconces that produced more smoke than light, hanging from nails driven into the wall; upon the tables brass candlesticks,--all was obscurity, ugliness, and sadness.
Les Miserables	but all this yet produced an austere and august impression, for the grand human thing called law, and the great divine thing called justice, could be felt in it.
Les Miserables	no one in this crowd paid any attention to him, for all eyes converged on a single point,--a wooden bench placed against a little door, along the wall on the left of the president; on this bench, which was illumined by several candles, sat a man between two gendarmes.
Les Miserables	this man was the man; he did not seek him, he saw him; his eyes went there naturally, as if they had known beforehand where that face was.
Les Miserables	he fancied he saw himself, aged, not absolutely alike in face, but exactly similar in attitude and appearance, with his bristling hair, with his savage restless eyeballs, and the blouse, just as he was on the day when he entered d----, full of hatred, and concealing in his mind that hideous treasure of frightful thoughts which he had spent nineteen years in collecting on the pavement of the bagne.
Les Miserables	he said to himself with a shudder, "my god!
Les Miserables	shall i become again like that?"
Les Miserables	this being appeared to be at least sixty years of age; he had something about him rough, stupid, and startled.
Les Miserables	on hearing the sound of the door, persons made way for the new comer, the president had turned his head, and guessing that the gentleman who had just entered was the mayor of m----, he bowed to him.
Les Miserables	the public prosecutor who had seen m. madeleine at m----, whither his duties had more than once called him, recognized him and also bowed.
Les Miserables	he scarce noticed it, for he was under a species of hallucination; he was looking at a judge, a clerk, gendarmes, a number of cruelly curious faces,--he had seen all this once, formerly, seven-and-twenty years ago.
Les Miserables	these mournful things he found again,--they were there, stirring, existing; it was no longer an effort of his memory, a mirage of his mind; they were real gendarmes, real judges, a real crowd, and real men in flesh and bone.
Les Miserables	he saw all the monstrous aspect of his past reappear, and live again around him, with all the terror that reality possesses.
Les Miserables	all this was yawning before him; he felt terrified, closed his eyes, and exclaimed in the depths of his mind.
Les Miserables	never!
Les Miserables	and by a tragic sport of fate which made all his ideas terrible and rendered him nearly mad, it was another himself who was there.
Les Miserables	this man who was being tried everybody called jean valjean.
Les Miserables	he had before him an unheard-of vision, a species of representation of the most horrible moment of his life played by his phantom.
Les Miserables	all was there,--it was the same machinery, the same hour of the night, almost the same faces of judges, soldiers, and spectators.
Les Miserables	the only difference was that there was a crucifix over the president's head, which had been removed from the courts at the time of his condemnation.
Les Miserables	when he was tried god was absent.
Les Miserables	there was a chair behind him, into which he fell, terrified by the idea that people could see him.
Les Miserables	when he was seated he took advantage of a pile of paste-board cases on the judges' desk to hide his face from the spectators.
Les Miserables	he could now see without being seen: he fully regained the feeling of the real, and gradually recovered.
Les Miserables	he attained that phase of calmness in which a man can listen.
Les Miserables	monsieur bamatabois was serving on the jury.
Les Miserables	he looked for javert, but could not see him, for the witnesses' bench was hidden by the clerk's table, and then, as we have said, the court was hardly lighted.
Les Miserables	at the moment when he came in, the counsel for the defence was ending his speech.
Les Miserables	the attention of all was excited to the highest pitch; for three hours they had seen a man, a stranger, a species of miserable being, deeply stupid or deeply clever, being gradually crushed by the weight of a terrible resemblance.
Les Miserables	this man, as we know already, was a vagabond who was found in a field, carrying a branch covered with ripe apples, which had been broken off a tree in a neighboring orchard.
Les Miserables	who was this man?
Les Miserables	inquiries had been made, and witnesses heard; they were unanimous, and light had flashed all through the trial.
Les Miserables	the accusation said,--"we have got hold not only of a fruit-stealer, a marauder, but we hold under our hand a bandit, a man who has broken his ban, an ex-convict, a most dangerous villain, a malefactor of the name of jean valjean, whom justice has been seeking for a long time, and who, eight years ago, on leaving toulon, committed a highway robbery with violence on a savoyard lad, called little gervais, a crime provided for by article 383 of the penal code, for which we intend to prosecute him hereafter, when the identity has been judicially proved.
Les Miserables	he has just committed a fresh robbery, and that is a case of relapse.
Les Miserables	find him guilty of the new offence, and he will be tried at a later date for the old one."
Les Miserables	the prisoner seemed highly amazed at this accusation and the unanimity of the witnesses; he made gestures and signs intended to deny, or else looked at the ceiling.
Les Miserables	he spoke with difficulty, answered with embarrassment, but from head to foot his whole person denied.
Les Miserables	he was like an idiot in the presence of all these intellects ranged in battle-array round him, and like a stranger in the midst of this society which seized him.
Les Miserables	still, a most menacing future was hanging over him; the probability of his being jean valjean increased with each moment, and the entire crowd regarded with greater anxiety than himself the sentence full of calamity which was gradually settling down on him.
Les Miserables	an eventuality even offered a glimpse of a death-penalty, should the identity be proved, and he was hereafter found guilty of the attack on little gervais.
Les Miserables	who was this man?
Les Miserables	of what nature was his apathy?
Les Miserables	was it imbecility or cunning?
Les Miserables	did he understand too much, or did he understand nothing at all?
Les Miserables	these questions divided the crowd, and the jury seemed to share their opinion.
Les Miserables	there was in this trial something terrific and something puzzling; the drama was not only gloomy, but it was obscure.
Les Miserables	the counsel for the defence had argued rather cleverly, in that provincial language which for a long time constituted the eloquence of the bar, and which all barristers formerly employed, not only at paris but at romorantin or montbrison, and which at the present day, having become classical, is only spoken by public prosecutors, whom it suits through its serious sonorousness and majestic movements.
Les Miserables	it is the language in which a husband is called a "consort;" a wife, a "spouse;" paris, "the centre of the arts and of civilization;" the king, "the monarch;" the bishop, a "holy pontiff;" the public prosecutor, the "eloquent interpreter of the majesty of the law;" the pleadings, the "accents which we have just heard;" the age of louis xiv., "the great age;" a theatre, the "temple of melpomene;" the reigning family, the "august blood of our kings;" a concert, "a musical solemnity;" the general commanding in the department, "the illustrious warrior who, etc.
Les Miserables	;" the pupils of the seminary, "those tender levites;" the mistakes imputed to the newspapers, "the imposture which distils its venom in the columns of these organs," etc., etc.
Les Miserables	the barrister had, consequently, begun by explaining away the robbery of the apples,--rather a difficult thing in this grand style; but bénigne bossuet himself was obliged to allude to a fowl in the midst of a formal speech, and got out of the difficulty with glory.
Les Miserables	the barrister had established the fact that the apple robbery was not materially proved,--his client, whom, in his quality as defender, he persistently called champmathieu, had not been seen by any one scaling a wall or breaking the branch; he had been arrested with the branch in his possession, but he declared that he found it on the ground and picked it up.
Les Miserables	where was the proof of the contrary?
Les Miserables	this branch had been broken off and then thrown away by the frightened robber, for doubtless there was one.
Les Miserables	but where was the evidence that this champmathieu was a robber?
Les Miserables	only one thing, his being an ex-convict.
Les Miserables	the counsel did not deny that this fact seemed unluckily proved.
Les Miserables	the prisoner had lived at faverolles; he had been a wood-cutter; the name of champmathieu might possibly be derived from jean mathieu; lastly, four witnesses unhesitatingly recognized champmathieu as the galley slave, jean valjean.
Les Miserables	to these indications, to this testimony, the counsel could only oppose his client's denial, which was certainly interested: but, even supposing that he was the convict jean mathieu, did that prove he was the apple-stealer?
Les Miserables	it was a presumption at the most, but not a proof.
Les Miserables	the accused, it was true,--and his counsel was obliged "in his good faith" to allow it,--had adopted a bad system of defence; he insisted in denying everything,--not merely the robbery, but his quality as convict.
Les Miserables	a confession on the latter point would have doubtless been better, and gained him the indulgence of his judges; the counsel had advised him to do so, but the prisoner had obstinately refused, probably in the belief that he would save everything by confessing nothing.
Les Miserables	this was wrong, but should not his scanty intellect be taken into consideration?
Les Miserables	this man was visibly stupid: a long misery at the galleys, a long wretchedness out of them, had brutalized him, etc., etc.
Les Miserables	; his defence was bad, but was that a reason to find him guilty?
Les Miserables	as for the offence on little gervais, the counsel need not argue that, as it was not included in the indictment.
Les Miserables	the counsel wound up by imploring the jury and the court, if the identity of jean valjean appeared to them proved, to punish him as a criminal who had broken his ban, and not apply the fearful chastisement which falls on the relapsed convict.
Les Miserables	the public prosecutor replied.
Les Miserables	he was violent and flowery, as public prosecutors usually are.
Les Miserables	he congratulated the counsel for the defence on his "fairness," and cleverly took advantage of it; he attacked the prisoner with all the concessions which his counsel had made.
Les Miserables	he appeared to allow that the prisoner was jean valjean, and he therefore was so.
Les Miserables	this was so much gained for the prosecution, and could not be contested; and here, reverting cleverly to the sources and causes of criminality, the public prosecutor thundered against the immorality of the romantic school, at that time in its dawn under the name of the "satanic school," which the critics of the _quotidienne_ and the _oriflamme_ had given it; and he attributed, not without some show of reason, the crime of champmathieu, or to speak more correctly, of jean valjean, to this perverse literature.
Les Miserables	these reflections exhausted, he passed to jean valjean himself.
Les Miserables	who was this jean valjean?
Les Miserables	here came a description of jean valjean, a monster in human form, etc.
Les Miserables	the model of this sort of description will be found in the recitation of théramène, which is not only useful to tragedy but daily renders great services to judicial eloquence.
Les Miserables	the audience and the jury "quivered," and when the description was ended, the public prosecutor went on, with an oratorical outburst intended to excite to the highest pitch the enthusiasm of the country papers which would appear the next morning.
Les Miserables	"and it is such a man, etc., etc., etc., a vagabond, a beggar, having no means of existence, etc., etc., etc., accustomed through his past life to culpable actions, and but little corrected by confinement in the bagne, as is proved by the crime committed on little gervais, etc., etc., etc.,--it is such a man, who, found on the high road with the proof of robbery in his hand, and a few paces from the wall he had climbed over, denies the fact, the robbery, denies everything, even to his name and his identity.
Les Miserables	in addition to a hundred proofs to which we will not revert, four witnesses recognize him,--javert, the upright inspector of police, and three of his old comrades in ignominy, the convicts brevet, chenildieu, and cochepaille.
Les Miserables	and what does he oppose to this crushing unanimity?
Les Miserables	he denies.
Les Miserables	what hardness of heart!
Les Miserables	but you will do justice, gentlemen of the jury, etc., etc., etc."
Les Miserables	while the public prosecutor was speaking, the prisoner listened with open mouth, and with a sort of amazement in which there was certainly some admiration.
Les Miserables	he was evidently surprised that a man could speak like this.
Les Miserables	from time to time, at the most energetic apostrophes, when eloquence, unable to restrain itself, overflows in a flux of branding epithets, and envelopes the prisoner in a tempest, he slowly moved his head from right to left, and from left to right, in a sort of dumb and melancholy protest, with which he had contented himself ever since the beginning of the trial.
Les Miserables	twice or thrice the spectators standing nearest to him heard him say in a low voice: "all this comes from not asking monsieur baloup."
Les Miserables	the public prosecutor drew the attention of the jury to this dull attitude, which was evidently calculated, and which denoted, not imbecility, but skill, cunning, and the habit of deceiving justice, and which brought out in full light the "profound perverseness" of this man.
Les Miserables	he concluded by reserving the affair of little gervais, and by demanding a severe sentence.
Les Miserables	the counsel for the defence rose, began by complimenting the public prosecutor on his "admirable speech," and then replied as well as he could, but feebly; it was plain that the ground was giving way under him.
Les Miserables	chapter x.   the system of denial.
Les Miserables	the moment for closing the trial had arrived: the president ordered the prisoner to stand up, and asked him the usual question: "have you anything to add to your defence?"
Les Miserables	the man, who was rolling in his hands his hideous cap, made no reply, and the president repeated his question.
Les Miserables	this time the man heard, and seemed to understand; he moved like a person who is waking up, looked around him, at the public, the gendarmes, his counsel, the jury, and the court, laid his monstrous fist on the wood-work in front of his bench, and, suddenly fixing his eyes on the public prosecutor, began to speak.
Les Miserables	it was an eruption; from the way in which the words escaped from his lips, incoherent, impetuous, and pell-mell, it seemed as if they were all striving to get out at the same time.
Les Miserables	he said:  "i have this to say: that i was a wheelwright in paris, and worked for master baloup.
Les Miserables	it is a hard trade, is a wheelwright's; you always work in the open air, in yards, under sheds when you have a good master, but never in a room, because you want space, look you.
Les Miserables	in winter you are so cold that you swing your arms to warm you, but the masters don't like when there is ice between the stones, is rough work; it soon uses a man up.
Les Miserables	you are old when quite young in that trade.
Les Miserables	at forty a man is finished.
Les Miserables	i was fifty-three, and had hard lines of it.
Les Miserables	and then the workmen are so unkind.
Les Miserables	when a man is not so young as he was, they call him an old fool, an old brute!
Les Miserables	i only earned thirty sous a day, for the masters took advantage of my age, and paid me as little as they could.
Les Miserables	with that i had my daughter, who was a washer-woman in the river.
Les Miserables	she earned a little for her part, and the pair of us managed to live.
Les Miserables	she was bothered too.
Les Miserables	all day in a tub up to your waist, in the snow and rain, and with the wind that cuts your face.
Les Miserables	when it freezes, it is all the same, for you must wash; there are persons who have not much linen, and expect it home; if a woman did not wash, she would lose her customers.
Les Miserables	the planks are badly joined, and drops of water fall on you everywhere.
Les Miserables	her petticoats were wet through, over and under.
Les Miserables	that penetrates.
Les Miserables	she also worked at the wash-house of the enfants rouges, where the water is got from taps.
Les Miserables	you are no longer in the tub; you wash at the tap before you, and rinse in the basin behind you.
Les Miserables	as it is shut up, you don't feel so cold.
Les Miserables	but there is a steam of hot water which ruins your sight.
Les Miserables	she came home at seven in the evening, and went to bed directly, for she was so tired.
Les Miserables	her husband used to beat her.
Les Miserables	he is dead.
Les Miserables	we were not very happy.
Les Miserables	she was a good girl, who did not go to balls, and was very quiet.
Les Miserables	i remember a mardi-gras, on which she went to bed at eight o'clock.
Les Miserables	i am telling the truth.
Les Miserables	you need only inquire.
Les Miserables	oh yes, inquire!
Les Miserables	what an ass i am!
Les Miserables	paris is a gulf.
Les Miserables	who is there that knows father champmathieu?
Les Miserables	and yet, i tell you, monsieur baloup.
Les Miserables	ask him.
Les Miserables	after all, i do not know what you want of me."
Les Miserables	the man ceased speaking and remained standing; he had said all this in a loud, quick, hoarse, hard voice, with a sort of wretched and savage energy.
Les Miserables	once he broke off to bow to somebody in the crowd.
Les Miserables	the affirmations which he seemed to throw out hap-hazard came from him in gasps, and he accompanied each by the gesture of a man who is chopping wood.
Les Miserables	when he had finished, his hearers burst into a laugh; he looked at the public, seeing they were laughing, and understanding nothing, he began to laugh himself.
Les Miserables	that did him mischief.
Les Miserables	the president, a grave and kind man, began speaking.
Les Miserables	he reminded the "gentlemen of the jury" that "monsieur baloup, formerly a wheelwright in whose service the accused declared that he had been, was a bankrupt, and had not been found when an attempt was made to serve him with a subpoena."
Les Miserables	then, turning to the prisoner, he requested him to listen to what he was about to say, and added: "you are in a situation which should cause you to reflect.
Les Miserables	the heaviest presumptions are weighing upon you, and may entail capital punishment.
Les Miserables	prisoner, i ask you for the last time to explain yourself clearly on the two following facts: in the first place, did you, yes or no, climb over the wall, break a branch, and steal apples, that is to say, commit a robbery with escalade?
Les Miserables	secondly, yes or no, are you the liberated convict, jean valjean?"
Les Miserables	the prisoner shook his head with a confident air, like a man who understands and knows what answer he is going to make.
Les Miserables	he opened his mouth, turned to the president, and said,--  "in the first place--"  then he looked at his cap, looked at the ceiling, and held his tongue.
Les Miserables	"prisoner," the public prosecutor said in a stern voice, "pay attention.
Les Miserables	you make no answer to the questions that are asked you, and your confusion condemns you.
Les Miserables	it is evident that your name is not champmathieu, but jean valjean, at first concealed under the name of jean mathieu, your mother's name; that you went to auvergne; that your birth-place is faverolles, and that you are a wood-cutter.
Les Miserables	it is evident that you stole ripe apples by clambering over a wall, and the gentlemen of the jury will appreciate the fact."
Les Miserables	the prisoner had sat down again, but he hurriedly rose when the public prosecutor had finished, and exclaimed,--  "you are a wicked man.
Les Miserables	this is what i wanted to say, but i could not think of it at first.
Les Miserables	i have stolen nothing.
Les Miserables	i am a man who does not eat every day.
Les Miserables	i was coming from ailly, and walking after a flood, which had made the whole country yellow; the very ponds had overflowed, and nothing grew in the sand except a few little blades of grass by the road-side.
Les Miserables	i found a branch with apples lying on the ground, and picked it up, little thinking that it would bring me into trouble.
Les Miserables	i have been in prison and bullied for three months, and after that people talk against me, i don't know why, and say to me, answer.
Les Miserables	the gendarme, who is a good-hearted fellow, nudges me with his elbow, and says, why don't you answer?
Les Miserables	i cannot explain myself, for i am no scholar, but only a poor man, and you are wrong not to see it.
Les Miserables	i have not stolen, i only picked up things lying on the ground.
Les Miserables	you talk about jean valjean and jean mathieu.
Les Miserables	i do not know these persons, they are countrymen.
Les Miserables	i used to work for monsieur baloup, boulevard de l'hôpital, and my name is champmathieu.
Les Miserables	you are a very clever fellow to tell me where i was born, for i don't know.
Les Miserables	it is not everybody who has a house to come into the world in.
Les Miserables	that would be too comfortable.
Les Miserables	i believe that my father and mother were folks who went about on the roads, but i do not know it after all.
Les Miserables	when i was a boy i was called little, and now i am called old.
Les Miserables	those are my christian names, and you can take them as you please.
Les Miserables	i have been in auvergne.
Les Miserables	i have been at faverolles.
Les Miserables	well, hang it!
Les Miserables	may not a man have been at those two places without having been to the galleys?
Les Miserables	i tell you that i have not stolen, and that my name is champmathieu.
Les Miserables	i worked for m. baloup, and kept house.
Les Miserables	you tire me with your foolishness.
Les Miserables	why is everybody so spiteful against me?"
Les Miserables	the public prosecutor, who had not sat down, here addressed the president.
Les Miserables	"in the presence of these confused but very clear denials on the part of the prisoner, who would like to pass for an idiot, but will not succeed,--we warn him,--we request that it may please you, sir, and the court to recall the prisoners brevet, cochepaille, and chenildieu, and police inspector javert, and examine them again as to the identity of the prisoner with jean valjean."
Les Miserables	"i must remark," said the president, "that inspector javert, having been recalled to his duties at a neighboring town, left the hall and the town immediately after giving his evidence; we authorized him to do so with the consent of the public prosecutor and the counsel for the defence."
Les Miserables	"perfectly correct, sir," the public prosecutor continued.
Les Miserables	"in the absence of inspector javert, i believe it my duty to remind the gentlemen of the jury of the statement he made here a few hours ago.
Les Miserables	javert is a worthy man, who honors by his rigorous and strict probity inferior but important functions.
Les Miserables	his evidence is as follows: "i do not require moral presumptions and material proof to contradict the prisoner's assertions, for i recognize him perfectly.
Les Miserables	this man's name is not champmathieu, he is jean valjean, an ex-convict of a very violent and formidable character.
Les Miserables	it was with great reluctance that he was liberated when he completed his time.
Les Miserables	he had nineteen years' hard labor for qualified robbery, and made five or six attempts to escape.
Les Miserables	in addition to the little gervais robbery and the larceny of the apples, i also suspect him of a robbery committed in the house of his grandeur the late bishop of d----.
Les Miserables	i frequently saw him when i was assistant jailer at toulon, and i repeat that i recognize him perfectly."
Les Miserables	such a precise declaration seemed to produce a lively effect on the audience and the jury, and the public prosecutor wound up by requesting that the other three witnesses should be brought in and reexamined.
Les Miserables	the president gave an order to an usher, and a moment after the door of the witness-room opened.
Les Miserables	the usher, accompanied by a gendarme, brought in the prisoner brevet.
Les Miserables	the audience were all in suspense, and their chests heaved as if they had but one soul among them.
Les Miserables	the ex-convict brevet wore the black and gray jacket of the central prisons; he was a man of about sixty years of age, who had the face of a business man and the look of a rogue,--these are sometimes seen together.
Les Miserables	he had become a sort of jailer in the prison to which new offences had brought him, and was a man of whom the officials said, "he tries to make himself useful."
Les Miserables	the chaplains bore good testimony to his religious habits, and it must not be forgotten that this trial took place under the restoration.
Les Miserables	"brevet," said the president, "as you have undergone a degrading punishment, you cannot be sworn."
